Jerusalem Talmud Megillah
תלמוד ירושלמי מגילה
The Jerusalem Talmud, translation and commentary by Heinrich W. Guggenheimer. Berlin, De Gruyter, 1999-2015
https://www.nli.org.il/he/books/NNL_ALEPH001901012/NLI

Jerusalem Talmud Megillah



Chapter 1



Halakhah 1

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> The scroll<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">The Esther scroll read on Purim.</i> is read on the Eleventh, the Twelfth, the Thirteenth, the Fourteenth, the Fifteenth<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">Of Adar; in an intercalary year the Adar preceding Nisan.</i>, no less and no more. Places walled<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">Greek χάραξ “palisade”.</i> from the days of Joshua bin Nun<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">The reason is explained in the third paragraph of the Halakhah.</i> read on the Fifteenth. Hamlets<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">Who either have no organized prayer service since they count less than ten adult males or they have nobody competent to read the scroll.</i> and large villages read on the Fourteenth, except that hamlets precede to the market day<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">Monday or Thursday preceding Purim. On these days one reads the scroll in the local synagogue for the benefit of the peasants who come to town to sell their produce on the market. If the 14<sup>th</sup>, Purim, is on Sunday, one reads for the peasants on the 11<sup>th</sup>. If it falls on Wednesday or Sabbath, one reads on the 12<sup>th</sup>; on Tuesday or Friday one reads on the 13<sup>th</sup>. (In the computed calendar, the 14<sup>th</sup> never falls on Monday, Wednesday, or Sabbath; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Megillah 1:2:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Megillah.1.2.2">Note 89</a>.)</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “The scroll is read,” etc. Rebbi Ila, Simeon bar Abba in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: <i>To confirm these Purim days in their times</i><sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Esther 9:31" href="/Esther.9.31"><i>Esth</i>. 9:31</a>.</i>. Why does the verse say, <i>in their times</i>? Rebbi Jonah and Rebbi Yose. Rebbi Jonah says, to give them other times<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">He would allow other days than those enumerated in the Mishnah if circumstances demand it.</i>. Rebbi Yose says, times which the Sages fixed for them. And those are these: the Eleventh, the Twelfth, the Thirteenth, the Fourteenth, the Fifteenth<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">Since the days enumerated in the Mishnah are supposed to have been decreed by the Great Assembly, they could be modified only by an assembly of greater authority. It is unlikely that such an assembly ever could be convened. Differently in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Megillah 2a" href="/Megillah.2a">Babli, 2a</a>.</i>. Or maybe only the Sixteenth and the Seventeenth? Rebbi Abbahu in the name of Rebbi Eleazar: <i>and it shall not pass by</i><sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Esther 9:27" href="/Esther.9.27"><i>Esth</i>. 9:27</a>.</i>, one shall not impregnate<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">The language is taken from calendar computation where “intercalation” is called “impregnation”. Here it means “changing the usual calendar data.”</i>. Rebbi Isaac bar Naḥman in the name of Samuel: <i>and it shall not pass by</i>, and one shall not let it pass<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">Reading the root עבר not as rabbinic Hebrew “to be pregnant” but as biblical Hebrew “to pass by”. One may not celebrate Purim later than the last date mentioned in the Scroll, the 15<sup class="footnote-marker">th</sup> of Adar.</i>. Maybe it is only the Ninth and the Tenth? Samuel bar Naḥman in the name of Rebbi Jonathan: It is not written “days” but <i>like the days</i><sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Esther 9:22" href="/Esther.9.22"><i>Esth</i>. 9:22</a>.</i>, pair days with days, the Eleventh and Twelfth corresponding to Fourteenth and Fifteenth. Or the Twelfth and Thirteenth corresponding to Fourteenth and Fifteenth? Rebbi Ḥelbo said, the Thirteenth was a day of war<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Esther 9:1" href="/Esther.9.1"><i>Esth</i>. 9:1</a>.</i>. It proves for itself that it was not a day of comfort. Then one should not read on this day. One reads before and after, on the day one should not read<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">Excluding the 13th would be technically impossible. Cf. Meïri <i>Megillah</i>, <i>ed. M. Herschler</i>, pp. 14/15.</i>? Or only the Ninth and the Tenth? <i>Like the days</i>, days close to the days. But if days close to the days, {maybe} only Sixteenth and Seventeenth? You cannot, for Rebbi Abbahu said in the name of Rebbi Eleazar: <i>and it shall not pass by</i>, one shall not impregnate<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">The language is taken from calendar computation where “intercalation” is called “impregnation”. Here it means “changing the usual calendar data.”</i>. [Rebbi Isaac bar Naḥman in the name of Samuel: <i>and it shall not pass by</i>, and one shall not let it pass.<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">Corrector’s addition from the earlier text, totally out of place since one speaks of predating, not postdating.</i>]
It was stated in the name of Rebbi Nathan: The entire month is qualified for reading the scroll<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">For example a person going on a trip who probably will not have a scroll available to him on the days enumerated in the Mishnah can discharge his obligation by reading the scroll before his departure.</i>. What is the reason? <i>The month which for them was turned from sorrow to joy</i><sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Esther 9:22" href="/Esther.9.22"><i>Esth</i>. 9:22</a>.</i>. Rebbi Ḥelbo said, but only up to the Fifteenth. As Rebbi Abbahu said in the name of Rebbi Eleazar: <i>and it shall not pass by</i>, one shall not impregnate<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">The language is taken from calendar computation where “intercalation” is called “impregnation”. Here it means “changing the usual calendar data.”</i>. Rebbi Isaac bar Naḥman in the name of Samuel: and it shall not pass by, and one shall not let it pass. That is what you are saying to read the scroll. But making a dinner<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote">The obligatory dinner to celebrate Purim.</i> one makes only on the Fourteenth and the Fifteenth; and one distributes [to the poor]<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">A separate obligation to give to the poor, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Esther 9:22" href="/Esther.9.22"><i>Esth</i>. 9:22</a>.</i> only on the Fourteenth and the Fifteenth. The eyes of the poor are only directed to the Fourteenth and the Fifteenth<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Megillah 4b" href="/Megillah.4b">Babli 4b</a>.</i>. Then these should be for reading and those for the dinner? Rebbi Ḥelbo, Rebbi Ḥuna in the name of Rav: <i>These days are remembered and executed</i><sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Esther 9:28" href="/Esther.9.28"><i>Esth</i>. 9:28</a>.</i>. <i>Remembered</i> by reading. <i>And executed</i> by dinner. This implies that the Scroll is given to be analyzed<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote">While in principle a text posterior to the Pentateuch cannot prescribe any new obligation, only testify to the practice of earlier generations in the interpretation of the law, an exception is made for the Esther scroll, whose prescriptions are considered divine and in which the story is repeated in Chapter 9 for legal interpretation only. Since Sadducees did not recognize Esther as a canonical book, this elevation of the scroll to Pentateuchal status must be considered an anti-Sadducee demonstration.</i> Rebbi Ḥelbo, Rebbi Yasa in the name of Rebbi Eleazar: It is said here<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Esther 9:30" href="/Esther.9.30"><i>Esth</i>. 9:30</a>.</i>, <i>words of peace and truth</i>. And it is said there,<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Proverbs 23:23" href="/Proverbs.23.23"><i>Prov</i>. 23:23</a>.</i> <i>truth buy but do not sell</i><sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote">It is permitted to pay for instruction in the Torah but forbidden to take money for teaching others. “Truth” in <i>Proverbs</i> always refers to the Torah.</i>. It is like the truth of the Torah. This one needs ruling, that one needs ruling<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote">Torah verses, as well as a Torah scroll, may only be written on lines, if parchment or leather impressed into the material with a metal ruler, if on paper the latter must be lined. (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Megillah 16b" href="/Megillah.16b">Babli 16b</a>.)</i>. This one is given to be analyzed, that one is given to be analyzed. Rebbi Jeremiah in the name of Rebbi Samuel bar Rav Isaac: The scroll which Samuel handed to David<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote">With the plan for the building of the Temple.</i> is given to be analyzed. What is the reason? <sup class="footnote-marker">298</sup><i class="footnote">This disqualifies all Aramaic translations, even Targum Onkelos canonized in Babylonia.</i><i>Everything in writing</i>, that is the Masora. <i>For the hand of the Eternal</i>, that is the Holy Spirit. <i>To me for understanding</i>, from here that it is given to be analyzed. Rebbi Jeremiah asked, and why do we not say, <i>the plan of everything was in spirit with him</i><sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="I Chronicles 28:12" href="/I_Chronicles.28.12"><i>1Chr</i>. 28:12</a>.</i>? Rebbi Mana said, what is בָר֨וּחַ֙? by the wind of his mouth<sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote">רוח may be interpreted as Divine inspiration only if indicated as such in the verse.</i>.
“Places walled from the days of Joshua bin Nun read on the Fifteenth.” Rebbi Simon in the name of Rebbi Joshua ben Levi: They honored the Land of Israel which was desolate in those days and attached it to the days of Joshua bin Nun. Then should not everybody read on the Fifteenth? Rebbi Abbahu analyzed “dwelling, dwelling”. Since “dwelling” mentioned there refers to the days of Joshua bin Nun, so also “dwelling” mentioned here refers to the days of Joshua bin Nun<sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote">The basis for this argument and the next one is <a class="refLink" data-ref="Esther 9:19" href="/Esther.9.19"><i>Esth</i>. 9:19</a>: <i>Therefore the dispersed Jews who dwell in open towns celebrate the Fourteenth of the Month of Adar</i>. While this does not prove that the inhabitants of walled cities read on the Fifteenth, it makes clear that those in open towns do not read on the Fifteenth. The only question is whether the town has to be open at the time of reading or had to be open at the time of the conquest. Now “dwelling” is mentioned in the verse, and it is mentioned as a duty to dwell in the Land after the conquest in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 12:10" href="/Deuteronomy.12.10"><i>Deut</i>. 12:10</a>.</i>. Then should not everybody read on the Fourteenth? Rebbi Jehudah bar Pazy analyzed “open, openness.” It is said here “openness” and it said there<sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 3:5" href="/Deuteronomy.3.5"><i>Deut</i>. 3:5</a>.</i> <i>except for very many open towns</i>. Since “open” mentioned there refers to the days of Joshua bin Nun, so also “openness” mentioned here refers to the days of Joshua bin Nun<sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote">This argument to exclude dwellers in towns that are not open from reading on the 14<sup class="footnote-marker">th</sup> is quoted in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Megillah 2b" href="/Megillah.2b">Babli 2b</a>.</i>. It was stated<sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Megillah (Lieberman) 1:1" href="/Tosefta_Megillah_(Lieberman).1.1">Tosephta 1:1</a>.</i>: “Rebbi Joshua ben Qorḥa says, from the days of Ahasuerus. Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Jehudah said to him, what has the reference to Susa the Fortress to do here? If you are inferring from Susa the Fortress then one should read in Susa the Fortress, but not read at any other place<sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote">Not even in the city of Susa which in the Scroll is clearly distinguished from the government complex, the Fortress.</i>.” It seems that there are three Tannaim. The first Tanna intends to say, from the days of Joshua bin Nun. Another Tanna intends to say, from the days of Ahasuerus. Another Tanna intends to say, “town” in any case<sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote">In any town not walled at the time of reading one reads on the 14<sup class="footnote-marker">th</sup>.</i>. It turns out that you may say that Rebbi Joshua ben Qorha and Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Jehudah say the same<sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote">It is not proven that R. Yose ben R. Jehudah disagrees with R. Joshua ben Qorha in practice since he does not proffer an alternative.</i>, only that each one of them infers from a separate argument; and it was stated so<sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Megillah (Lieberman) 1:1" href="/Tosefta_Megillah_(Lieberman).1.1">Tosephta 1:1</a>.</i>: “what is close to a walled place and what is seen with it has the same standing as it<sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote">As Amoraic statement <a class="refLink" data-ref="Megillah 2b" href="/Megillah.2b">Babli 2b</a>. These places read on the 15<sup class="footnote-marker">th</sup>.</i>.” Rebbi Ayvo bar Naggari in the name of Rebbi Ḥiyya bar Abba: For example like Ḥamata. Since he only said “like”, therefore Ḥamata itself was walled in the days of Joshua bin Nun. From the following<sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Joshua 19:35" href="/Joshua.19.35"><i>Jos</i>. 19:35</a>, in the description of the territory of Naftali. Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Makkot 10a" href="/Makkot.10a">Babli <i>Makkot</i> 10a</a>.</i>: <i>Fortified towns, Haṣsiddim, Ṣer, and Ḥamat, Raqqat, and Kinneret. Haṣsiddim</i> is Wheat village<sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote">Possibly Hattim on the plain above Tiberias.</i>, <i>Ṣer</i> is next to it, <i>Ḥamat</i> Ḥamata<sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote">In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Megillah 6a" href="/Megillah.6a">Babli 6a</a> this is identified as the hot springs of Tiberias (which, however, has no archeological remains of the time of Joshua.)</i>, <i>Raqqat</i> Tiberias<sup class="footnote-marker">43</sup><i class="footnote">Tell Raqqat is slightly to the North of Herodian Tiberias.</i>, <i>Kinneret</i>, Genezareth. Rebbi Levi objected, is it not written<sup class="footnote-marker">44</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Joshua 12:3" href="/Joshua.12.3"><i>Jos</i>. 12:3</a>, as Northern border of the kingdom of Siḥon, given to the tribe of Gad.</i>, <i>the prairie to the Kinneret lake</i>? Were there two Genesareths<sup class="footnote-marker">45</sup><i class="footnote">Since the Jordan plain of Gad ended at the Southern end of the Sea of Galilee whereas Genezareth is close to its Northern end.</i>? Or were there only two autonomous districts<sup class="footnote-marker">46</sup><i class="footnote">Greek ʼαυτονομία.</i>, like Bet Yeraḥ and Zinbarai, which are towers on Lake Kinneret, where the fortification was abandoned and taken over by Gentiles. Come and see, there one does not read, outside one reads<sup class="footnote-marker">47</sup><i class="footnote">Since this is unreasonable, one may only read in the suburbs on the 15<sup>th</sup> if there is organized communal reading in the city on the 15<sup>th</sup>.</i>?
Ḥizqiah read it<sup class="footnote-marker">48</sup><i class="footnote">The Esther scroll in Tiberias. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Megillah 5b" href="/Megillah.5b">Babli 5b</a>.</i> on the Fourteenth and the Fifteenth, since he was apprehensive about what Rebbi Simeon ben Yohay had stated, <i>a person who would sell a dwelling-house in a walled city</i><sup class="footnote-marker">49</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 25:29" href="/Leviticus.25.29"><i>Lev</i>. 25:29</a>.</i>, that excludes Tiberias whose wall is the lake<sup class="footnote-marker">50</sup><i class="footnote">While Herodian Tiberias had a wall on the land side since there was none on the lake side it was of little military value. The question is whether such military value is required for the definition of “walled city” both for the laws of the Jubilee and for reading the Scroll.</i>. Rebbi Joḥanan read it in the Kifra synagogue and said, this is the main ancient Tiberias<sup class="footnote-marker">51</sup><i class="footnote">It seems that this synagogue (at other places called Kufra) was outside the wall. In his opinion this was the original Raqqat which was encroached upon by Herod building Tiberias.</i>; he was not apprehensive about what Rebbi Simeon ben Yoḥay had stated. They were lenient in matters of reading it, as we have stated there<sup class="footnote-marker">52</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Arakhin 9:5" href="/Mishnah_Arakhin.9.5">Mishnah <i>Arakhin</i> 9:5</a>, about the rules of houses in walled cities for which there exists a right of buying back only during one year.</i>: “Anything inside the walls is treated as a house of a walled city except fields. Rebbi Meïr says, even fields.” And it was stated so<sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Megillah (Lieberman) 1:1" href="/Tosefta_Megillah_(Lieberman).1.1">Tosephta 1:1</a>.</i>: “what is close to a walled place and what is seen with it has the same standing as it.<sup class="footnote-marker">53</sup><i class="footnote">While R. Simeon ben Yoḥay may be correct for the laws of the Jubilee, his argument is irrelevant for reading the Scroll.</i>”
<sup class="footnote-marker">54</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Ta`aniot</i> 4:8, Notes 340–342.</i> Rebbi Joḥanan said, from Gibbethon to Antipatris there were 60’000 villages. The smallest among them was Bet Shemesh, about which it is written<sup class="footnote-marker">55</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="I Samuel 6:19" href="/I_Samuel.6.19"><i>1S</i>. 6:19</a>.</i>, <i>He smote of the men of Bet Shemesh, for they looked at the Ark of</i><i>the Eternal</i>, etc. These were in one direction. If you execute this with reeds it will not carry it. Rebbi Ḥanina said, the Land of Israel folded its fist.
All the towns which Joshua enumerated are at least one hundred. Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish said, he enumerated those walled. Rebbi Yose bar Ḥanina said, he enumerated those close to the border. The verse supports Rebbi Yose bar Ḥanina: <i>their border was Meḥelef</i> <sup class="footnote-marker">56</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Joshua 19:33" href="/Joshua.19.33"><i>Jos</i>. 19:33</a>. The verse establishes that the localities mentioned in <i>Jos</i>. are boundary markers, in this case of Naftali. All localities mentioned in the verse will be identified later in the paragraph.</i> [etc. <i>And Qaḥtat, and Nahallal, and Shimron, and Yedeala, and Bet Leḥem</i><sup class="footnote-marker">57</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Joshua 19:15" href="/Joshua.19.15"><i>Jos</i>. 19:15</a>, description of the border between Zevulun and Naftali. Addition by the corrector since the localities will later be discussed in the text. But since of the first verse only one locality was named, the addition seems to be unwarranted. Clearly, the interest is on Tiberias and its wider neighborhood.</i>.] <i>Meḥelef</i>, Ḥelef. <i>Me’elon</i>, Aylin. <i>Baṣa`ananim</i>, Basin of Qedesh. <i>And Adami</i>, Damin. <i>Hanneqeb</i>, Ṣaidata. <i>And Yabneel</i>, Kefar Yama. <i>Up to Laqqum</i>, Loqim<sup class="footnote-marker">58</sup><i class="footnote">Most of the places are not identified. If the root of בצעננים is בצעה “swamp”, the translation would have to be “swamp near Qedesh Naftali.” <i>Damin</i> seems to be the village of <i>Dameh</i> near Tiberias. <i>Hanneqeb</i> seems to be Arabic נקב “hole, breach”, a good description for the mountain pass controlling the road from Tiberias to the Mediterranean. <i>Kefar Yama</i> may be the place Yamnia mentioned by Josephus (<i>Bell</i>. II.xx.6) in Galilee.</i>. <i>And Qaṭtat</i>, Qeṭonit. <i>And Nahallal</i>, Mahlul. <i>And Shimron</i>, Simonieh. <i>And Yedeala</i>, Ḥirieh. <i>And Bet Leḥem</i>, Bet Leḥem Sarieh<sup class="footnote-marker">59</sup><i class="footnote">The places mentioned in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Joshua 19:15" href="/Joshua.19.15"><i>Jos</i>. 19:15</a>, in the center of Lower Galilee; in Talmudic times the region of Sepphoris. Except for the Galilean Bet Lehem the places are unidentified.</i>.
Rebbi Aḥa, Rebbi Jehudah ben Levi, Rebbi Joshua ben Levi in the name of Rebbi: Lod and <i>Ge-Haḥarašim</i><sup class="footnote-marker">60</sup><i class="footnote">Mentioned first in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nehemiah 11:35" href="/Nehemiah.11.35"><i>Neh</i>. 11:35</a> as settlements in the region of Benjamin. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Megillah 4a" href="/Megillah.4a">Babli 4a</a>.</i> were walled from the times of Joshua bin Nun. Why did he not say, “and Ono”? Because it is written<sup class="footnote-marker">61</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Nehemiah 11:35" href="/Nehemiah.11.35"><i>Neh</i>. 11:35</a>.</i>. But so is Lod and it was mentioned. But because it was stated<sup class="footnote-marker">62</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Arakhin 9:6" href="/Mishnah_Arakhin.9.6">Mishnah <i>Arakhin</i> 9:6</a>, describing walled cities.</i>, “Gamla, Gedor, Hadid, and Ono, and Jerusalem, and those similar to them.” But is it not written<sup class="footnote-marker">63</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="I Chronicles 8:12" href="/I_Chronicles.8.12"><i>1Chr</i>. 8:12</a>, descendants of Benjamin.</i>, <i>The sons of Elpa‘al: Ever, and Misham, and Shemer; he built Ono and Lod and its dependencies</i>? Rebbi Eleazar ben Rebbi Yose said, it was destroyed in the times of the Concubine at Gibea<sup class="footnote-marker">64</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Megillah 4a" href="/Megillah.4a">Babli 4a</a>.</i>; Elpa‘al rebuilt it. That is what is written<sup class="footnote-marker">65</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Judges 20:48" href="/Judges.20.48"><i>Jud</i>. 20:48</a>.</i>, <i>also all existing towns they put to the fire</i>. Rebbi Ḥanania in the name of Rebbi Phineas: Lod and Ono are <i>Ge-Haḥarašim</i><sup class="footnote-marker">66</sup><i class="footnote">It is possible to read <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nehemiah 11:35" href="/Nehemiah.11.35"><i>Neh</i>. 11:35</a> as: <i>Lod and Ono are Ge-Harašim</i> (valley of the artisans.) He therefore disagrees with Rebbi’s statement.</i>.
Rebbi Ḥelbo, Rebbi Ḥuna in the name of the Great Rebbi Ḥiyya. Everybody may fulfill his obligation on the Fourteenth, which is the time of its reading<sup class="footnote-marker">67</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Shekalim 1:1:4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Shekalim.1.1.4"><i>Šeqalim</i> 1:1, Note 24</a>; also quoted twice in the sequel. He asserts that even though villagers may read early and inhabitants of walled cities have to read on the Fifteenth, reading on the Fourteenth satisfies the basic requirement. Those who are reading on both days, as mentioned later, because the tradition about the existence of a walled settlement in the days of Joshua bin Nun is not reliable, do not recite a benediction on the second day.</i>. It only comes to teach you that the obligations apply to the Second Adar<sup class="footnote-marker">68</sup><i class="footnote">As explained in <i>Šeqalim</i>, if the Scroll was read on the 15<sup class="footnote-marker">th</sup> of Adar and then an intercalary month was declared, the reading has to be repeated but even in a walled town may be read on the 14<sup class="footnote-marker">th</sup>.</i>. Rebbi Yose and Rebbi Aḥa were sitting together. Rebbi Yose said to Rebbi Aḥa, it is only reasonable for the past; but for the future<sup class="footnote-marker">69</sup><i class="footnote">If the scroll was read by error on the 14th it does not have to be repeated since the basic obligation was fulfilled, but a place which by tradition is obligated to read on the 15<sup class="footnote-marker">th</sup> cannot be permitted in the future to read on the 14<sup class="footnote-marker">th</sup>.</i>? And did we not state, at a place where one is used to read it on two days, one reads it on two days? He said to him, I also am of this opinion. Rebbi Mana said, this is correct. If somebody read it on the Fourteenth and read it again on the Fifteenth, would one not listen to him? If you are saying so<sup class="footnote-marker">70</sup><i class="footnote">That a place reading and celebrating on the 15<sup>th</sup> may be permitted to change to the 14<sup>th</sup>.</i>, with your hand you uproot the time of walled places.
Naḥman the son of Rebbi Samuel bar Naḥman: <i>Joy and enjoyment</i><sup class="footnote-marker">71</sup><i class="footnote">The entire statement is an explanation of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Esther 9:10" href="/Esther.9.10"><i>Esth</i>. 9:10</a>, but the expression “joy and enjoyment” is from 8:17 instead of the correct “joy” (as in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Megillah 5b" href="/Megillah.5b">Babli, 5b</a>.)</i>, this implies that eulogies are forbidden. <i>And festive dinner</i>, this implies that fasting is forbidden. <i>And holiday</i>, this implies that work is forbidden<sup class="footnote-marker">72</sup><i class="footnote">The Babli notes that it is not clear from the statement whether this refers to both days of Purim or only to the day celebrated in the community.</i>. Rebbi Ḥelbo said, many times I sat before Rebbi Samuel bar Naḥman but did not hear these things from him. He said to him, did you hear all the traditions which my father had? The action of Rebbi disagrees, for Rebbi acted demonstratively on two days in the year. He took a bath on the Seventeenth of Tammuz<sup class="footnote-marker">73</sup><i class="footnote">To emphasize that the restrictions of the 9<sup class="footnote-marker">th</sup> of Av do not apply to the 17<sup class="footnote-marker">th</sup> of Tammuz.</i> and planted saplings on Purim<sup class="footnote-marker">74</sup><i class="footnote">To make it clear that work is not forbidden on Purim; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Megillah 5b" href="/Megillah.5b">Babli 5b</a>.</i>. Rebbi Ḥabiba in the names of the Rabbis of there: Any place where it is written <i>holiday</i> it is not written <i>they</i><i>accepted</i>, and any place where it is written <i>they accepted</i> it is not written <i>holiday</i><sup class="footnote-marker">75</sup><i class="footnote">In the list of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Esther 9:19" href="/Esther.9.19"><i>Esth</i>. 9:19</a> “holiday” is mentioned, but in the note 9:22 that the Jews accepted the rules of Purim, only joy and dinner are mentioned (in addition to obligations to send food to one another and gifts to the poor.)</i>. And here it is written <i>holiday</i> and it is written <i>they accepted</i><sup class="footnote-marker">76</sup><i class="footnote">This can be read either as proof of R. Ḥelbo’s position (as a question), or (as a statement) by Naḥman that 9:23 asserts that the Jews accepted all that Mordocai had written to them, implicitly endorsing the holiday status.</i>? Rebbi Simon in the name of Rebbi Samuel bar Naḥman: <i>Family and family</i>, these are the hamlets. <i>City and city</i>, these are the fortified places. <i>Town and town</i>, these are the small towns<sup class="footnote-marker">77</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Esther 9:28" href="/Esther.9.28"><i>Esth</i>. 9:28</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Megillah 2b" href="/Megillah.2b">Babli 2b</a>.</i>.
Samuel stated: Why do they<sup class="footnote-marker">78</sup><i class="footnote">The inhabitants of isolated hamlets.</i> precede to the market day? So they may provide [water and]<sup class="footnote-marker">79</sup><i class="footnote">Corrector’s addition from the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Megillah 4a" href="/Megillah.4a">Babli 4a</a>, to be deleted. Water is drawn from local wells.</i> food to their brothers in the fortified places<sup class="footnote-marker">79</sup><i class="footnote">Corrector’s addition from the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Megillah 4a" href="/Megillah.4a">Babli 4a</a>, to be deleted. Water is drawn from local wells.</i>. Rebbi Philippos ben Protos said before Rebbi Jonah: Could this mean that it is forbidden for {agricultural} work<sup class="footnote-marker">80</sup><i class="footnote">If you invite the farmers to listen to the reading of the scroll in the city synagogue because you want them to bring their produce to the local market, do you not tell them to harvest their produce early in the morning and this certainly is agricultural work.</i>? He said to him, if from this you can conclude nothing. Are there not the intermediate days of holidays which are forbidden for {agricultural} work<sup class="footnote-marker">81</sup><i class="footnote">As explained in <i>Mo`ed qatan</i>, work forbidden on the intermediate days of holidays essentially is agricultural work.</i> but they provide food to their brothers in the fortified places. You only have this from Rebbi, for Rebbi acted demonstratively on two days in the year. He took a bath on the Seventeenth of Tammuz<sup class="footnote-marker">73</sup><i class="footnote">To emphasize that the restrictions of the 9<sup class="footnote-marker">th</sup> of Av do not apply to the 17<sup class="footnote-marker">th</sup> of Tammuz.</i> and planted saplings on Purim<sup class="footnote-marker">82</sup><i class="footnote">Therefore Rebbi choose agricultural work to perform in public on Purim.</i>.
Rebbi Justus ben Rebbi Shunem asked before Rebbi Mana: Did not Ezra institute that one reads in the Torah Mondays, Thursdays, and Sabbath afternoon<sup class="footnote-marker">83</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Kamma 82a" href="/Bava_Kamma.82a">Babli <i>Bava qamma</i> 82a</a> (and below Halakhah 4:1, 75a l. 21). The Torah reading on Sabbath morning is attributed to Moses.</i>? And Mordocai and Esther institute on basis of what Ezra will institute in the future<sup class="footnote-marker">84</sup><i class="footnote">Since the text dates Purim to Xerxes but Ezra to Artaxerxes, basing a date for the reading of the Scroll on an institution of Ezra clearly is anachronistic.</i>? He said to him, he who ordered the Mishnah based it on Scripture: <i>Family and family, city and city, town and town</i><sup class="footnote-marker">85</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Esther 9:28" href="/Esther.9.28"><i>Esth</i>. 9:28</a>. No fixed dates are indicated, only that there are variable times according to circumstances. The fixation of market days to Monday and Thursday is a later development in the many centuries between Persian rule and Mishna composition.</i>.

Halakhah 2

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> How is this? If it falls on Monday, hamlets and large villages read on this day, but walled cities the next day. If it falls on Tuesday or Wednesday, hamlets precede to the market day, large villages read on this day, but walled cities the next day.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> In all cases where we are stating “if it falls” it refers to the Fourteenth<sup class="footnote-marker">86</sup><i class="footnote">Of Adar (II).</i>. Rebbi Yose said, the Mishnah implies this, “walled cities the next day.” Rebbi Yose<sup class="footnote-marker">87</sup><i class="footnote">Who published the calendar rules which insure that the Day of Atonement never falls on Friday or Sunday (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Eruvin 3:9:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Eruvin.3.9.3"><i>Eruvin</i> Chapter 3, Note 199</a>.)</i> said, there is no “if it falls on Monday”, [there is no] “if it falls on the Sabbath.<sup class="footnote-marker">88</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Megillah 1:3:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Megillah.1.3.1">Mishnah 4</a>.</i>” If it falls on Monday, the Great Fast Day is on Sunday; if it falls on the Sabbath, the Great Fast Day falls on Friday<sup class="footnote-marker">89</sup><i class="footnote">In the computed calendar only the lengths of the Months Marḥeshwan and Kislew are variable. Therefore if the day of the week of any date after 1 Tevet is known, the day of week of any later date in the year may easily be computed. In addition, the Adar preceding Nisan always has 29 days. After the 14<sup>th</sup>, there are 15 days left in Adar. From Nisan 1 to Tishre 1 are three months of 30 days and three of 29 days. Together with the 10 days of Tishre to the Day of Atonement this makes</i>.

Halakhah 3

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If it falls on Thursday, hamlets and large villages read on this day, but walled cities on the next day.<br>If it falls on Friday, hamlets precede to the market day<sup class="footnote-marker">90</sup><i class="footnote">Thursday.</i>, large villages and walled cities read on this day<sup class="footnote-marker">91</sup><i class="footnote">One forbids reading the scroll on a Sabbath, to avoid having a person carrying the scroll in the public domain in a place having no <i>eruv</i>.</i>.<br>If it falls on the Sabbath, hamlets and large villages precede and read on the market day, but walled cities on the next day.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> They asked before Rebbi Ze`ira<sup class="footnote-marker">92</sup><i class="footnote">Two generations before R. Yose, when the 14th on Monday and Sabbath was not impossible.</i>: Come and see, if it falls so that in walled cities one should read on the Sabbath, one reads on Friday<sup class="footnote-marker">93</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Megillah 1:3:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Megillah.1.3.1">Mishnah 4</a>.</i>. If it falls so that in villages one should read on the Sabbath, it is being pushed to the market day<sup class="footnote-marker">94</sup><i class="footnote">Mishnah 5.</i>! Rebbi La said, because of the honor of walled cities, that one should not say, we saw walled cities and villages read at the same day. Then it should not be pushed to the market day, that one should not say, we saw hamlets and villages read on the same day. Then if it falls on Monday, falls on [Thursday, falls on]<sup class="footnote-marker">95</sup><i class="footnote">Corrector’s addition, probably against the intent of the original compiler, cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Megillah 1:3:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Megillah.1.3.3">Note 87</a>.</i> the Sabbath? It was found stated, Hamlets [and villages]<sup class="footnote-marker">96</sup><i class="footnote">Corrector’s addition. Since villages always read between hamlets and walled cities, the note is correct but logically unnecessary.</i> and walled cities may read together; and this Tanna does not show (respect) [even]<sup class="footnote-marker">97</sup><i class="footnote">The [corrector’s] note is incomprehensible; follow the (scribe’s) text.</i> for any creature. He holds that walled cities cannot precede villages; he holds that any which is pushed away is pushed to the market day. “Rebbi says, all shall be pushed to the market day.<sup class="footnote-marker">98</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Megillah (Lieberman) 1:2" href="/Tosefta_Megillah_(Lieberman).1.2">Tosephta 1:2</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Megillah 4b" href="/Megillah.4b">Babli 4b</a>.</i>” For he holds that walled cities cannot precede villages; for he holds that any which is pushed away is pushed to the market day. How does one act? Rebbi Yose said, following the Mishnah<sup class="footnote-marker">99</sup><i class="footnote">In all actual cases one follows the applicable one of Mishnaiot 2–5</i>.
They asked before Rebbi Ze`ira<sup class="footnote-marker">92</sup><i class="footnote">Two generations before R. Yose, when the 14th on Monday and Sabbath was not impossible.</i>: As you are saying there, if it falls so that in walled cities one should read on the Sabbath, one reads on Friday<sup class="footnote-marker">93</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Megillah 1:3:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Megillah.1.3.1">Mishnah 4</a>.</i>. If it falls so that in villages one should read on the Sabbath, should one not read it on Friday<sup class="footnote-marker">100</sup><i class="footnote">Since if the 15th is on a Sabbath one reads on the 14th, on Friday, there seems to be no reason why one should not read on Friday if the 14th is on the Sabbath, against the Mishnah.</i>? But the Mishnah must be by two Tannaim. The first Tanna is of the opinion that any which is pushed away is pushed to the market day<sup class="footnote-marker">101</sup><i class="footnote">This is the Mishnah.</i>. [The other Tanna is of the opinion that not everything which is pushed away is pushed to the market day.]<sup class="footnote-marker">102</sup><i class="footnote">Corrector’s addition. Probably not part of the original text, in which Rebbi Ze`ira immediately states that one follows Rav who explains that all Mishnaiot are consistent.</i> He said to them, so says Rav: it is one Tanna. Why did they say, if for walled cities it falls on a Sabbath, one reads it on Friday? Because Rebbi Ḥelbo, Rebbi Ḥuna in the name of the Great Rebbi Ḥiyya: Everybody may fulfill his obligation on the Fourteenth, which is the time of its reading<sup class="footnote-marker">67,</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Shekalim 1:1:4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Shekalim.1.1.4"><i>Šeqalim</i> 1:1, Note 24</a>; also quoted twice in the sequel. He asserts that even though villagers may read early and inhabitants of walled cities have to read on the Fifteenth, reading on the Fourteenth satisfies the basic requirement. Those who are reading on both days, as mentioned later, because the tradition about the existence of a walled settlement in the days of Joshua bin Nun is not reliable, do not recite a benediction on the second day.</i><sup class="footnote-marker">103</sup><i class="footnote">If for walled cities it is a Sabbath, then Friday is the 14th and is valid reading for everybody (after the fact).</i>. And similarly, if for villages it would fall on the Sabbath<sup class="footnote-marker">104</sup><i class="footnote">But if Sabbath is the 14th, Friday is not acceptable following R. Ḥelbo, and there are two reasons why one should read on Thursday.</i>; as Rebbi Ḥelbo said, the Thirteenth was a day of war<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Esther 9:1" href="/Esther.9.1"><i>Esth</i>. 9:1</a>.</i>. It proves for itself that it was not a day of comfort. It is found stated, hamlets read on Friday<sup class="footnote-marker">105</sup><i class="footnote">Following R. La in the preceding Halakhah. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Megillah 5a" href="/Megillah.5a">Babli 5a</a>.</i>; large villages are pushed to the market day, and walled cities after the Sabbath. How does one act? Rebbi Yose said, it is impossible<sup class="footnote-marker">87</sup><i class="footnote">Who published the calendar rules which insure that the Day of Atonement never falls on Friday or Sunday (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Eruvin 3:9:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Eruvin.3.9.3"><i>Eruvin</i> Chapter 3, Note 199</a>.)</i>, but if one would be possible, following the Mishnah<sup class="footnote-marker">99</sup><i class="footnote">In all actual cases one follows the applicable one of Mishnaiot 2–5</i>.
Rebbi Abba in the name of Rav Jehudah: In any case they said, it should be pushed away from its place, only with ten<sup class="footnote-marker">106</sup><i class="footnote">For the Yerushalmi it is clear that on the 14<sup class="footnote-marker">th</sup> (or for walled cities the 15<sup class="footnote-marker">th</sup>), reading the Scroll is not a sacral act and public worship; therefore it does not have to be performed in public by 10 adult males. The only problem is if the scroll has to be read on another day; where in one opinion it remains a private act but in the other it needs publicity, a public reading in the presence of at least ten adult males. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Megillah 5a" href="/Megillah.5a">Babli 5a</a>.</i>. But we see Rabbis even single? Rebbi Abuna in the name of Rebbi<sup class="footnote-marker">107</sup><i class="footnote">Read: Rav.</i> Assi: In any case they said, any that is pushed to the market day, only with ten. Rebbi Ḥanania said, Rav Ḥuna and Rav Jehudah disagree. One says, only with ten; the other one says, even singly. The one who said only for ten objected to the one who said even singly: then why does he not read at his place<sup class="footnote-marker">108</sup><i class="footnote">Why should not everybody read privately on the 14<sup>th</sup>?</i>?
Rebbi said, the Mishnah comes out on the Eleventh, the Twelfth, the Thirteenth, the Fourteenth, the Fifteenth<sup class="footnote-marker">109</sup><i class="footnote">The 11<sup>th</sup> appears as a day of reading the scroll only if the 14th is a Sunday, and this case is missing in the Mishnah in the Yerushalmi; it is in the Mishnah in the Babli (and in Maimonides’s autograph Mishnah). Rebbi declares the Mishnah in the Babli and independent Mishnah mss. as the correct last edition of the text.</i>.

Halakhah 4

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> What is a large village? Any in which there are ten unoccupied people<sup class="footnote-marker">110</sup><i class="footnote">Since a day laborer is supposed to work from sunrise to sunset, and the farmer or householder who hires him must supervise and work with him during that time, regular synagogue service during the week is possible only if there are at least 10 adult males available after sunrise and before sunset for public morning and afternoon services. Since the inhabitants of hamlets are supposed to come to the synagogue to hear the reading of the scroll, if the synagogue is not open they cannot come. If the community has people able to read the scroll but cannot support a daily synagogue service, they are reading on the 14th.</i>. Less than that is a hamlet. About these<sup class="footnote-marker">111</sup><i class="footnote">The days preceding Purim.</i> it was said that one precedes and does not trail. But for the times of offering of wood by the priests and the people<sup class="footnote-marker">112</sup><i class="footnote">As described in Mishnah <i>Ta`anit</i> 4:6.</i>, and the Ninth of Av<sup class="footnote-marker">113</sup><i class="footnote">Which is pushed to the 10th if the 9th is a Sabbath.</i>, and the festive offering<sup class="footnote-marker">114</sup><i class="footnote">The festival offering required from every pilgrim at one of the three yearly festivals of pilgrimage cannot be offered prior to the start of the festival, but may be offered anytime in the holiday week (which in case of Pentecost is after the end of the holiday).</i>, and the public assembly<sup class="footnote-marker">115</sup><i class="footnote">The public Torah reading on Tabernacles of the Sabbatical year; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 31:10-13" href="/Deuteronomy.31.10-13"><i>Deut</i>. 31:10–13</a>.</i>, one trails but does not precede<sup class="footnote-marker">116</sup><i class="footnote">If any of these dates falls on a Sabbath, the obligation is transferred to the following Sunday.</i>. Even though they said, one precedes but does not trail, one is permitted to eulogize, and to fast<sup class="footnote-marker">117</sup><i class="footnote">The prohibition of mourning and fasting refers only to Purim itself, not to the dates of reading the scroll if these precede the 14th of Adar.</i>, and gifts to the poor<sup class="footnote-marker">118</sup><i class="footnote">As stated before (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Megillah 1:1:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Megillah.1.1.3">Note 21</a>), these obligations also are independent of the date of reading the scroll.</i>. Rebbi Jehudah said, when has this been said? At places where one assembles Monday and Thursday. But at a place where one assembles neither Monday nor Thursday one reads on its time<sup class="footnote-marker">110</sup><i class="footnote">Since a day laborer is supposed to work from sunrise to sunset, and the farmer or householder who hires him must supervise and work with him during that time, regular synagogue service during the week is possible only if there are at least 10 adult males available after sunrise and before sunset for public morning and afternoon services. Since the inhabitants of hamlets are supposed to come to the synagogue to hear the reading of the scroll, if the synagogue is not open they cannot come. If the community has people able to read the scroll but cannot support a daily synagogue service, they are reading on the 14th.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> It was stated: Ten who are free from work for the synagogue<sup class="footnote-marker">119</sup><i class="footnote">It is not implied that these people do not work at all; only that they are free to go to the synagogue mornings and evenings. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Megillah 5a" href="/Megillah.5a">Babli 5a</a>.</i>. Rav Jehudah said, like us who do not need time to study<sup class="footnote-marker">120</sup><i class="footnote">He already has passed all his exams and obtained a job as communal rabbi; therefore he is free (probably better: obligated) to come to the synagogue every morning and evening.</i>. It was stated, a place with less than ten inhabitants, its improvement is by its deficiency and is treated like a village<sup class="footnote-marker">121</sup><i class="footnote">If there are less than 10 inhabitants it is not worth while for the nearby village to organize a reading for them; since the market day is irrelevant for them automatically they have to read on the 14th.</i>.
“About these<sup class="footnote-marker">111</sup><i class="footnote">The days preceding Purim.</i> it was said that one precedes and does not trail.” Reading the Scroll and paying the <i>Sheqel</i> tax one precedes and does not trail; the Meal of the New Moon and the Purim Meal one trails<sup class="footnote-marker">122</sup><i class="footnote">The meal at which the New Moon is declared by the calendar court by its nature cannot be celebrated before the New Moon has been declared; the Purim meal is scheduled for the afternoon of the 14th (or 15th), probably extending into the next night.</i> but does not precede. Rebbi Ze`ira said before Rebbi Abbahu: then could one make it on the Sabbath<sup class="footnote-marker">123</sup><i class="footnote">For which one has to go to the expenses of a festive meal anyhow.</i>? He answered him, <i>to make them days of drinking and joy</i><sup class="footnote-marker">124</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Esther 9:22" href="/Esther.9.22"><i>Esth</i>. 9:22</a>.</i>; those whose joy depends on the court<sup class="footnote-marker">125</sup><i class="footnote">The calendar court, before the publication of the computed calendar. After the publication, every date is permanently depending on R. Yose’s court.</i>; this excludes those whose joy depends on Heaven.
“<sup class="footnote-marker">126</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Megillah (Lieberman) 1:5" href="/Tosefta_Megillah_(Lieberman).1.5">Tosephta 1:5</a>; Babli <i>Bava meṣia`</i> 78b.</i> The Purim collection is for Purim<sup class="footnote-marker">127</sup><i class="footnote">The gifts to the poor demanded by <a class="refLink" data-ref="Esther 9:22" href="/Esther.9.22"><i>Esth</i>. 9:22</a> must be used by the poor to buy food for the Purim meal.</i>. Rebbi Eleazar<sup class="footnote-marker">128</sup><i class="footnote">The ms. sources (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Megillah 1:4:4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Megillah.1.4.4">Note 126</a>) are about evenly split between Eleazar and Eliezer.</i> said, only that the poor should not change it from its destination to {buy from it} laces for his shoes. One does not check eligibility for the money given as Purim charity; one gives to anybody who extends his hand to take.” One may not change Purim monies; does this imply that other {charity} monies one may change<sup class="footnote-marker">129</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kiddushin 1:6:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kiddushin.1.6.1">Mishnah <i>Qiddušin</i> 1:6</a> states that a promise to Heaven has the legal consequences of delivery to persons. If monies promised to charity must be delivered, what else may be changed?</i>? But the intended use of any monies may be changed<sup class="footnote-marker">130</sup><i class="footnote">While the fact that the monies must be given to charity cannot be changed, the name and nature of the charity may be changed before the monies are delivered to the administrators of the charity. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Megillah (Lieberman) 2:15" href="/Tosefta_Megillah_(Lieberman).2.15">Tosephta 2:15</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Arakhin 6a" href="/Arakhin.6a">Babli <i>Arakhin</i> 6a</a>.</i> before they are given into the hands of the administrators; after they are given into the hands of the administrators they may not be changed.
<sup class="footnote-marker">131</sup><i class="footnote">This paragraph also is <i>Ta`aniot</i> 4:6 (Notes 187–194), <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Shekalim 4:1:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Shekalim.4.1.2"><i>Šeqalim</i> 4:1 (Notes 10–18</a>); Babli <i>Ta`anit</i> 12a.</i>“But for the times of offering of wood by the priests.” For what reason were the times of wood by the priests and the people to be counted? Only that at the time when Israel returned from the Diaspora and did not find wood in the chamber, those came forward and volunteered wood from their own and donated it to the public. It was used to offer public sacrifices. The prophets among them stipulated for them that even if the chamber was full of wood, if those came and offered and volunteered wood from their own, that the sacrifice should only be brought first from theirs. Rebbi Aḥa said, this is Rebbi Yose’s, since Rebbi Yose said, also he may volunteer as unpaid trustee. Rebbi Yose in the name of Rebbi Ila, it is the opinion of everybody. Where do they disagree? About the body of the offering. But for enablers of the offering everybody agrees that a private offering can be turned into public offering. It was stated, a woman who made a coat for her son has to surrender it to the public. Rebbi Aḥa said, this is Rebbi Yose’s, since Rebbi Yose said, also he may volunteer as unpaid trustee. Rebbi Yose in the name of Rebbi Ila, it is the opinion of everybody. Where do they disagree? About the body of the offering. But for enablers of the offering everybody agrees that a private offering can be turned into public offering.. A <i>baraita</i> disagrees with Rebbi Yose: “Those days are observed at the time of sacrifices and not at the time of sacrifices; Rebbi Yose says, they are observed only at the time of sacrifices.” Also from the following: “Rebbi Eleazar ben Rebbi Sadoq said, we were of the descendants of Senaah ben Benjamin. When the Ninth of Av fell on a Sabbath, we postponed it to the end of the Sabbath and we were fasting but not completing.”
“And the Ninth of Av.” <sup class="footnote-marker">132</sup><i class="footnote">This paragraph also is in <i>Ta`aniot</i> 4:9, Notes 370–377.</i> Rebbi Jeremiah in the name of Rebbi Ḥiyya, it should have been logical that one should fast on the Tenth, since on it it was burned. Why on the Ninth? Because the calamity started on it. So it was stated, on the Seventh they entered, on the Eighth they were misbehaving in it, on the Ninth they started the fire, and on the Tenth it was burned. Rebbi Joshua ben Levi fasted Ninth and Tenth. Rebbi Levi fasted on the Ninth and the night of the Tenth. Rebbi Abba bar Zavda in the name of Rebbi Ḥanina: Rebbi wanted to uproot the Ninth of Av but they did not let him. Rebbi Eleazar said to him, I was with you but it was not said so, but Rebbi wanted to uproot the Ninth of Av which fell on a Sabbath but they did not let him. He said, because it was pushed aside, let it be pushed aside. They said to him, let it be pushed to the next day. In a similar case, that which we stated there: “Rebbi Joḥanan ben Beroqa said, for both of them it says, <i>God blessed them and God said to them</i>, <i>be fruitful and multiply</i><sup class="footnote-marker">133</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Genesis 1:28" href="/Genesis.1.28"><i>Gen</i>. 1:28</a>.</i>, etc.” Rebbi Eleazar in the Name of Rebbi Ḥanina: Practice follows Rebbi Joḥanan ben Beroqa. Rebbi Abba bar Zavda said to him, I was with you and it was only said, if she asks to be married the law is on her side. He said about them, <i>two are better than one</i><sup class="footnote-marker">134</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Ecclesiastes 4:9" href="/Ecclesiastes.4.9"><i>Eccl</i>. 4:9</a>.</i>.
“And the festive offering.” One verse says, <i>the harvest festival</i>, <i>the first fruits of your work</i><sup class="footnote-marker">134*</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Ex</i>. 23:16.</i>. Another verse says, <i>any productive work you shall not do</i><sup class="footnote-marker">135</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 23:21" href="/Leviticus.23.21"><i>Lev</i>. 23:21</a>. The first verse seems to require harvesting on Pentecost, the second verse forbids it. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Chagigah 17b" href="/Chagigah.17b">Babli <i>Ḥagigah</i> 17b</a>.</i>. Rebbi Ḥanania said, how could both verses be sustained? If it falls on a weekday you bring the festival offering and refrain from work. If it falls on the Sabbath, the following day you bring the festival offering and harvest<sup class="footnote-marker">136</sup><i class="footnote">Since Pentecost is only one day, the next day is a regular work day.</i>. Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Abun said, only ears for her dough<sup class="footnote-marker">137</sup><i class="footnote">On a day of sacrificing one is forbidden gainful work, as on a holiday. Therefore on the Sunday after Pentecost on Sabbath the only harvesting permitted is collecting for immediate consumption.</i>. As what was stated<sup class="footnote-marker">138</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Pesachim 4:1:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Pesachim.4.1.2"><i>Pesaḥim</i> 4:1, Notes 6–7</a>; <i>Megillat Ta`anit</i> Chapter 5.</i>, “therefore anybody who has an obligation for wood and first fruits. He who says, I am taking upon me {to bring} wood for the altar and logs for the arrangement<sup class="footnote-marker">139</sup><i class="footnote">The arrangement of firewood on the altar.</i> on that day are forbidden funeral orations, and fasting, and working<sup class="footnote-marker">140</sup><i class="footnote">Work is not forbidden in a formal sense as on the Sabbath or holiday, only any gainful employment, including harvest for later sale.</i>.”
“And the public assembly.” Rebbi Abba, son of Rebbi Ḥiyya bar Abba, said, because of horn blowing<sup class="footnote-marker">141</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Sotah (Lieberman) 7:15" href="/Tosefta_Sotah_(Lieberman).7.15">Tosephta <i>Sotah</i> 7:15</a> states that Cohanim blow golden trumpets at the public assembly. There is no horn blowing on the Sabbath.</i>. Rebbi Isaac ben Rebbi Ḥiyya said, because of the platform<sup class="footnote-marker">142</sup><i class="footnote">On which the king sits to read from the Torah, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Sotah (Lieberman) 7:13" href="/Tosefta_Sotah_(Lieberman).7.13">Tosephta <i>Sotah</i> 7:13</a>. This argument follows the opinion that the Torah was read inside the Temple enclosure, not on the Temple Mount outside the enclosure following R. Eliezer ben Jacob.</i>. Could they not make it the day before? Not to compress the courtyard<sup class="footnote-marker">143</sup><i class="footnote">The Torah is read on the first of the intermediate days of Tabernacles. The courtyard is rather small and could not accommodate the worshippers on the first day of the holiday if it already had been constructed there. Quoted by <a class="refLink" data-ref="Rashi on Megillah 5a" href="/Rashi_on_Megillah.5a">Rashi 5a</a>.</i>. Rebbi Mattaniah said, because of <i>you shall not plant any wooden Ashera</i><sup class="footnote-marker">144</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 16:21" href="/Deuteronomy.16.21"><i>Deut</i>. 16:21</a>. In his opinion, any permanent wooden construction is forbidden in the Temple precinct. R. Eliezer ben Jacob reads the verse as forbidding any wooden structure in the Temple precinct (<i>Sifry Deut</i>. 145); therefore he must hold that the reading was outside on the Mount. This raises the question whether R. Mattaniah quotes the verse in support of R. Isaac ben R. Ḥiyya or in opposition since for R. Eliezer ben Jacob only R. Abba ben R. Ḥiyya’s reason would be valid (Maimonides <i>Ḥagigah</i> 3:7).</i>.
The Mishnah is Rebbi Meïr’s, since Rebbi Meïr said, “not to eulogize” one is forbidden to fast, “not to fast” one is permitted to eulogize. “Not to” unspecified is as “not to fast.” Rebbi Jonah said, “these are the days not to fast on them, and partially not to eulogize on them.” Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel said, why does it say “on them” twice? To teach that the night is permitted but the day forbidden. As it was stated, “therefore a person who takes it on himself has to forbid himself in prayer.” Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Abun said, that he must mention it on the preceding evening. This comes following what Rebbi Ze`ira said in the name of Rav Ḥuna: Similar to Friday night and Sabbath day.
The Mishnah either follows Rebbi Yose on the eleventh or Rebbi Meïr on the twelfth. But it is difficult for Rebbi Meïr. Was it not stated, “on the twelfth of it is Tirion Day?” But Rebbi Jacob bar Aḥa said, Tirion Day was disestablished; the day when Julianus and Pappos were killed. “On the thirteenth of it is Nikanor Day.” What is Nikanor Day? “A commander of the Greek government was passing by on his way to Alexandria when he saw Jerusalem, and insulted, blasphemed, and vituperated and said, <i>when I shall return in peace I shall destroy this tower</i> One of the Hasmoneans went out against him, killed his soldiers until he came to his car When he came to his car he hacked off his hand and cut off his head, stuck them on a piece of wood and wrote under it, the mouth which spoke in guilt and the hand stretched out in haughtiness, and hang it on a pole outside Jerusalem.” In Rebbi Meïr’s opinion it is understandable, it comes to forbid the previous day. What does it come to forbid for Rebbi Yose? To forbid the day before he does not need since itself it forbidden because of the fourteenth. It comes to inform you that eulogies are forbidden. And also for Rebbi Meïr it is not difficult; was it not stated, “on the twelfth of it is Tirion Day?” But Rebbi Jacob bar Aḥa said, Tirion Day was disestablished; the day when Julianus and Pappos were killed. “On the fourteenth and the fifteenth is Purim, not to eulogize. On the sixteenth they started to build the wall of Jerusalem, not to eulogize.” In Rebbi Meïr’s opinion it is understandable, it comes to forbid the previous day. What does it come to forbid for Rebbi Yose? To forbid the day before he does not need since itself is forbidden because of the fifteenth. It comes to inform you that eulogies are forbidden.
“On the seventeenth of it Gentiles attacked the remainder of the Sopherim at the city of Khalkis and Bet-Zabdin and there was relief.” In Rebbi Meïr’s opinion it is understandable, it comes to forbid the {day} itself. What does it come to forbid for Rebbi Yose? Rebbi Yose said, all these matters neither support nor contradict either Rebbi Meïr or Rebbi Yose; they only come to report the days on which miracles were done for Israel. You have to understand that this is so since we have stated: “On the New Moon of Nisan the Daily Offering was settled, one may not eulogize.” Without this is it not already forbidden to him because of the New Moon? “But on Sabbath and holidays one fasts before and after them. What do you understand to be lenient with these and restrictive with those? For these are words of the Torah, and words of the Torah do not need reinforcement. But those are words of the Sopherim, and words of the Sopherim need reinforcement.” That is what you were saying, as long as <i>Megillat Ta`anit</i> was not abolished. But since <i>Megillat Ta`anit</i> was abolished, all these were abolished. Rebbi Ḥanina and Rebbi Joshua ben Levi both said, <i>Megillat Ta`anit</i> was abolished. Rebbi Abba and Rebbi Simon both said, <i>Megillat Ta`anit</i> was abolished. Rebbi Jonathan said, <i>Megillat Ta`anit</i> was abolished. Rebbi Joḥanan said, yesterday I was repeating, “it happened that in Lydda they decided on a fast-day on Ḥanukkah. They said about Rebbi Eliezer that he got a haircut, and about Rebbi Joshua that he took a {thermal} bath. Rebbi Joshua said to them, go and fast because you fasted.” And you are saying, <i>Megillat Ta`anit</i> was abolished? Rebbi Abba said, even if you are saying that <i>Megillat Ta`anit</i> was abolished, Ḥanukkah and Purim were not abolished.
The actions of the rabbis imply that <i>Megillat Ta`anit</i> was abolished. Rebbi Jonathan fasted every New Year’s Day’s eve. Rebbi Abun fasted every Sabbath eve. Rebbi Ze`ira fasted 300 fasts, and some say 900, and disregarded <i>Megillat Ta`anit</i>. Rebbi Jacob bar Aḥa commanded the schoolteachers, if a woman comes to ask you, tell her that one may fast any time except Sabbaths, holidays, New Moons, intermediate days of holidays, Ḥanukkah and Purim.
Simeon bar Abba said, there came a case before Rebbi Joḥanan and he instructed following Rebbi Yose. Rebbi Eleazar was sorry about this; he said, does one disregard the anonymous {Mishnah} and follow an isolated opinion? He found that Rebbi Ḥiyya stated this in the name of Rebbi Meïr. When he understood that Rebbi Ḥiyya stated this in the name of Rebbi Meïr, he said, the old man understands his chapters well. Rebbi Mana asked before Rebbi Yudan: Did not Rebbi Ḥizqiah, Rebbi Abbahu say in the name of Rebbi Eleazar, every place where Rebbi taught a disagreement and afterwards taught it anonymously, practice follows the anonymous statement. He said to him, and if not Rebbi, maybe somebody else. How is this? If it is found that Rebbi taught a disagreement and afterwards taught it anonymously, then practice follows the anonymous statement. In a case where Rebbi did not teach a disagreement but others taught it in disagreement and Rebbi taught it anonymously, certainly practice has to follow the anonymous text. There come Rebbi Ḥizqiah, Rebbi Jacob bar Aḥa, Rebbi Simeon bar Abba in the name of Rebbi Eleazar, every place where Rebbi taught a disagreement and afterwards taught it anonymously, practice follows the anonymous statement. Why does he instruct here following the isolated opinion? Rebbi Samuel bar Ina in the name of Rebbi Aḥa: That is, if no disagreement is stated together with the anonymous opinion. But if a disagreement is stated together with the anonymous opinion, practice does not follow the anonymous opinion.
<sup class="footnote-marker">146</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Megillah 7" href="/Megillah.7">Babli 7a/b</a>, where however the text in the printed editions is unreliable and originally was a metathesis of the Yerushalmi text; cf. <i>Dikduke Soferim Megillah</i> pp. 24–25, Notes <b>3-ג.</b> Since this story illustrates the meaning of <i>sending meals one person to the other and gifts to the poor</i> which is mentioned in Mishnah 7, it seems that this paragraph, which has no connection with the subjects of Mishnah 6, really belongs to the next Halakhah.</i> Rebbi Yudan the Patriarch sent the Great Rebbi Hoshaia a roast and a pitcher<sup class="footnote-marker">147</sup><i class="footnote">Greek λάγηνος, Latin <i>lagena</i>.</i> of wine. He sent and told him, you fulfilled with us <i>gifts to the needy</i>. Then he sent him a calf and a barrel of wine. He sent and told him, you fulfilled with us, <i>sending meals one person to the other</i>.
It was stated: In a place (where they are in danger) [where they assemble]<sup class="footnote-marker">150</sup><i class="footnote">Obviously the text of the (scribe) is the correct one; the [corrector’s] has to be deleted. The scribe’s text is quoted by R. S. Nathanson in <i>Ziun Yerushalaim</i> in the name of Nahmanides in the Wilna edition of the Yerushalmi; the quote is not found in Nahmanides”s <i>Novellae</i> to <i>Megillah</i> edited by M. Goldstein, Jerusalem 1973.</i> one reads it on the Fourteenth. Rebbi Yose asked: If it is a place (where they are in danger) [where they assemble] they should not read it at all<sup class="footnote-marker">151</sup><i class="footnote">A customary obligation is not to be executed if it leads to danger of life.</i>.

Halakhah 5

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If the Scroll was read during the first Adar and then the year was intercalated<sup class="footnote-marker">148</sup><i class="footnote">While the Scroll was read correctly, since now actually the Adar preceding Nisan is the next month the reading has to be repeated.</i> one has to read it during the second Adar. There is no difference between first and second Adar except reading the Scroll and gifts to the needy<sup class="footnote-marker">149</sup><i class="footnote">In this form it is not quite correct since the intercalation also makes the first Adar 30 days but the second is 29 days. One has to explain that on the 14th (or 15th) of the second one reads the scroll, gives gifts to the needy and sends food to friends, but the prohibition of fasting and mourning extends to the same days in the first Adar.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> The Mishnah if it was intercalated and afterwards they read it; but if they read it and after that it was intercalated it does not apply<sup class="footnote-marker">152</sup><i class="footnote">In this rejected opinion, if the reading the first time was legitimate, it does not have to be repeated. The accepted opinion leaves open the question whether also the benedictions recited for the reading have to be repeated. Differently <a class="refLink" data-ref="Megillah 6b-7a" href="/Megillah.6b-7a">Babli 6b–7a</a>.</i>. The Mishnah does not say so, but “if the Scroll was read during the first Adar and then the year was intercalated one has to read it during the second Adar.” Rebbi Simon in the name of Rebbi Joshua ben Levi: it is written<sup class="footnote-marker">153</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Esther 9:29" href="/Esther.9.29"><i>Esth</i>. 9:29</a>.</i>, <i>to validate this Purim letter</i>; why does the verse say, <i>the second</i>? But from here that If the Scroll was read during the first Adar and then the year was intercalated one has to read it during the second Adar.
<sup class="footnote-marker">154</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Megillah 7a" href="/Megillah.7a">Babli 7a</a>.</i> Rebbi Jeremiah in the name of Rebbi Samuel bar Rav Isaac: What did Mordocai and Esther do? They wrote a letter and sent to our rabbis<sup class="footnote-marker">155</sup><i class="footnote">The established rabbinate in the Holy Land, the only one authorized to decree practices valid everywhere.</i>. So they said to them, do you accept upon yourselves these two days every year? They answered them, are not the troubles which come upon us enough, that you want to add to ours the trouble of Haman? They insisted and wrote them a second letter; that is what is written<sup class="footnote-marker">156</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Esther 9:29" href="/Esther.9.29"><i>Esth</i>. 9:29</a>.</i>, <i>to confirm to them this second [Purim]letter</i>. What was written in it? They told them, if this is your fear, already it is written and deposited in Archives<sup class="footnote-marker">157</sup><i class="footnote">Greek ’αρχεῑον.</i>: <i>are these not written in the annals of the kings of Media and Persia</i><sup class="footnote-marker">158</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Esther 10:2" href="/Esther.10.2"><i>Esth</i>. 10:2</a>.</i>. Rebbi Samuel bar Naḥman in the name of Rebbi Jonathan: 85 Elders and among them more than 30 prophets<sup class="footnote-marker">159</sup><i class="footnote">In Babli sources: 120 Elders.</i> were sorry about this matter. They said, it is written<sup class="footnote-marker">160</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 27:34" href="/Leviticus.27.34"><i>Lev</i>. 27:34</a>. Quoted in the same sense in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Shabbat 104a" href="/Shabbat.104a">Babli <i>Šabbat</i> 104a</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Yoma 80b" href="/Yoma.80b"><i>Yoma</i> 80b</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Temurah 16b" href="/Temurah.16b"><i>Temurah</i> 16b</a>.</i>: <i>These are the commandments which the Eternal commanded to Moses</i>. These are the commandments which we were commanded by Moses’s saying. So did Moses say to us: No other prophet will reveal to you anything afterwards. And Mordocai and Esther want to introduce something new<sup class="footnote-marker">161</sup><i class="footnote">Since from the middle of Chapter 9 to the end of the Scroll the rules of Purim are written to be interpreted as biblical laws. This cannot be compared to Ḥanukkah which is a purely rabbinical time of remembrance. Writing new rules of biblical status is a clear violation of the prohibition <i>neither to add nor to subtract</i>(<a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 4:2" href="/Deuteronomy.4.2"><i>Deut</i>. 4:2</a>). This is a sufficient reason for Sadducees to reject the scroll and the attendant questionable activities.</i>? They did not move from there but discussed [the matter]<sup class="footnote-marker">162</sup><i class="footnote">Corrector’s addition in Babylonian style.</i> until the Holy One, praise to Him, illuminated their eyes and they found it written in the Torah, in Prophets, and in Hagiographs. This is what is written<sup class="footnote-marker">163</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 17:14" href="/Exodus.17.14"><i>Ex</i>. 17:14</a>.</i>, <i>the Eternal said to Moses</i>, <i>write this in a book for remembrance</i>. <i>This</i> is the Torah, as you are saying, <i>this is the Torah which Moses put before the Children of Israel</i><sup class="footnote-marker">164</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 4:44" href="/Deuteronomy.4.44"><i>Deut</i>. 4:44</a>.</i>. <i>Remembrance</i> are the Prophets, <i>a book of remembrance is being written before Him for those who fear the Eternal</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">165</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Malachi 3:16" href="/Malachi.3.16"><i>Mal</i>. 3:16</a>.</i> etc. <i>In a book</i> are the Hagiographs, <i>Esther’s word confirmed the matter of these Purim days and it was written in a book</i><sup class="footnote-marker">166</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Esther 9:32" href="/Esther.9.32"><i>Esth</i>. 9:32</a>.</i>.
Rav, and Rebbi Ḥanina, and Rebbi Jonathan, and Bar Qappara, and Rebbi Joshua ben Levi said, this scroll had be said to Moses on Sinai; only there is no earlier and later in the Torah<sup class="footnote-marker">167</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Pesachim 6a" href="/Pesachim.6a">Babli <i>Pesaḥim</i> 6a</a>. Since the Torah is a timeless document, the historical succession must be irrelevant. In philosophical terms, they support Mendelssohn and possibly Maimonides, proponents of an abstract Judaism, against Jehudah Hallevi’s Judaism as historical religion. The argument, <i>mutatis mutandis</i>, is found again in the attempts at justification of the medieval pseudepigraph <i>Zohar</i> as Tannaitic document.</i>. Rebbi Joḥanan and Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish. Rebbi Joḥanan said, Prophets and Hagiographs will in the future be nullified, but the five books of the Torah will never be nullified. What is the reason? <i>A strong voice</i>, <i>never ending</i><sup class="footnote-marker">168</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 5:19" href="/Deuteronomy.5.19"><i>Deut</i>. 5:19</a>.</i>. Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish said, also the Esther scroll and practices will never be nullified. It is said here, <i>a strong voice</i>, <i>never ending</i>, and it is said there, <i>their remembrance will not end from their descendants</i><sup class="footnote-marker">169</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Esther 9:28" href="/Esther.9.28"><i>Esth</i>. 9:28</a>.</i>. Practices? <i>His practices are eternal</i><sup class="footnote-marker">170</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Habakkuk 3:6" href="/Habakkuk.3.6"><i>Hab</i>. 3:6</a>. R. Joḥanan follows the first generation Amoraim in accepting Judaism as an abstract religion, which therefore is open to total re-interpretation [which even envisages disappearance of biblical holidays (<i>Midrash Mishle</i> 9[2]; Responsa Rashba #93.] R. Simeon ben Laqish accepts Judaism as historical religion whose rules do evolve but cannot be revolutionized.</i>.
Rebbi Levi in the name of Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish. The Holy One, praiuse to Him, saw that the evil Haman in the future would weigh his money against Israel. He said, it is better that my children’s money should precede this evil one’s money. Therefore one precedes and reads the paragraph about <i>Šeqalim</i><sup class="footnote-marker">171</sup><i class="footnote">A fundraising sermon concept to accompany the reading of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 30:11-16" href="/Exodus.30.11-16"><i>Ex</i>. 30:11–16</a> on the Sabbath preceding or falling on the New Moon of Adar (2). Originally this was introduced as a proclamation that the Temple tax was due. After the destruction of the Temple this reason disappeared and the Temple tax was turned into charity money and merged with the gifts due to the poor on Purim. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Megillah 13b" href="/Megillah.13b">Babli 13b</a>.</i>.
Rebbi Abbahu in the name of Rebbi Eleazar: <i>Every year by year</i><sup class="footnote-marker">172</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Esther 9:21" href="/Esther.9.21"><i>Esth</i>. 9:21</a>. The implication is that Purim should be celebrated in an intercalary year the way it is celebrated in a regular year.</i>. It bundled an intercalary year with one which is not intercalary. Since in a year which is not intercalary Adar is next to Nisan, also in an intercalary year Adar is next to Nisan. Rebbi Ḥelbo said, in order to make one liberation close to the other liberation<sup class="footnote-marker">173</sup><i class="footnote">In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Megillah 6b" href="/Megillah.6b">Babli 6b</a> both reasons are attributed to Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel. The opposing opinion of R. Eleazar ben R. Yose is not mentioned in the Yerushalmi.</i>.
Rebbi Levi in the name of Rebbi Ḥama bar Ḥanina: That year<sup class="footnote-marker">174</sup><i class="footnote">The year described in the Esther Scroll.</i> was intercalary. What is the reason? <i>From day to day</i>, <i>and from month to month</i>, <i>to the twelfth month which is the month of Adar</i><sup class="footnote-marker">175</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Esther 3:7" href="/Esther.3.7"><i>Esth</i>. 3:7</a>, describing Haman’s casting of lots (following the Babylonian calendar in which the intercalary month also was Adar.)</i>. Is the first Adar additional? Is the second Adar additional? What is the difference between them? Rebbi Samuel ben Rav Isaac said, the two lambs of Pentecost are between them<sup class="footnote-marker">176</sup><i class="footnote">They are prescribed to be yearlings. On the face of it, they have nothing to do with the rules of Adar since they are due in Siwan. The meaning of the statement is that if the first Adar is the addition, the second Adar is the last in the year and the year is stretched to 13 months. But if the second Adar is the addition then a month-year by definition has 12 months; the thirteenth month is neither of the preceding nor of the following year. (This argument is not found otherwise in the Talmudim.) In the first case, a lamb is a yearling up to the date of its birth in the following year; in the second case it is a yearling for 12 months from the date of its birth.</i>. If it was born on the fifteenth of Adar in a non-intercalary year and it enters an intercalary year; if you are saying that the first Adar is additional, it is a long year. If you are saying that the second Adar is additional, it<sup class="footnote-marker">176*</sup><i class="footnote">The lamb as a yearling.</i> has only up to the fifteenth of the first Adar. Rebbi Ayvo bar Naggari said, a Mishnah states that the first Adar is additional, as we have stated there<sup class="footnote-marker">177</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Eduyot 7:7" href="/Mishnah_Eduyot.7.7">Mishnah <i>Idiut</i> 7:7</a>.</i>: “They testified that one may intercalate the year during all of Adar; for there were those who said, until Purim. They testified that one may intercalate the year conditionally<sup class="footnote-marker">178</sup><i class="footnote">The calendar court has the entire month of Adar (necessarily the first) to decree the year as intercalary (and thereby changing the current Adar from 29 to 30 days) In the absence of the Patriarch the court may decree an intercalation subject to later approval by the Patriarch.</i>.” This implies that the first Adar is additional. If you would say that the second Adar is the addition<sup class="footnote-marker">179</sup><i class="footnote">See Note 176.</i>, it no longer is the year and he intercalates it?
