Mar Uqba found two letters<sup class="footnote-marker">180</sup><i class="footnote">This is the traditional style in which the Patriarch’s court announces intercalation; cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sanhedrin 1:2:17" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sanhedrin.1.2.17"><i>Sanhedrin</i>: Yerushalmi 1:1 Notes 179ff</a>., <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Megillah (Lieberman) 2:2-11" href="/Tosefta_Megillah_(Lieberman).2.2-11">Tosephta 2:2–11</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Megillah 11b" href="/Megillah.11b">Babli 11b</a>. In this case these were letters addressed to the Head of the Diaspora in Babylonia.</i>. In one was written, “it is good in my eyes and the eyes of my colleagues to add thirty days to the year.” And in one it was written, “it is good in my eyes and the eyes of my colleagues to add a month to the year.” He who says “thirty days” implies that the first Adar is additional<sup class="footnote-marker">181</sup><i class="footnote">Since as noted at the end, the regular Adar preceding Nisan has 29 days.</i>; he who says “a month” implies that the second Adar is additional. If from there, you cannot infer anything, as Rebbi Jacob bar Aḥa and Rebbi Yudan from Gezur, Rebbi Simon in the name of Rebbi Joshua ben Levi said, forever both months may be augmented<sup class="footnote-marker">182</sup><i class="footnote">The rule that the Adar preceding Nisan has 29 days is a desideratum; its violation does not invalidate any religious act.</i>. If he augmented the first and did not augment the second, what he did is well done. Always the Adar close to Nisan is defective<sup class="footnote-marker">183</sup><i class="footnote">Without explicit change from a competent authority.</i>.
<sup class="footnote-marker">184</sup><i class="footnote">A parallel text for this and the following paragraph is in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Shekalim 1:1:9" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Shekalim.1.1.9"><i>Šeqalim</i> 1:1, Notes 51</a> ff.</i> It was stated: Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel says, Obligations which apply to the Second Adar do not apply to the First Adar except for eulogy and for fasting which are equal for both<sup class="footnote-marker">185</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Megillah (Lieberman) 1:6" href="/Tosefta_Megillah_(Lieberman).1.6">Tosephta 1:6</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Megillah 6b" href="/Megillah.6b">Babli 6b</a>.</i>. Rebbi Abba, Rebbi Jeremiah in the name of Rav, Rebbi Simeon in the name of Rebbi Joshua ben Levi: Practice follows Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel. Rebbi Ḥuna, the rabbi of Sepphoris said, Rebbi Ḥanina made it a custom in Sepphoris following Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel. He only said custom, therefore not as practice<sup class="footnote-marker">186</sup><i class="footnote">It is obligatory only in places which adopted the ruling.</i>. But in matters of documents one writes the First Adar, the Second Adar, only that for the Second Adar one writes תִינָייָן. Rebbi Jehudah says, the Second Adar one writes ת, and it is enough<sup class="footnote-marker">187</sup><i class="footnote">In the anonymous opinion for both months one writes Adar, but the second one is qualified by “second” (in Aramaic, the language of contracts in both Talmudim.) R. Jehudah agrees that “Adar” alone means the First Adar in an intercalary year, but the Second may be indicated by a single letter ת֗, first letter of the Aramaic word “second”. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nedarim 63a" href="/Nedarim.63a">Babli <i>Nedarim</i> 63a</a>.</i>.
<sup class="footnote-marker">188</sup><i class="footnote">The origin of this paragraph is <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Shekalim 1:1:4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Shekalim.1.1.4"><i>Šeqalim</i> 1:1 (Notes 19</a> ff.); therefore the Mishnah here is quoted as “there”.</i> There, we have stated: “The only difference between a First Adar and a Second Adar is the reading of the Esther scroll and gifts to the poor”. Rebbi Simon in the name of Rebbi Joshua ben Levi, also proclamations regarding <i>sheqalim</i> and <i>kilaim</i> are between them<sup class="footnote-marker">189</sup><i class="footnote">Mishnah <i>Sheqalim</i> 1:1 in an intercalary year refers to the first day of Second Adar.</i>. Rebbi Ḥelbo and Rav Ḥuna, Rav in the name of the Great Rabbi Ḥiyya: Everybody may fulfill his obligation on the Fourteenth, which is the time of its reading<sup class="footnote-marker">67</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Shekalim 1:1:4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Shekalim.1.1.4"><i>Šeqalim</i> 1:1, Note 24</a>; also quoted twice in the sequel. He asserts that even though villagers may read early and inhabitants of walled cities have to read on the Fifteenth, reading on the Fourteenth satisfies the basic requirement. Those who are reading on both days, as mentioned later, because the tradition about the existence of a walled settlement in the days of Joshua bin Nun is not reliable, do not recite a benediction on the second day.</i>. Rebbi Yose said, this<sup class="footnote-marker">190</sup><i class="footnote">This refers to the statement of R. Simon.</i> is correct. Did they not say,. one proclaims about <i>sheqalim</i>, so that Israel should bring their <i>sheqalim</i> in time? If you would say on the First Adar, there still would be sixty days {left} in the year. Did they not say, also one goes to inspect for <i>kilaim</i>, not that the plants should be recognizable? If you would say in the First Adar, they still would be small.

Halakhah 6

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> The only difference between holiday and Sabbath refers to preparation of food<sup class="footnote-marker">191</sup><i class="footnote">There are many differences in the rules which are different for holidays and for the Sabbath. What is stated here is that what is biblically forbidden on the Sabbath (enumerated in <i>Sabbath</i> 7:2) is biblically forbidden on a holiday except anything used for the preparation of food which is explicitly exempted from prohibition (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 12:16" href="/Exodus.12.16"><i>Ex</i>. 12:16</a>.</i>. The only difference between Sabbath and the Day of Atonement is that in the first case its intentional desecration is punished by man and in the second case by extirpation<sup class="footnote-marker">192</sup><i class="footnote">Anything biblically forbidden on a Sabbath is forbidden on the Day of Atonement without exception (but eating is forbidden on the Day of Atonement and required on the Sabbath.)</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> <sup class="footnote-marker">193</sup><i class="footnote">Corrector’s addition. Except for the introductory sentence this is copied from <i>Yom Ṭov</i> 5:2 (Notes 82–88).</i>[It was stated in the name of Rebbi Jehudah, they also permitted actions preparatory to the preparation of food<sup class="footnote-marker">194</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Megillah (Lieberman) 1:7" href="/Tosefta_Megillah_(Lieberman).1.7">Tosephta 1:7</a>. Since the preparation of food is permitted on a holiday but prohibited on a Sabbath, there necessarily is a gray area where the decision between permitted and prohibited actions must be rabbinical by biblical standards.</i>. What is between them? Rav Ḥisday said, to sharpen the tip of the spit is between them. Rebbi Ḥanania the son of Rebbi Abbahu said, to produce fire from stones is between them. Rebbi Jehudah bar Pazi stated from Barzilah: what is the matter? Is the matter to sharpen the tip of the spit, or to produce fire from stones? You only have the following<sup class="footnote-marker">195</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Yom Ṭov</i> 3:8.</i>: “One does not sharpen the knife but he cleanses it on another.” Rav Ḥisday said, this is Rebbi Jehudah’s, for Rebbi Jehudah in the name of Samuel said, it is everybody’s opinion, to remove the fat on it.]
<sup class="footnote-marker">196</sup><i class="footnote">The paragraph is from <i>Yom Tov</i>5:2, Notes 77 ff., based on the quote from Mishnah 5:2.</i>“All these were said on a holiday, so much more on the Sabbath..” Are these it? Are there no others<sup class="footnote-marker">197</sup><i class="footnote">One proves that the list is incomplete.</i>? There is stoning on the Sabbath, there is no stoning on a holiday. There is extirpation on the Sabbath, there is no extirpation on a holiday. There is flogging on a holiday, there is no flogging on the Sabbath<sup class="footnote-marker">198</sup><i class="footnote">Desecrating the Sabbath is a capital crime, intentional desecration being punishable by stoning if there are witnesses, by divine extirpation otherwise. Desecration of a holiday is a simple transgression, intentional desecration before witnesses is punishable by flogging.</i>. If you would say, we are coming to state cases connected with food, did we not state: “one lowers produce through a skylight on the holiday but not on the Sabbath<sup class="footnote-marker">199</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Beitzah 5:1:1-8" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Beitzah.5.1.1-8">Mishnah <i>Yom Tov</i> 5:1</a>.</i>”? In addition from the following, one grinds aromatic wood for a circumcision on a holiday but not on the Sabbath<sup class="footnote-marker">200</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Shabbat 134a" href="/Shabbat.134a">Babli <i>Šabbat</i> 134a</a>.</i>. Rebbi Yose said, only if he circumcised<sup class="footnote-marker">201</sup><i class="footnote">Since grinding aromatic wood is not preparation of food it may be done if the necessity is clearly established, not in advance of an expected event.</i>. In addition from what was stated<sup class="footnote-marker">202</sup><i class="footnote">The following is copied from <i>Yom Tov</i> 4:2, Notes 60–61.</i>, “The Sages agree with Rebbi Meïr about seals in the ground that one pushes aside, and removes, and unties, and cuts. On the Sabbath one pushes aside but one does not remove nor untie nor cut. For implements on the Sabbath it is permitted; it is not necessary to mention on a holiday.”
<sup class="footnote-marker">203</sup><i class="footnote">This paragraph appears also in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Ketubot 3:1:2-19" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Ketubot.3.1.2-19"><i>Ketubot</i>3:1</a> (<b>כ</b>), and in slightly different form in <i>Terumot</i> 7:1, in both cases in a much longer discussion about payment for torts inflicted during the commission of a crime. It is commonly held that civil claims cannot be enforced if the perpetrator is subject to criminal prosecution in a capital case; there is dispute whether this extends to cases punishable by whipping and/or extirpation by judgment of Heaven.</i>“The only difference between Sabbath and the Day of Atonement is that in the first case its intentional desecration is punished by man and in the second case by extirpation.” Therefore, for payment both follow the same rules<sup class="footnote-marker">204</sup><i class="footnote">Payments for torts committed while desecrating the Day of Atonement are treated as if they were committed on the Sabbath.</i>. This is by Rebbi Neḥoniah ben Haqanah since Rebbi Neḥoniah ben Haqanah says, the Day of Atonement follows the rules of Sabbath for payment. But Rebbi Simeon ben Menassiah says those subject to extirpation equal those subject to capital punishment. What is between them? Rebbi Aḥa in the name of Rebbi Abuna said, a menstruating adolescent girl is between them<sup class="footnote-marker">205</sup><i class="footnote">If a single girl close to adulthood is raped, the rapist is subject to a fine and he has to marry her (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 22:28-29" href="/Deuteronomy.22.28-29"><i>Deut</i>. 22:28–29</a>). If he has sexual relations with her, he is subject to extirpation (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 20:18" href="/Leviticus.20.18"><i>Lev</i>. 20:18</a>).</i>. Rebbi Mana said, also his wife’s sister is between them<sup class="footnote-marker">206</sup><i class="footnote">The wife’s sister is forbidden during the wife’s lifetime (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 18:18" href="/Leviticus.18.18"><i>Lev</i>. 18:18</a>). No punishment is indicated in that verse, but in v. 26 all incest prohibitions are treated as equal; therefore it is implied that the wife’s sister is prohibited under penalty of extirpation by Heaven.</i>. In the opinion of Rebbi Neḥoniah ben Haqanah, just as the Sabbath does not become permitted after its prohibition started; so the Day of Atonement does not become permitted after its prohibition started. However, these may become permitted after their prohibition started, he has to pay. But Rebbi Simeon ben Menassiah said, extirpation applies to the Sabbath and the day of Atonement; extirpation does not apply to these, he has to pay. Rebbi Judah bar Pazi asked: What say these Tannaim about whipping and extirpation<sup class="footnote-marker">207</sup><i class="footnote">Since the prior discussion (not repeated here) was between R. Joḥanan and R. Simeon ben Laqish, it is of interest to know what are the positions of the Tannaim R. Neḥoniah ben Haqanah and R. Simeon ben Menassiah.</i>? Rebbi Yose said, that is a problem for the rabbis. Rebbi Jonah said, why can we not understand it from what Rebbi Simeon ben Yoḥai stated? As Rebbi Simeon ben Yoḥai stated: Rebbi Tarphon says, extirpation was mentioned for the Sabbath<sup class="footnote-marker">208</sup><i class="footnote">While desecrating the Sabbath is a capital crime, a desecration can be prosecuted in a human court only if the act was witnessed and criminal intent was proven by testimony that the perpetrator was duly warned before the act not to commit a capital crime. A Sabbath desecration not prosecutable in court is punishable by Heaven by extirpation (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 31:14" href="/Exodus.31.14"><i>Ex</i>. 31:14</a>).</i> and the Day of Atonement. Since for extirpation mentioned for the Sabbath there can be no whipping connected to extirpation<sup class="footnote-marker">209</sup><i class="footnote">In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Ketubot 34b-35a" href="/Ketubot.34b-35a">Babli, <i>Ketubot</i> 34b/35a</a>, this is deduced from <a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 21:22-23" href="/Exodus.21.22-23"><i>Ex</i>. 21:22–23</a>, where payment is due if a pregnant woman is injured and an abortion caused, but no payment is due if the woman is murdered.</i>, so for extirpation mentioned for the Day of Atonement there is no whipping in a case involving extirpation. Rebbi Mana said before Rebbi Yose: When do we need this? Not for Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish? But for Rebbi Joḥanan, if he admits flogging in death penalty cases, certainly flogging in extirpation cases, since they disagreed: If somebody slaughters an animal and its young for idolatrous purposes<sup class="footnote-marker">210</sup><i class="footnote">Slaughtering an animal and its young on the same day is a simple prohibition (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 22:28" href="/Leviticus.22.28"><i>Lev</i>. 22:28</a>), punishable by whipping. Slaughter as idolatrous sacrifice is a capital crime.</i>. Rebbi Joḥanan says, if he was cautioned about an animal and its young, he is flogged, about idolatry, he is stoned to death<sup class="footnote-marker">211</sup><i class="footnote">Since no conviction is possible without proof of criminal intent, actual prosecution is possible only if the nature of the crime to be committed was known to the perpetrator. For R. Joḥanan the rules apply only to cases that actually come before the court.</i>. Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish said, even if he was cautioned about an animal and its young, he is not flogged since he would be stoned to death had he been cautioned about idolatry<sup class="footnote-marker">212</sup><i class="footnote">For R. Simeon ben Laqish the rules of capital cases apply if capital punishment is a legal possibility even if the circumstances of the actual case prohibit its imposition.</i>. He said to him, we need it even for Rebbi Joḥanan! Here are two cases, there it is one case<sup class="footnote-marker">213</sup><i class="footnote">The prohibiton of slaughtering an animal and its young also applies to profane slaughter; it is unrelated to the prohibition of idolatry. The parallel discussion in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Chullin 81b" href="/Chullin.81b">Babli is <i>Ḥulin</i>81b</a>, where it is pointed out that one has to presume that the first animal was slaughtered for food but the second for idolatry; only in that case does one act result in two transgressions. But in the question asked here one deals with one transgression which, however, cannot be prosecuted for lack of witnesses.</i>.
<sup class="footnote-marker">214</sup><i class="footnote">This paragraph is the end of the long discussion about the possibility of fines if a crime was committed and has absolutely no connection with the topic discussed here. It is copied from <i>Ketubot</i> together with the preceding paragraph.</i> According to Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish, what is between these Tannaim and those rabbis<sup class="footnote-marker">215</sup><i class="footnote">The Tannaim quoted in the preceding paragraph and the rabbis (presumed to be R. Meïr and his students) who formulated <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Ketubot 3:1:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Ketubot.3.1.1">Mishnah <i>Ketubot</i> 3:1</a> which states that fines are due in all cases except capital crimes.</i>? Prohibitions without extirpation. Rebbi Judan said, he who sleeps with a bastard girl is between them<sup class="footnote-marker">216</sup><i class="footnote">The bastard is a child born from adulterous or incestuous sexual relations. It is stated in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 23:2" href="/Deuteronomy.23.2"><i>Deut</i>. 23:2</a>: <i>No bastard may come into the Eternal’s congregation</i>. This may be read (with Maimonides, <i>Issure Biah</i> 15:2) that marriage with a (male or female) bastard is punishable; then the rape of an unmarried bastard girl unquestionably requires a fine. Or it may be read (with RAVaD) that “come to” in rabbinic interpretation everywhere means sexual relations; then the rape of a bastard girl also incurs the penalty of flogging. Only relations and marriage between a male and a female certain bastards are permitted according to everybody.</i>. Rebbi Ḥananiah said, he who puts fire to the grain stack of his neighbor on a holiday<sup class="footnote-marker">217</sup><i class="footnote">Whose violation is punished by flogging; there is no extirpation.</i> is between them. These Tannaim think, since there is no extirpation, he pays. Those rabbis think, since there is flogging he does not pay. But then “these are the adolescent girls who can claim a fine” cannot follow the rabbis<sup class="footnote-marker">218</sup><i class="footnote">Since it clearly states that crimes punishable by Heaven incur fines in human courts.</i>! Rebbi Mattaniah said, explain it according to everybody if it refers to a bastard male who sleeps with a bastard girl<sup class="footnote-marker">216</sup><i class="footnote">The bastard is a child born from adulterous or incestuous sexual relations. It is stated in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 23:2" href="/Deuteronomy.23.2"><i>Deut</i>. 23:2</a>: <i>No bastard may come into the Eternal’s congregation</i>. This may be read (with Maimonides, <i>Issure Biah</i> 15:2) that marriage with a (male or female) bastard is punishable; then the rape of an unmarried bastard girl unquestionably requires a fine. Or it may be read (with RAVaD) that “come to” in rabbinic interpretation everywhere means sexual relations; then the rape of a bastard girl also incurs the penalty of flogging. Only relations and marriage between a male and a female certain bastards are permitted according to everybody.</i> But is his brother’s wife<sup class="footnote-marker">219</sup><i class="footnote">This is a separate Note, not connected to the preceding. According to the Mishnah the brother’s virgin wife is entitled to the fine if she is raped by the brother in law. If the brother is alive, it is adultery, a capital crime. If the brother is dead, since she still is a virgin she has no children. If the brother has no children from another woman, any sex act between her and her brother in law, even rape, makes her the brother in law’s wedded wife, with a claim to <i>ketubah</i> but no fine.</i> not his sister-in-law? Explain it that the man’s brother had children and had performed the preliminary wedding with a woman when he died and his brother came and raped her<sup class="footnote-marker">220</sup><i class="footnote">She is forbidden to him (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 18:16" href="/Leviticus.18.16"><i>Lev</i>. 18:16</a>) and by the argument of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Megillah 1:6:4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Megillah.1.6.4">Note 206</a> there is extirpation.</i>.

Halakhah 7

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> The only difference between one who is under a vow {not to have} usufruct from another<sup class="footnote-marker">221</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nedarim 4:1:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nedarim.4.1.1">Mishnah <i>Nedarim</i> 4:1</a>. A is restricted in that B made a vow that A may have no usufruct from him. (If A makes a vow that B may have no usufruct of him, the active mode is used.)</i> and one who is under a vow about use of food is passing through his real estate<sup class="footnote-marker">222</sup><i class="footnote">Setting his foot on the other’s property.</i> and vessels that cannot be used to prepare food. The only difference between vows and voluntary gifts<sup class="footnote-marker">223</sup><i class="footnote">Dedications of animals as Temple sacrifices.</i> is that for vows one is responsible if they died or were lost, and for voluntary gifts one is not responsible if they died or were lost.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> <sup class="footnote-marker">224</sup><i class="footnote">This is copied from <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nedarim 4:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nedarim.4.1"><i>Nedarim</i> 4:1 (Notes 5–12</a>, נ) since it refers to parts of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nedarim 4:1:1-8:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nedarim.4.1.1-8.1">Mishnah <i>Nedarim</i> 4</a>:1 not repeated in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Megillah 1:7:2-8:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Megillah.1.7.2-8.1"><i>Megillah</i> 1:9</a>. Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Nedarim 33a" href="/Nedarim.33a">Babli <i>Nedarim</i> 33a</a>.</i> Therefore vessels used to prepare food are forbidden. But did he not make the vow about food? Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish: so is the Mishnah: The only difference between one who is under a vow {not to have} usufruct from another and one who is under a vow about use for food. In the House of Rebbi it was stated thus: “One who is under a vow about use<sup class="footnote-marker">225</sup><i class="footnote">This is the text of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nedarim 4:1:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nedarim.4.1.1">Mishnah <i>Nedarim</i> 4:1</a> in the Yerushalmi; in the Babli “use for” is missing. It indicates that the original Yerushalmi Mishnah was the same as the Babli.</i> of food should not borrow from him a fine or coarse sieve, a grindstone, or an oven.” It was stated: But he may borrow from him cups and bowls and fruit bowls, since these are not used to prepare food, only to serve food. Pans<sup class="footnote-marker">226</sup><i class="footnote">Greek λοπάς.</i> and pots are forbidden. To mill grain and to press grapes is forbidden. Harvesting grain is a problem, harvesting grapes is a problem. What kind of usufruct of food? It was stated: But he may borrow an axe from him. We wanted to say, an axe to split. Explain it by a hatchet for weeding, and you cannot infer anything. Rebbi Abuna said, that <i>zwsta</i><sup class="footnote-marker">227</sup><i class="footnote">Neither the form given in <i>Megillah</i> nor that in <i>Nedarim</i> are satisfactorily explained.</i> since one removes and separates the bran from flour with it, it is like a sieve.
<sup class="footnote-marker">228</sup><i class="footnote">His obligation is extinguished only by the offering of a qualified animal as sacrifice, irrespective of other circumstances.</i>“What is the vow about which they spoke? He who says, on me is the obligation for an elevation offering<sup class="footnote-marker">229</sup><i class="footnote">His obligation is limited to this particular animal. If it dies or develops a disqualifying blemish, his obligation is terminated.</i>. What is the voluntary gift about which they spoke? He who says, this one is for an elevation offering.”<sup class="footnote-marker">230</sup><i class="footnote">Mishnah <i>Qinnim</i> 1:1. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Megillah 8a" href="/Megillah.8a">Babli 8a</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Rosh Hashanah 6a" href="/Rosh_Hashanah.6a"><i>Roš Haššanah</i> 6a</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Chullin 139a" href="/Chullin.139a"><i>Ḥulin</i> 139a</a>.</i> Rebbi Ḥama the colleague of the rabbis asked, if he said, “on me is the obligation”, and came back and said, “this one is”<sup class="footnote-marker">231</sup><i class="footnote">Is this a new obligation or is it a dedication of a specific animal intended to fulfill its prior general obligation? It is presumed that the second statement immediately follows the first.</i>? Rebbi Ḥinena asked, would it not be reasonable if he said, “this one is”, and came back and said, “on me is the obligation”? A severe prohibition falls one a simple prohibition but a simple prohibition does not fall on a severe prohibition<sup class="footnote-marker">232</sup><i class="footnote">In the second case, the voluntary gift is transformed into a vow. In the first case, the vow is not transformed into a voluntary gift.</i>.

Halakhah 8

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> The only difference between a sufferer from flux who experienced two episodes and one who experienced three is the sacrifice<sup class="footnote-marker">233</sup><i class="footnote">The impurity of the sufferer, a male from gonorrhea or a female from any genital emission, is the topic of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 15:1-30" href="/Leviticus.15.1-30"><i>Lev</i>. 15:1–30</a> and Tractate <i>Zavim</i>. After one emission of fluid the sufferer becomes impure; he needs immersion in a <i>miqweh</i> and becomes pure at sundown. If he has two emissions in one day or in two successive days, he is a source of severe impurity and cannot become pure unless he was seven consecutive days without symptoms. If he has three emissions in at most three consecutive days, he cannot participate in any kind of sacred offerings unless after his purification he brings a sacrifice of two birds (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 15:14" href="/Leviticus.15.14"><i>Lev</i>. 15:14</a>,<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 15:29" href="/Leviticus.15.29">29</a>).</i>. The only difference between a sufferer from skin disease who is quarantined and one declared absolute is torn clothing, untended hair, shaving, and birds<sup class="footnote-marker">234</sup><i class="footnote">In a number of cases of skin disease when the diagnosis is not clear cut, the sufferer is quarantined 7 or 14 days (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 13:4" href="/Leviticus.13.4"><i>Lev</i>. 13:4</a>,<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 13:5" href="/Leviticus.13.5">5</a>,<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 13:21" href="/Leviticus.13.21">21</a>,<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 13:26" href="/Leviticus.13.26">26</a>,<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 13:31" href="/Leviticus.13.31">31</a>. Even if after the quarantine the priest declares him not impure, he has to immerse himself and the clothing he wore during his quarantine in a <i>miqweh</i>, but during the quarantine he does not have to wear torn clothing and dishevelled hair like the person declared as certain sufferer (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 13:45" href="/Leviticus.13.45"><i>Lev</i>. 13:45</a>). After he is healed he still has to immerse himself and the clothing he wore during his impurity, but he does not have to undergo the purification ceremony described in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 14:1-32" href="/Leviticus.14.1-32"><i>Lev</i>. 14:1–32</a>. (Tractate <i>Nega`im</i>).</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> Rebbi Jehudah bar Pazi said, this is Rebbi Eleazar’s, for “Rebbi Eleazar said, [also] the third time one checks him out because of the sacrifice.<sup class="footnote-marker">235</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Zavim 2:2" href="/Mishnah_Zavim.2.2">Mishnah <i>Zavim</i> 2:2</a>. The Mishnah states that the male sufferer from flux is impure only if he has a discharge for a medical condition. If it is possible that the discharge is an ejaculation of semen because of some exterior influence, he still is impure and needs immersion but this is not cumulative and does not start a count. Then the Mishnah restricts this investigation to the first two times. A third episode within 3 days establishes a pattern; the cause must be medical.<br>The majority of sources including Maimonides’s autograph Mishnah (and the organization of the Mishnah) indicate that the name is correctly “Eleazar” and not “Eliezer”.</i>” But for the rabbis, the second time if forced he is pure, but the third time if forced is impure<sup class="footnote-marker">236</sup><i class="footnote">According to the anonymous majority, the Mishnah should mention that there is another difference between two and three times flux, that the second may be checked and eliminated whereas the third may not.</i>. Rebbi Yose said, if it is not the rabbis’, it also is not Rebbi Eleazar’s, as it was stated: Rebbi Eleazar said, I did not say to undo his count<sup class="footnote-marker">237</sup><i class="footnote">He agrees that two consecutive counts establish a pattern which creates severe impurity but he holds that an obligatory sacrifice may be brought only if the obligation is clearly established, whereas impurity in most cases has to be observed on a suspicion.</i>, only that he may not bring a sacrifice. I did not say that he is not a sufferer from flux to undo the count of episodes; not to upset his status did I say but that he may not bring a sacrifice. This implies that it<sup class="footnote-marker">238</sup><i class="footnote">Mishnah 10.</i> is everybody’s opinion. If you would say it is Rebbi Eleazar’s it should state: the second time is subject to undoing but not a sacrifice, the third time to undoing and sacrifice<sup class="footnote-marker">239</sup><i class="footnote">Similar to what was noted for the majority in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Megillah 1:8:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Megillah.1.8.2">Note 236</a>, but with a different background.</i>. In Rebbi Eleazar’s opinion, if she experienced two episodes of easy flux<sup class="footnote-marker">240</sup><i class="footnote">Which establishes severe impurity.</i> and one in difficulty she undoes<sup class="footnote-marker">241</sup><i class="footnote">If the third time would not be counted had it been the second, it cannot be counted for a sacrifice. If therefore an unquestionable third episode happens on a later date, the count for sacrifice has to start anew.</i> and does not bring a sacrifice.
<sup class="footnote-marker">242</sup><i class="footnote">Here starts the discussion of the second part of the Mishnah, the sufferer from skin disease.</i> Rebbi Yudan said: The Mishnah is about two persons<sup class="footnote-marker">243</sup><i class="footnote">Not the rare case of a person about to be declared pure after quarantine when suddenly in the presence of the Cohen signs of impurity appear; Mishnah <i>Nega`im</i> 7:3.</i>. Rebbi Ḥanina said, the Mishnah says so, “the only difference between a sufferer from skin disease who is quarantined and one declared absolute is torn clothing, untended hair, shaving, and birds.” This implies that we are dealing with two persons. If you would say about one person, one should state: “the only difference between a large white spot and a small white spot.<sup class="footnote-marker">244</sup><i class="footnote">A large white spot is one looking depressed and containing a white hair which is impure (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 13:3" href="/Leviticus.13.3"><i>Lev</i>. 13:3</a>). A small one is one not looking depressed and without a white hair (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 13:4" href="/Leviticus.13.4"><i>Lev</i>. 13:4</a>) which requires quarantine.</i>” Just as you are saying there, we are dealing with two persons, so also here we are dealing with two persons<sup class="footnote-marker">245</sup><i class="footnote">Also the first sentence of the Mishnah refers to two different people.</i>.
If it spread while in quarantine<sup class="footnote-marker">246</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 13:13" href="/Leviticus.13.13"><i>Lev</i>. 13:13</a>. If the entire skin of the patient is diseased, he is pure. Naturally while the disease is spreading he satisfies all conditions of severe purity, but since this requires a pronouncement by the Cohen, if there were no clear signs of impurity when he was put in quarantine and at the end of the quarantine the entire skin already was diseased, the Cohen who sees him only at the start and the end of his 7 days of quarantine has to declare him pure out of quarantine without ever pronouncing him absolutely impure.</i>, Rebbi Joḥanan said, he needs birds. Rebbi Eleazar said, he does not need birds. Rebbi Abba bar Mamal said, a <i>baraita</i> supports Rebbi Joḥanan: <sup class="footnote-marker">247</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Sifra Mesora`</i> Introduction 6–8.</i>“<i>From the sufferer from skin disease</i><sup class="footnote-marker">248</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 14:3" href="/Leviticus.14.3"><i>Lev</i>. 14:3</a>. As introduction to the bird ceremony the Cohen has “to see that the skin disease was healed from the sufferer from skin disease.” Skin disease can be healed only from a sufferer from the disease; the final remark seems to be redundant. It is added to include also a sufferer who never was declared as such.</i>; to include one on whom it spread on his entire person that he needs birds. Is that not a logical argument? If a person became pure and the signs of what made him impure are not on him shall need birds<sup class="footnote-marker">249</sup><i class="footnote">The disappearance of his symptoms is the sign that he is healed, in contrast to the person who is pure but far from healed in that all his skin is infected.</i>, (should not) [is it not logical that]<sup class="footnote-marker">250</sup><i class="footnote">The corrector’s changes are from the (Babylonian style) <i>Sifra</i>.</i> one who became pure and the signs of what made him impure are on him [shall] need birds? [But] the one on whom it was stable for two weeks<sup class="footnote-marker">251</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 13:6" href="/Leviticus.13.6"><i>Lev</i>. 13:6</a>. If the white spot does not grow within 14 days nor develop a white hair, the person has to be declared pure (after immersion) even though his problem skin is still visible.</i> shall disprove it, since he became pure and the signs of what made him impure are on him, but he does not need birds. So do not wonder if one on whom it spread while in quarantine and the signs of what made him impure are on him does not need birds. The verse says, <i>from the sufferer of skin disease</i> to include one on whom it spread on his entire person that he needs birds.” If you would say that one becoming pure in quarantine does not need birds, should he not have objected that it would have been better to argue spreading against spreadings and not staying stable against spreadings<sup class="footnote-marker">252</sup><i class="footnote">This is the proof that the <i>Sifra</i> supports the opinion of R. Joḥanan since following R. Eleazar instead of appealing to purity after a lengthy quarantine the Tanna should have mentioned the case of fast spreading skin disease which at the next inspection by the Cohen already has changed from a sign of impurity to one of purity and does not need birds.</i>? Rebbi Ḥananiah the colleague of the rabbis: the <i>baraita</i> supports Rebbi Joḥanan since the Tanna answers his colleague, no. If you are saying about this one who never was up to be declared absolute, what can you say about the one who was to be declared absolute<sup class="footnote-marker">253</sup><i class="footnote">The case of dispute between R. Joḥanan and R. Eleazar is not comparable to other cases of skin disease since the Cohen is not empowered to inspect during the quarantine; the patient never was in a state to be declared absolutely impure.</i>? Because he was to be declared absolute, he needs birds. Rebbi Jacob bar Aḥa said, the closing statement<sup class="footnote-marker">254</sup><i class="footnote">The second sentence of the Mishnah.</i> supports Rebbi Eleazar. The one becoming pure in quarantine is not liable for torn clothing, and untended hair, and shaving, and birds. All of this is what we are considering here, about spreadings<sup class="footnote-marker">255</sup><i class="footnote">This seems to refer to a statement similar to the wording of the Mishnah in the Babli: The only difference between one declared pure after quarantine and one declared pure after being absolutely impure is shaving and birds. Since this envisages a situation like the one discussed here, it explicitly supports the Babylonian R. Eleazar.</i>. Rebbi Samuel bar Eudaimon said, all they are disagreeing is about bringing birds. But in the matter of bringing a sacrifice everybody agrees that he does not bring a sacrifice. It was stated thus: On the seventh he has to shave, on the eighth he shall bring<sup class="footnote-marker">256</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 14:9" href="/Leviticus.14.9"><i>Lev</i>. 14:9</a>,<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 14:10" href="/Leviticus.14.10">10</a>.</i>; one who needs shaving brings a sacrifice, one who does not need shaving does not bring a sacrifice. Rebbi Ḥama bar Uqba in the name of Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Ḥanina: <i>All days on which the skin disease is on him he will be impure</i><sup class="footnote-marker">257</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 13:46" href="/Leviticus.13.46"><i>Lev</i>. 13:46</a>.</i>, one whose impurity is caused by his skin disease. This excludes him whose (purity) [impurity]<sup class="footnote-marker">258</sup><i class="footnote">Both the scribe’s and the corrector’s texts give the same meaning; the correction is unnecessary.</i> depends on the count of his days<sup class="footnote-marker">259</sup><i class="footnote">Since the Cohen cannot judge him during the intermediate days of his quarantine.</i>. So far torn clothing and unkempt hair. From where shaving and birds<sup class="footnote-marker">260</sup><i class="footnote">What is the biblical source of the statement of the Mishnah regarding these items?</i>? Rebbi Eleazar the Southerner in the name of Rebbi Shammai: <i>This shall be the doctrine of the sufferer from skin disease on the day</i><sup class="footnote-marker">261</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 14:2" href="/Leviticus.14.2"><i>Lev</i>. 14:2</a>.</i>. One who may become impure and pure on one day; this excludes one who cannot become impure and pure on one day<sup class="footnote-marker">262</sup><i class="footnote">Since quarantine makes impure for a minimum of 7 days.</i>.

Halakhah 9

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> The only difference between Torah scrolls and Tefillin<sup class="footnote-marker">263</sup><i class="footnote">Boxes containing parchment with the verses <a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 13:2-12" href="/Exodus.13.2-12"><i>Ex</i>. 13:2–12</a>,<a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 11-16" href="/Exodus.11-16">11–16</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 6:4-9" href="/Deuteronomy.6.4-9"><i>Deut</i>. 6:4–9</a>,<a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 11:13-21" href="/Deuteronomy.11.13-21">11:13–21</a>, “to be tied to the arms and be a sign between the eyes.”</i> and Mezuzot<sup class="footnote-marker">264</sup><i class="footnote">A single sheet with the same verses, “to be affixed to the doorposts of your house and your gates.”</i> is that Torah scrolls may be written in any language<sup class="footnote-marker">265</sup><i class="footnote">In translation for the purpose of study.</i> but Tefillin and Mezuzot may be written only in Hebrew square script. Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel says, also Torah scrolls they only permitted to be written in Greek<sup class="footnote-marker">266</sup><i class="footnote">In translation, not in phonetic transcription into Greek letters as in Origenes’s Hexapla.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> It is written<sup class="footnote-marker">267</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Genesis 11:1" href="/Genesis.11.1"><i>Gen</i>. 11:1</a>.</i> <i>all Earth was one tongue and unified words</i>. Rebbi Eleazar and Rebbi Joḥanan. One said, they were speaking seventy languages<sup class="footnote-marker">268</sup><i class="footnote">Everybody understood all languages of the 70 peoples mentioned as descendants of Noah.</i>. But the other said, they were speaking in the language of the Unique of the World, the holy language. Bar Qappara stated, <i>may God embellish Japhet and dwell in the tents of Shem</i><sup class="footnote-marker">269</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Genesis 9:27" href="/Genesis.9.27"><i>Gen</i>. 9:27</a>.</i>, that one should speak Japhet’s language in Shem’s tents<sup class="footnote-marker">270</sup><i class="footnote">Greek was the common language of all Jews in the Roman Empire.</i>. <i>The sons of Japhet: Gomer and Magog</i>, <i>and Yawan</i>, <i>and Tubal</i>, <i>and Meshekh</i>, <i>and Tiras</i><sup class="footnote-marker">271</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Genesis 10:2" href="/Genesis.10.2"><i>Gen</i>. 10:2</a>.</i>. Gomer, Germamia<sup class="footnote-marker">272</sup><i class="footnote">The district Jerma in N. Assyria. It is to be noted that Saadia Gaon in his Arabic Pentateuch translation disregards the determinations both of the Yerushalmi and the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Yoma 10a" href="/Yoma.10a">Babli (<i>Yoma</i> 10a</a>) for the descendants of Sem and Japhet but follows the determinations given here for the descendants of Ham (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Genesis 10:18-19" href="/Genesis.10.18-19"><i>Gen</i>. 10:18–19</a>).</i>. Magog, Gothica. Madai, as it is understood<sup class="footnote-marker">273</sup><i class="footnote">Media.</i>. Yawan, Ephesus<sup class="footnote-marker">274</sup><i class="footnote">Representing all of Ionia.</i>. Tuval, Bythinia. Meshekh, Moesia. Tiras, Rebbi Simon said Persia, but the rabbis said Thrace. <i>The sons of Gomer: Ashkenaz</i>, <i>and Riphat</i>, <i>and Togarma</i><sup class="footnote-marker">275</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Genesis 10:3" href="/Genesis.10.3"><i>Gen</i>. 10:3</a>.</i>. Asia<sup class="footnote-marker">276</sup><i class="footnote">Not the continent, nor Asia Minor, but probably the city of Sardis.</i>, and Adiabene, and Germanicia<sup class="footnote-marker">277</sup><i class="footnote">A city in the province of Comagene, near the border of Cappadocia.</i>. <i>The sons of Yawan: Elisha, and Tarshish</i>, <i>Kittim and Dodanim</i><sup class="footnote-marker">278</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Genesis 10:4" href="/Genesis.10.4"><i>Gen</i>. 10:4</a>.</i>. Elis<sup class="footnote-marker">279</sup><i class="footnote">Possibly Aeolis. Note that in the text two names are written as one.</i>, Tarsos, Euboea, and Dardania<sup class="footnote-marker">280</sup><i class="footnote">Dardanos in the Troas.</i>. <i>The Arwadite</i><sup class="footnote-marker">281</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Genesis 10:18" href="/Genesis.10.18"><i>Gen</i>. 10:18</a>.</i>, Rhodos. <i>The Ṣamarite</i>, Ḥoms. <i>The Ḥamatite</i>, Ḥama. <i>Up to</i> Lasha`. Rebbi Eleazar said, to Kallirrhoe. Rebbi Yudan bar Shalom said, from here about the Targum<sup class="footnote-marker">282</sup><i class="footnote">Since the names in the Hebrew text are unintelligible, the text itself authorizes translations which make it intelligible.</i>.
Rebbi Jonathan from Bet Guvrrin said, four languages are appropriate that the world should use them, and they are these: The Foreign Language<sup class="footnote-marker">283</sup><i class="footnote">Greek, the non-Hebrew language common to the Jews in the Roman Empire.</i> for song, Latin for war, Syriac for elegies, Hebrew for speech. Some are saying, also Assyrian<sup class="footnote-marker">284</sup><i class="footnote">Hebrew square script, adopted from the Aramaic script of Northern Iraq, used by the Persian administration of the territories West of the Euphrates for their Reichsaramaic.</i> for writing.
Assyrian has a script but no language; Hebrew has a language but no script. They choose for themselves Assyrian script and Hebrew language<sup class="footnote-marker">285</sup><i class="footnote">This refers to the tradition quoted in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sanhedrin 21a" href="/Sanhedrin.21a">Babli, <i>Sanhedrin</i> 21a</a>: “Originally the Torah was given to Israel in Hebrew script and the Holy Language. It was given to them a second time in the days of Ezra in Assyrian script and Aramaic language. Israel chose for themselves Assyrian script and the Holy language.” For this entire passage the Babli reference is <i>Sanhedrin</i> 21b–22a.</i>. Why is it called Assyrian? Because it is beautiful script; Rebbi Levi said, because they brought it with them from Assyria<sup class="footnote-marker">286</sup><i class="footnote">In the first version the name of the script has nothing to do with Assyria. In the second it is asserted that it is the Aramaic script of what earlier was Assyria.</i>. It was stated: Rebbi Yose said, Ezra was worthy that the Torah could have been given through him, only Moses’s generation preceded him. Even though the Torah was not given through him, but he gave writing and language<sup class="footnote-marker">285</sup><i class="footnote">This refers to the tradition quoted in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sanhedrin 21a" href="/Sanhedrin.21a">Babli, <i>Sanhedrin</i> 21a</a>: “Originally the Torah was given to Israel in Hebrew script and the Holy Language. It was given to them a second time in the days of Ezra in Assyrian script and Aramaic language. Israel chose for themselves Assyrian script and the Holy language.” For this entire passage the Babli reference is <i>Sanhedrin</i> 21b–22a.</i>; <i>and the script of the letter written in Aramaic and explained in Aramaic</i><sup class="footnote-marker">287</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Ezra 4:7" href="/Ezra.4.7"><i>Ezra</i> 4:7</a>.</i>. <i>And they could not read this script</i><sup class="footnote-marker">288</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Daniel 5:8" href="/Daniel.5.8"><i>Dan</i>. 5:8</a>.</i>; this teaches that it was given on that day<sup class="footnote-marker">289</sup><i class="footnote">The Babylonian sages could not read the script on the wall because it was new. This claims divine origin for the square script.</i>. Rebbi Nathan says, the Torah was given in paleo-Hebrew; this follows Rebbi Yose<sup class="footnote-marker">290</sup><i class="footnote">The Babylonian R. Nathan follows the Babylonian tradition that traces Targum Onkelos to Ezra and asserts that he transcribed the Torah into Aramaic script.</i>. Rebbi said, the Torah was given in Assyrian, but when they sinned it was changed into paleo-Hebrew. When they merited it in the days of Ezra it was changed into Assyrian: <i>Also today I shall return to you what was told to change</i><sup class="footnote-marker">291</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Zechariah 9:12" href="/Zechariah.9.12"><i>Zach</i>. 9:12</a>. The translation here tries to express the homily implied by the quotes.</i><i>; he shall write for himself this changing Torah in a scroll</i><sup class="footnote-marker">292</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 17:18" href="/Deuteronomy.17.18"><i>Deut</i>. 17:18</a>.</i>, a script which in the future is apt to change. It was stated: Rebbi Simeon ben Eleazar says in the name of Rebbi Eleazar ben Protos who said it in the name of Rebbi Eleazar from Modiin, the Torah was given in Assyrian script. What is the reason? <i>The hooks of the pillars</i><sup class="footnote-marker">293</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 27:10" href="/Exodus.27.10"><i>Ex</i>. 27:10</a>.</i>, that the letters <i>vav</i> of the Torah look like pillars<sup class="footnote-marker">294</sup><i class="footnote">In paleo-Hebrew the letter <i>vav</i>, meaning “hook”, really looks like a hook on a stick. In square script the hook is lost, only the stick is left.</i>. Rebbi Levi said, for him who said, the Torah was given in paleo-Hebrew, the letter <i>ayin</i> was a miracle<sup class="footnote-marker">295</sup><i class="footnote">This does not refer to the Torah but to the stone tablets. From the description that the tablets were written on both sides it is inferred that the letters pierced the stone; the same letters were visible on both sides. This creates a problem for circular shaped letters, <i>ayin</i> in paleo-Hebrew and <i>samekh</i> in square script.</i>. He who said, the Torah was given Assyrian, the letter <i>samekh</i> was a miracle. Rebbi Jeremiah in the name of Rebbi Ḥiyya bar Abba and Rebbi Simon both were saying, in earlier copies of the Torah neither <i>he</i> nor final <i>mem</i> were closed<sup class="footnote-marker">296</sup><i class="footnote">The open final <i>mem</i> is exemplified in the Aramaic inscription of King Uziahu’s ossuary. In early Medieval mss. the <i>he</i> looks like a ח, only that the left leg is not at the left end but touching the vertical bar somewhat to the right. The open <i>he</i> is recommended in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Menachot 29b" href="/Menachot.29b">Babli, <i>Menaḥot</i> 29b</a>. For a thorough discussion, cf. S. Liebermann, <i>Tarbiz</i> 4 (1933) pp. 292–293.</i>. Therefore <i>samekh</i> was closed<sup class="footnote-marker">297</sup><i class="footnote">This justifies R. Levi’s remark that only <i>samekh</i> but not final <i>mem</i> represented a problem.</i>.
It was stated: Rabban Simeon ben Gamliel says, even Torah scrolls they only permitted to be written in Greek. They investigated and found that the Torah may be correctly translated only into Greek. A villager invented for them Aramaic from the Greek<sup class="footnote-marker">298</sup><i class="footnote">This disqualifies all Aramaic translations, even Targum Onkelos canonized in Babylonia.</i>. Rebbi Jeremiah in the name of Rebbi Ḥiyya bar Abba: Akylas the proselyte translated the Torah<sup class="footnote-marker">299</sup><i class="footnote">Into Greek. In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Megillah 3a" href="/Megillah.3a">Babli 3a</a> he is credited with the Aramaic translation as restoration of Ezra’s Aramaic translation.</i> before Rebbi Eliezer and Rebbi Joshua; they praised him [and said to him], <i>you are a superhuman beauty</i><sup class="footnote-marker">300</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Psalms 45:3" href="/Psalms.45.3"><i>Ps</i>. 45:3</a>.</i>. Rebbi Jehudah bar Pazy said, Rebbi Joshua ben Levi asked, why do we not say there is a rule valid for Torah scrolls which does not apply to Tefillin and Mezuzot, in that Torah scrolls may be written on two sheets<sup class="footnote-marker">301</sup><i class="footnote">Even a single book of the Torah is written on many sheets which then are sewn together. The short texts of Tefillin and Mezuzot must be written on single sheets. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Menachot 33a" href="/Menachot.33a">Babli <i>Menaḥot</i> 33a</a>.</i>, but Tefillin and Mezuzot may only be written on single sheets. Rebbi Isaac the son of Rebbi Ḥiyya the scribe asked, a stronger question, in that Torah scrolls may be written on two hides<sup class="footnote-marker">302</sup><i class="footnote">The leather on which the scroll is written does not have to be prepared from a single hide.</i>, but Tefillin and Mezuzot may only be written on single hides. The colleagues said before Rebbi Mana, or should we say, one column of two different hides? Is that not stronger? He said to them, because even scrolls are not written in this way<sup class="footnote-marker">303</sup><i class="footnote">Rectangular sheets of leather may be sewn together to form a scroll; the stiches must be between the columns and may not intrude into the writing.</i>. One suspends in scrolls<sup class="footnote-marker">304</sup><i class="footnote">If a letter or a word in the text was forgotten it may be added between the lines over of the place to which it belongs.</i>; one suspends neither in Tefillin nor in Mezuzot. One does not suspend in scrolls which were written as Tefillin and Mezuzot; one suspends in Tefillin and Mezuzot written as scrolls<sup class="footnote-marker">305</sup><i class="footnote">If in writing the paragraphs required for Tefillin or Mezuzot one has the choice to use the written text either as part of a scroll or as Tefillin or Mezuzah, one must follow the more stringent rules. But if these paragraphs are written as part of columns of a scroll, they cannot be used in Tefillin or Mezuzot and errors may be corrected by suspending the missing letters or words.</i>. Rebbi Ze`ira in the name of Rebbi Immi: If he was writing אלא עם, if the paragraph was made in this way it is qualified, otherwise disqualified<sup class="footnote-marker">306</sup><i class="footnote">If a line is short, in this example 6 spaces (the Babli requires a minimum of 9) it is acceptable if and only if the entire column is equally slim.</i>. Rebbi Ze`ira in the name of Rebbi Immi bar Ḥinena: The script of scrolls is used as script of Tefillin and Mezuzot<sup class="footnote-marker">307</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Menachot 29b" href="/Menachot.29b">Babli <i>Menaḥot</i> 29b</a>.</i>.
Rebbi Ze`ira in the name of Rav: If there is a hole in the hip of a <i>he</i>, if one shaves it off and there remains a small hip it is qualified, otherwise it is disqualified<sup class="footnote-marker">308</sup><i class="footnote">If there is a hole in the leather where the right hand side vertical stroke of ה is written, the letter is not complete and therefore unacceptable. Rav permits to shave off the ink around the hole and below. If a small vertical stroke is left, the letter now is legible and entire; the text may be used for public reading from the scroll or as Tefillin or Mezuzah.</i>. Rebbi Ze`ira in the name of Rav Ḥisda: If a <i>gimel</i> was at the end (of the border) [of the leather]<sup class="footnote-marker">309</sup><i class="footnote">It seems that the corrector’s change is the result of his misunderstanding the text. It is required that each letter in the text be surrounded by blank space. If a letter reaches the border of the sheet, Rav Ḥisda permits shaving off a little bit to create the blank space if the remaining writing still is legible.</i>. If one shaves it off and there remains a small hip it is qualified, otherwise it is disqualified. Rebbi Ze`ira in the name of Ashian bar Nidba<sup class="footnote-marker">310</sup><i class="footnote">In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Menachot 29a" href="/Menachot.29a">Babli <i>Menaḥot</i> 29a</a> his name is Ashian bar Nidbakh.</i>. If there was a hole in the middle of a <i>bet</i>. If the (border) [leather] surrounds it on every side it is qualified, otherwise it is disqualified<sup class="footnote-marker">311</sup><i class="footnote">He denies that the space around the letter can be created by shaving off some ink from the letter in question. He then requires that the entire letter be removed and rewritten.</i>. Rebbi Ze`ira said, it turns out that Rav Ḥisda follows Rav and both disagree with the system of Ashian bar Nidba. The colleagues are saying, Ashian bar Nidba parallels Rebbi Joḥanan.
<sup class="footnote-marker">312</sup><i class="footnote">The following two paragraphs are also in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Berakhot 2:3:16-19" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Berakhot.2.3.16-19"><i>Berakhot</i> 2:3 (Notes 145–152</a>), as part of a series of statements of R. Idi in the name of R. Simon. Factually the first paragraph has no relevance in <i>Berakhot</i> and the second does not fit here.</i> As it was stated. If he mixed the letters. There are Tannaim who state, it is qualified. There are Tannaim who state, it is disqualified. Rebbi Ada bar Simeon<sup class="footnote-marker">313</sup><i class="footnote">As noted, read: R. Idi in the name of R. Simon (ben Laqish).</i> in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: He who says qualified, at the top. He who says disqualified, at the bottom; for example ארצך. תפארתינו ,ארצינו is questionable, תפארתך is questionable<sup class="footnote-marker">314</sup><i class="footnote">(Rashba Responsa 1:711.) If a letter is completely written and its foot accidentally touches the following letter, as with a slightly too long foot of נ in נו, one may scratch off the superfluous length and has a complete acceptable letter. But when a letter has its head elongated, so that from the start the letter was not standing free, then the entire letter has to be scratched out and the writing has to start anew. The questionable case is a situation with a letter that is hanging down below the line, as in the combination תך when the small foot of ת meets the downward stroke of ך. In that case, the ת was complete but the ך was not; this case remains unsettled (i. e., for practical rulings both letters have to be erased and rewritten.)</i>.
Rebbi Aḥa the son of Rebbi Simeon<sup class="footnote-marker">313</sup><i class="footnote">As noted, read: R. Idi in the name of R. Simon (ben Laqish).</i> in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: No man should stand at an elevated place and pray. What is the reason? Rebbi Abba the son of R. Pappai said: <i>From the depth I call on You</i>, <i>Eternal!</i><sup class="footnote-marker">315</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Psalms 130:1" href="/Psalms.130.1"><i>Ps</i>. 130:1</a>.</i> Rebbi Ada bar Simeon in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan said: No man should pray when he has an urge to go to the bathroom. What is the reason? <i>Israel</i>, <i>prepare yourself before Your God</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">316</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Amos 4:12" href="/Amos.4.12"><i>Am</i>. 4:12</a>.</i> Rebbi Alexandri said: <i>Watch your feet when you go to God’s house</i><sup class="footnote-marker">317</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Ecclesiastes 4:17" href="/Ecclesiastes.4.17"><i>Eccl</i>. 4:17</a>.</i>; watch yourself from the drops that drip from between your feet. That means, for urine. But for defecation, if he can bear it, let him bear it. Rebbi Jacob bar Abiah in the name of Rebbi Aḥa: <i>Watch your feet when you go to God’s house</i>; watch yourself when you are called to God’s house that you should be pure and innocent. Rebbi Abba said: <i>Your fountain shall be blessed</i><sup class="footnote-marker">318</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Proverbs 5:18" href="/Proverbs.5.18"><i>Prov</i>. 5:18</a>.</i>, your being called to the grave shall be blessed. Rebbi Berekhiah said: <i>A time to be born and a time to die</i><sup class="footnote-marker">319</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Ecclesiastes 3:2" href="/Ecclesiastes.3.2"><i>Eccl</i>. 3:2</a>.</i>, hail to the man whose hour of death is like the hour of his birth; just as at the hour of his birth he was innocent so at the hour of his death may he be innocent.
Rebbi Abba in the name of Rav Jehudah: if it was made like half a bagel<sup class="footnote-marker">320</sup><i class="footnote">If a Mezuzah is written in calligraphy in the shape of half-circles one inside the other then the inner arcs are shorter than the outer ones and contain fewer words.</i>. The uppermost line under which are two others must contain at least three words, the middle one two, the lowest one even [<i>on the</i>] <i>earth</i><sup class="footnote-marker">321</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 11:21" href="/Deuteronomy.11.21"><i>Deut</i>. 11:21</a>. The addition by the corrector clearly has to be deleted.</i>. Rebbi Ze`ira in the name of Rav Ḥisda: if it was made like a double flute<sup class="footnote-marker">322</sup><i class="footnote">Greek συμφωνία. It is \_/-shaped, having the two mouth pieces close together but the bodies of the flutes diverging.</i>, the uppermost line under which are two others must contain at least three words, the lowest two. The middle one I do not know<sup class="footnote-marker">323</sup><i class="footnote">Whether it need 2 or 3 words. Therefore 3 certainly are sufficient. The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Menachot 30b" href="/Menachot.30b">Babli <i>Menaḥot</i> 30b</a> quotes the first Tanna as R. Yose (whom Babylonian practice follows), the second one as R. Jehudah’s.</i>. Rebbi Jeremiah said it: Rebbi Ze`ira in the name of Rav Ḥisda; Rebbi Jonah and Rebbi Yose both say, Rebbi Ze`ura in the name of Ashian bar Nidba; the colleagues say, Rebbi Ze`ira in the name of Rav Ḥananel. If the ink comes out of the holes it is disqualified. What does he do? He licks it off with his tongue and it jells<sup class="footnote-marker">323</sup><i class="footnote">Whether it need 2 or 3 words. Therefore 3 certainly are sufficient. The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Menachot 30b" href="/Menachot.30b">Babli <i>Menaḥot</i> 30b</a> quotes the first Tanna as R. Yose (whom Babylonian practice follows), the second one as R. Jehudah’s.</i>. If he made an error and omitted the Name. There are Tannaim who state, he suspends the Name. There are Tannaim who state, he erases the profane, writes the Name, and suspends the profane<sup class="footnote-marker">324</sup><i class="footnote">If there is a hole in the leather or parchment which is visible once the ink has dried, the letter is incomplete and disqualified. But if the ink when dry covers the hole completely so that the reader will not notice the hole, it is qualified. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Shabbat 108a" href="/Shabbat.108a">Babli <i>Šabbat</i> 108a</a>.</i>. Rebbi Ze`ira, Rav Ḥananel in the name of Rav: practice follows him who says, he erases the profane, writes the Name, and suspends the profane. Rebbi Ze`ira, Rav Ḥananel in the name of Rav: but if it was like <i>I am the Eternal your God</i><sup class="footnote-marker">325</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 15:41" href="/Numbers.15.41"><i>Num</i>. 15:41</a>.</i> it is permitted. Why? Because these are three words or because part of it is profane? What is the difference between them? <i>God</i>, <i>Highest Power</i>, <i>Eternal</i><sup class="footnote-marker">326</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Joshua 22:22" href="/Joshua.22.22"><i>Jos</i>. 22:22</a>. The situation does not occur in the Pentateuch.</i>. If you are saying because they are three words, they are three words. If you are saying, because part of it is profane, nothing there is profane. <i>For the Eternal</i> is questionable, <i>by the Eternal</i> is questionable<sup class="footnote-marker">327</sup><i class="footnote">Since it will be stated later that prefixes of the Name may be erased while suffixes may not, the question is whether an erasable prefix has the same status as a profane word preceding the Name.</i>.
Rav Shesha the son of Rav Ḥananel said, one has to write ה below the knee joint of the ל, <i>so you want to repay to the Eternal</i>? In this way you want to repay to the Eternal<sup class="footnote-marker">328</sup><i class="footnote">In <a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 32:6" href="/Deuteronomy.32.6"><i>Deut</i>. 32:6</a> one has to write ה לי֙י, an enlarged <i>he</i> of which, however, the horizontal bar has to be aligned with the horizontal bar of the following <i>lamed</i>. The larger size will appear at the bottom where the vertical bars of this <i>he</i> are longer than those of the following Name.</i>? The Mezuzah. There are Tannaim who state, open; there are Tannaim who state, closed<sup class="footnote-marker">329</sup><i class="footnote">Paragraphs in the Hebrew Bible are either ending in a blank space of a third of a line, with the next paragraph starting on the same line on which the previous paragraph ended, this is called a closed paragraph. The other possibility is that the remainder of the last line remains empty; this is called open paragraph. Both paragraphs written in a Mezuzah, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 6:4-9" href="/Deuteronomy.6.4-9"><i>Deut</i>. 6:4–9</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 11:13-21" href="/Deuteronomy.11.13-21">11:13–21</a>, are closed in a Torah scroll; the first one follows an open paragraph, the second a closed one.</i>. Samuel bar Shilat in the name of Rav: Practice follows him who said open, since that is not its place<sup class="footnote-marker">330</sup><i class="footnote">The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Menachot 32a" href="/Menachot.32a">Babli disagrees (<i>Menaḥot</i> 32a</a>).</i>. Open at the start is closed. Open at the bottom is open. Open on both sides is closed<sup class="footnote-marker">331</sup><i class="footnote">“Open at the start” means that the writing of the first paragraph starts only in the middle of the line. This is normal for paragraphs following a closed paragraph. Open at the bottom means that the remainder of the last line of the paragraph remains empty; this is the definition of an open paragraph. Open on both sides implies open at the start.</i>. If in error he omitted one verse, if it is two or three lines one repairs and reads from it; four one does not read from it<sup class="footnote-marker">332</sup><i class="footnote">The scroll may not be used for public readings.</i>. Rebbi Ze`ira in the name of Rav Ḥananel, the same holds for a tear<sup class="footnote-marker">333</sup><i class="footnote">A tear in the parchment damaging up to three lines may be fixed by sewing the tear. If it is greater the page has to be removed and buried.</i>. It was stated, a scroll in which there are [two or three errors on every page one fixes and reads from it. Four one does not read from it. But was it not stated, a scroll in which there are]<sup class="footnote-marker">334</sup><i class="footnote">It seems that the corrector’s addition is necessary from the statements following.</i> 85 errors, as the paragraph <i>it was when the Ark travelled</i><sup class="footnote-marker">335</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 10:35-36" href="/Numbers.10.35-36"><i>Num</i>. 10:35–36</a> is a separate text and contains exactly 85 letters.</i>, one repairs and reads from it? Rebbi Shammai said, here in a large scroll, there in a small scroll<sup class="footnote-marker">336</sup><i class="footnote">A small scroll is one of 28 colums, which may exhibit 3 errors on each column and not reach 85. A large scroll has more columns; one has to check each column for the number of errors.</i>. Rebbi Ze`ira in the name of Rav Ḥananel, if he found in it one page which is whole, it saves everything. How? Whole in that there are (no) [not three]<sup class="footnote-marker">337</sup><i class="footnote">The later text shows clearly that the corrector’s text has to be deleted.</i> errors? Or whole that there are no four? Ḥagra the brother of Rebbi Abba bar Bina gave a Torah scroll to Rav Ḥananel who checked it. He said to him, this your Torah scroll needs prayer<sup class="footnote-marker">338</sup><i class="footnote">There are so many errors that the scribe clearly sinned when writing the scroll.</i>. At the end he found in it a page which was whole, which saved everything. How? Whole in that there are no errors? Or whole that there are no four<sup class="footnote-marker">339</sup><i class="footnote">Since no answer is given, in praxi one requires a column totally without error. Babli <i>Mehaḥot</i> 29b.</i>?
Rebbi Ze`ira, Rav Ḥananel in the name of Rav: Its sewing<sup class="footnote-marker">340</sup><i class="footnote">That the leaves of a Torah scroll and the Tefillin cases must be sewn together with sinews. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Makkot 11a" href="/Makkot.11a">Babli <i>Makkot</i> 11a</a>.</i> is practice of Moses from Sinai. Rebbi Jeremiah in the name of Rebbi Samuel bar Rav Isaac: the knots of Tefillin<sup class="footnote-marker">341</sup><i class="footnote">The straps of the Tefillin on the arm must be tied together with a small knot imitating the shape of י; the straps of the head Tefillin must be tied behind the head in a ד. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Menachot 35b" href="/Menachot.35b">Babli <i>Menaḥot</i> 35b</a>.</i> are practice of Moses from Sinai. A Tefillin strip of Rebbi Jeremiah broke; he asked Rebbi Abba bar Mamal who said to him, <i>and you stall tie them</i><sup class="footnote-marker">342</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 6:8" href="/Deuteronomy.6.8"><i>Deut</i>. 6:8</a>.</i>, even simple tying<sup class="footnote-marker">343</sup><i class="footnote">The suffix תם is read as a separate word, “unblemished, straight-forward.” The knots <i>must</i> be at the prescribed places; the shapes are desideratum (since the shapes would not fit if Moses wrote paleo-Hebrew.)</i>. Rebbi Ze`ira’s wall of the strip broke<sup class="footnote-marker">344</sup><i class="footnote">The Tefillin are small cubes sitting on a square leather base. To this base is added a rectangular tube through which the strap is drawn (in Babylonian sources called תִּיתּוּרָא “augmentation”.) The wall became defective. Since it is augmentation, not the essence of Tefillin, it may be patched up.</i>. He asked Rav Huna and Rav Qatina, and they permitted him. It broke a second time and they permitted him, not from what they had learned<sup class="footnote-marker">345</sup><i class="footnote">To permit patch of a patch was their decision, not traditional practice.</i>. Rebbi Abba the son of Rebbi Ḥiyya bar Abba, Rebbi Ḥiyya in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: The practice is that he who sews must leave space on top and bottom<sup class="footnote-marker">346</sup><i class="footnote">If one sews leather sheets together, the top and bottom stitches should not be too close to the rim. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Megillah 19b" href="/Megillah.19b">Babli 19b</a>.</i> so it should not tear. They hit him on the skull, if it is practice why “should not tear”? And if “should not tear” why practice<sup class="footnote-marker">347</sup><i class="footnote">Since “practice” here means “practice from Moses on Sinai”, it needs no justification.</i>? It is practice of Moses from Sinai that one write on leather, and write with ink, and rule with a ruler<sup class="footnote-marker">348</sup><i class="footnote">The lines on which the horizontal bars of the letters are written must be impressed with a wooden or metal tool, not drawn by pencil.</i>, and ties with hair<sup class="footnote-marker">349</sup><i class="footnote">The sheets used for Tefillin are made into scrolls which then are tied with animal hair before being out into Tefillin boxes. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Shabbat 108a" href="/Shabbat.108a">Babli <i>Sabbat</i> 108a</a>.</i>, and patches with a patch<sup class="footnote-marker">350</sup><i class="footnote">If the leather on which the Torah scroll is written has a tear, it can be patched up at the back with a leather patch.</i>, and glues with glue<sup class="footnote-marker">351</sup><i class="footnote">If the text of the arm-Tefillin were written on separate sheets, they may be glued together to form one page.</i>, and sews<sup class="footnote-marker">352</sup><i class="footnote">The Torah scroll and the cases for Tefillin.</i> with sinews. As he sews, he has to sew this stitch<sup class="footnote-marker">353</sup><i class="footnote">The stitches must be evenly spaced.</i>. One needs to leave between line and line the width of a line, between word and word the width of a letter, between letter and letter something, between column and column a thumb-width<sup class="footnote-marker">354</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Menachot 30a" href="/Menachot.30a">Babli <i>Menaḥot</i> 30a</a>; <i>Soferim</i> 2:2.</i>. If he made the end of the column equal to its beginning he disqualified it<sup class="footnote-marker">355</sup><i class="footnote">One may not start writing on a page below the top, that it may not look deficient.</i>. One must leave on the top of the scroll {a margin of} two finger-breadths and at the bottom three. Rebbi says, in the Torah at the top three and at the bottom a hand-breadth. Between book and book one has to leave four lines empty<sup class="footnote-marker">356</sup><i class="footnote">Between the books of the Pentateuch. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Batra 13b" href="/Bava_Batra.13b">Babli <i>Bava batra</i> 13b</a>; <i>Soferim</i> 2:3.</i>, in the Twelve Prophets three. One has to end in the middle of a column and start in its middle<sup class="footnote-marker">357</sup><i class="footnote">In writing a Torah scroll containing all five books.</i>; but for a Prophet he ends at the bottom and starts at the top<sup class="footnote-marker">358</sup><i class="footnote">Since the books of Prophets are independent of one another.</i>; but for the Twelve Prophets it is forbidden<sup class="footnote-marker">359</sup><i class="footnote">A sheet of a single column is possible at the end of Deuteronomy.</i>. One makes no sheet less than three columns and none more than eight<sup class="footnote-marker">360</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Menachot 30a" href="/Menachot.30a"><i>Menaḥot</i> 30a</a>; <i>Soferim</i> 2:6.</i>. That you are saying, at the start. But at the end even a little<sup class="footnote-marker">361</sup><i class="footnote">Since the Twelve Prophets form a single book.</i>; and for parchment<sup class="footnote-marker">362</sup><i class="footnote">Or rather split leather. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Batra 14a" href="/Bava_Batra.14a">Babli <i>Bava batra</i> 14a</a>.</i> the Sages did not give any size.
It is required that he write on leather on the place of the hair and on parchment on the place of its split<sup class="footnote-marker">363</sup><i class="footnote">Where the script is more readable. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Menachot 32a" href="/Menachot.32a">Babli <i>Menaḥot</i> 32a</a>.</i>. If he changed this, he disqualified it. One should not write half of it on leather and half of it on parchment, but he may write on leather from a pure domestic animal and half on leather from a pure wild animal. One only writes on leather from a pure animal<sup class="footnote-marker">364</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Shabbat 108a" href="/Shabbat.108a">Babli <i>Šabbat</i> 108a</a>.</i>. What is the reason? <i>That the Eternal’s teaching be in your mouth</i><sup class="footnote-marker">365</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 13:9" href="/Exodus.13.9"><i>Ex</i>. 13:9</a>.</i>, from what you are putting into your mouth. But was it not stated, one writes on leather from carcasses and torn animals<sup class="footnote-marker">364</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Shabbat 108a" href="/Shabbat.108a">Babli <i>Šabbat</i> 108a</a>.</i>? The kind which you are putting into your mouth<sup class="footnote-marker">366</sup><i class="footnote">Since hide and leather are inedible anyhow.</i>. One makes a staff at the end of a book, but for the Torah one on both sides. Therefore one rolls a book up to its beginning but the Torah to its middle<sup class="footnote-marker">367</sup><i class="footnote">The Torah scroll can be stored to open at the place where one stopped reading.</i>. Rebbi Samuel, Rebbi Ze`ira in the name of Rav Ḥiyya bar Joseph: Even two sheets<sup class="footnote-marker">368</sup><i class="footnote">The scroll shows two sheets when it is opened.</i>. Rebbi Ze`ira, Samuel bar Shilat in the name of Kahana: but only at the place of the suture<sup class="footnote-marker">369</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Megillah 32a" href="/Megillah.32a">Babli 32a</a>.</i>. Rebbi Aḥa in the name of Rebbi Samuel bar Naḥman: A scroll which has no cover one turns on the writing so the writing should not be degraded<sup class="footnote-marker">370</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Eruvin 10:3:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Eruvin.10.3.3"><i>Eruvin</i> 10 (Note 79</a>); <i>Soferim</i> 3:16.</i>.
Of all double letters<sup class="footnote-marker">371</sup><i class="footnote">The letters which in square script have two forms, as indicated in the next sentences.</i> in the alphabet one writes the first form at the start of a word and in the middle of a word, but the last form at the end, and if one changed this he disqualified it. In the name of Rebbi Matthew ben Ḥarash they said, מנצפ״ך is a practice of Moses from Sinai. What is מנצפ״ך? Rebbi Jeremiah in the name of Rebbi Samuel bar Rav Isaac: what the seers instituted for you<sup class="footnote-marker">372</sup><i class="footnote">While R. Matthew ben Ḥarash holds that the Torah was given in square script, R. Jeremiah dates square script to the time of the (later) prophets; he holds that the Torah was given in Paleo-Hebrew. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Megillah 2b" href="/Megillah.2b">Babli 2b</a>.</i>. Who are those seers? <sup class="footnote-marker">373</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Shabbat 104a" href="/Shabbat.104a">Babli <i>Šabbat</i> 104a</a>.</i> It happened on a day of rainstorm that the Sages did not come to the assembly hall, but the children entered. They said, come and let us form an assembly that it should not be cancelled. They said, what is that that it is written <i>mem mem</i>, <i>nun nun</i>, <i>ṣade ṣade</i>, <i>peh feh</i>, <i>kaf khaf</i>. From utterance to utterance, from trustworthy to trustworthy, from just to just, from mouth to mouth, from the hand of the Holy One, praise to Him, to the hand of Moses<sup class="footnote-marker">374</sup><i class="footnote">The entire homily refers to the giving of the Torah, from utterance of God to utterance of Moses, etc.</i>. The Sages identified them and they all grew to become important personalities. They said, Rebbi Eliezer and Rebbi Joshua were from these. Rebbi Jeremiah in the name of Rebbi Ḥiyya bar Abba and Rebbi Simon both were saying, in earlier copies of the Torah neither <i>he</i> nor final <i>mem</i> were closed<sup class="footnote-marker">296</sup><i class="footnote">The open final <i>mem</i> is exemplified in the Aramaic inscription of King Uziahu’s ossuary. In early Medieval mss. the <i>he</i> looks like a ח, only that the left leg is not at the left end but touching the vertical bar somewhat to the right.
