The open <i>he</i> is recommended in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Menachot 29b" href="/Menachot.29b">Babli, <i>Menaḥot</i> 29b</a>. For a thorough discussion, cf. S. Liebermann, <i>Tarbiz</i> 4 (1933) pp. 292–293.</i>. Therefore <i>samekh</i> was closed<sup class="footnote-marker">297</sup><i class="footnote">This justifies R. Levi’s remark that only <i>samekh</i> but not final <i>mem</i> represented a problem.</i>.
Rebbi Simon and Rebbi Samuel bar Naḥman both were saying, the people of Jerusalem were writing Jerusalem, to Jerusalem, and were not differentiating. And similarly North, to North; South, to South<sup class="footnote-marker">375</sup><i class="footnote">In the Hebrew of late Second Temple times, as exemplified in the Qumran texts, locative is freely used as nominative (already noted by Geiger, Urschrift p. 233; cf. S. Liebermann <i>Tarbiz</i> 4(1933) p.293, E. Kutscher, Isaiah Scroll, p. 67).</i>.
Thirteen things they changed for King Ptolemy. They wrote for him, “God created the beginning<sup class="footnote-marker">376</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Genesis 1:1" href="/Genesis.1.1"><i>Gen</i>. 1:1</a>; change not found in LXX.</i>.” “I shall make man in stature and form<sup class="footnote-marker">377</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Genesis 1:26" href="/Genesis.1.26"><i>Gen</i>. 1:26</a>; change not found in LXX.</i>.” “Male and his openings He created them<sup class="footnote-marker">378</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Genesis 1:27" href="/Genesis.1.27"><i>Gen</i>. 1:27</a>; change not found in LXX.</i>.” “He finished on the Sixth and rested on the Seventh<sup class="footnote-marker">379</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Genesis 2:2" href="/Genesis.2.2"><i>Gen</i>. 2:2</a>; change found in LXX.</i>” “Now I shall descend<sup class="footnote-marker">380</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Genesis 11:7" href="/Genesis.11.7"><i>Gen</i>. 11:7</a>; change found in LXX.</i>.” “Sarah laughed in her surroundings, saying.<sup class="footnote-marker">381</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Genesis 18:12" href="/Genesis.18.12"><i>Gen</i>. 18:12</a>; LXX: “in herself”.</i>” “For in their rage they slew a bull and in their will uprooted a trough<sup class="footnote-marker">382</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Genesis 49:6" href="/Genesis.49.6"><i>Gen</i>. 49:6</a>; change not found in LXX.</i>.” “Moses took his wife and his sons and let them ride on people-carriers<sup class="footnote-marker">383</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 4:20" href="/Exodus.4.20"><i>Ex</i>. 4:20</a>. LXX: “beast of burden”.</i>.” “The dwelling of the Children of Israel, which they dwelled in Egypt and other lands, was 430 years.<sup class="footnote-marker">384</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 12:40" href="/Exodus.12.40"><i>Ex</i>. 12:40</a>. LXX: “In the land of Egypt and the land of Canaan.”</i>” <i>And the hare</i><sup class="footnote-marker">385</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 11:6" href="/Leviticus.11.6"><i>Lev</i>. 11:6</a> (in LXX 11:5). LXX: “rough-foot”, a designation of the hare.</i>, “and the young of foot.” King Ptolemy’s mother was called “hare<sup class="footnote-marker">386</sup><i class="footnote">The dynasty of the Ptolemies was called the Lagides, after an ancestor Lagos “hare”.</i>”. “Not one precious thing I took from them<sup class="footnote-marker">387</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 16:15" href="/Numbers.16.15"><i>Num</i>. 16:15</a>; change found in LXX.</i>” “Which the Eternal, your God, distributed them to give light to all peoples under all the heavens.<sup class="footnote-marker">388</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 4:19" href="/Deuteronomy.4.19"><i>Deut</i>. 4:19</a>; change not found in LXX.</i>” “Which I did not command peoples to worship them.<sup class="footnote-marker">389</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 17:3" href="/Deuteronomy.17.3"><i>Deut</i>. 17:3</a>; change not found in LXX.<br>The comparisons with the LXX text was done on the basis of Rahlfs’s edition; the history of the text between the time of the Jewish translation in Alexandria and its adaptation by Christian editors in the Roman Empire is unknown. The same list is in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Megillah 9a" href="/Megillah.9a">Babli 9a</a>.</i>”
All letters written with the Name as prefix are profane and may be erased, e. g., to the Eternal, by the Eternal, as the Eternal, that the Eternal, since we find that on the diadem it was separate from it, “holy to” at the bottom, “the Eternal” at the top<sup class="footnote-marker">390</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Yoma 4:1:6" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Yoma.4.1.6"><i>Yoma</i>4:1 Note 27</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Shabbat 63b" href="/Shabbat.63b">Babli <i>Šabbat</i> 63b</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sukkah 5a" href="/Sukkah.5a"><i>Sukkah</i> 5a</a>. For the following, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Shevuot 35b" href="/Shevuot.35b"><i>Ševuot</i> 35b</a>.</i>. All letters written with the Name as suffix are holy and may not be erased, e. g., our God, your God. These are the Names which may not be erased: one who writes the Name of four letters, whether YH or AD<sup class="footnote-marker">391</sup><i class="footnote">The Name אֲדֹנָי used as pronunciation of the Tetragrammaton. If the intention is to אֲדֹנַי “my masters”, the word is profane and may be erased.</i>. “El, Elohim, Elohei, Elohenu, Elohekhem, Shadday, Sabaoth, Ehye-Ašer-Ehye.” If one writes AD from Adonai, EH from Ehye, ŠD from Shadday, ṢB from Sabaoth, these may be erased and one erases their additions<sup class="footnote-marker">392</sup><i class="footnote">אֵד “vapor”, אַה has no sense, שַׁד “female breast” שֶׁד “spirit”, צָב “turtle” are profane words.</i>. “To Elohim” one erases “to”; “to the Eternal” one erases “to”<sup class="footnote-marker">393</sup><i class="footnote">As noted at the start of the paragraph.</i>. If he wrote YH<sup class="footnote-marker">394</sup><i class="footnote">Which is a divine Name in its own right and should have been mentioned in the list.</i> of the four-letter Name, EL from Elohim, it may not be erased. This is the rule: Any which at another place is a permanent Name may not be erased.
It was stated: Rebbi Yose says, the family Ḥagira were professional scribes in Jerusalem, who were erasing Sabaoth since it is a profane substantive at another place, <i>the military commanders take position at the head of the people</i><sup class="footnote-marker">395</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 20:9" href="/Deuteronomy.20.9"><i>Deut</i>. 20:9</a>.</i>. All Names written regarding our father Abraham are holy except one which is profane, <i>it was when the gods made me err from my father’s house</i><sup class="footnote-marker">396</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Genesis 20:13" href="/Genesis.20.13"><i>Gen</i>. 20:13</a>. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Shevuot 35b" href="/Shevuot.35b">Babli <i>Ševuot</i> 35b</a>.</i>. But some are saying, this one also is holy, “for unless God, they already would have made me err.” All Names written regarding Micha<sup class="footnote-marker">397</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Judges 17-18" href="/Judges.17-18"><i>Jud</i>. 17–18</a>.</i> are profane, even though they are written with YH, except one which is holy, <i>all time that the House of God was at Siloh</i><sup class="footnote-marker">398</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Judges 18:31" href="/Judges.18.31"><i>Jud</i>. 18:31</a>.</i>. All Names written regarding Naboth, even though they are written with EL are holy, <i>Naboth cursed God and King</i><sup class="footnote-marker">399</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="I Kings 21:13" href="/I_Kings.21.13"><i>1K</i>. 21:13</a>. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Shevuot 35b" href="/Shevuot.35b"><i>Ševuot</i> 35b</a>.</i>. But “merciful and compassionate, patient and full of grace, King of kings, elevated, and great, and awesone, and supreme, powerful, just, straight, saintly, flawless, strong,” these are profane<sup class="footnote-marker">400</sup><i class="footnote">These are attributes, not Names.</i>. The following are the names which cannot be split: Amiel, Amishadday, Ṣuriel, Ṣurishaddai, Gamliel, Pedahzur, Pedahel, may not be split<sup class="footnote-marker">401</sup><i class="footnote">While theophoric names are profane, isolating the divine name by splitting the word would create a word which may not be erased since the syllable was intended as a Name.</i> These are names that may be split, Beth-El<sup class="footnote-marker">402</sup><i class="footnote">A geographic name, written without any intent of endowing it with holiness.</i>, Beth-Awen, getting angry, I shall get angry, Poti-phera, Ṣafenath-Paneaḥ.
<sup class="footnote-marker">403</sup><i class="footnote">The text from here to the end of the Halakhah is copied in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sukkah 3:10:2-4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sukkah.3.10.2-4"><i>Sukkah</i>3:12 (Notes 117–136</a>).</i> Rav and Samuel. One said <i>hallelu yah</i>, and the other one said <i>hallēluja</i>. For him who said <i>hallelu yah</i> it is split but cannot be erased. For him who said <i>hallelujah</i> it may be erased but cannot be split. We do not know who said what. Since Rav said, I heard from my uncle, if somebody would give me a book of Psalms written by Rebbi Meïr I could erase all <i>hallelujah</i> in it since he did not intend to sanctify them. This implies that he said <i>hallelujah</i>. The words of the rabbis disagree, as Rebbi Simon said in the name of Rebbi Joshua ben Levi, the book of Psalms was said with ten expressions of praise, with “hail”, with “excellence”, with melody, with song, with chant, with instruction, with shout, [with thanksgiving,] with prayer, with blessing. The most beatific of all of them is <i>hallelujah</i> since both the Name and praise are contained in it.
Rebbi Ze`ira asked before Rebbi Abbahu: He told him, (on top) [in the mouth] before you. Rebbi Jonah answered both ways. Rebbi Eleazar answered both ways. As it was stated, if he heard but did not answer, he fulfilled his obligation; if he answered but did not hear he did not fulfill his obligation<sup class="footnote-marker">404</sup><i class="footnote">The recitation of the holiday <i>Hallel</i>(<a class="refLink" data-ref="Psalms 13-18" href="/Psalms.13-18"><i>Ps</i>. 13–18</a>).</i>. Rav in the name of Rebbi Abba bar Ḥana; but some say Rebbi Abba bar Ḥana in the name of Rav: Only if he answered at the start of the Chapters. Rebbi Ze`ira asked, what are the starts of Chapters? <i>Hallelujah, praise, servants of the Eternal, praise the Name of the Eternal</i><sup class="footnote-marker">405</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Psalms 113:1" href="/Psalms.113.1"><i>Ps</i>. 113:1</a>.</i>. They asked before Rebbi Ḥiyya bar Abba: From where that he who heard but did not answer has fulfilled his obligation? He told them, since we are seeing great rabbis standing among the public when these say <i>praised be he who comes</i> and the others say <i>in the name of the Eternal</i><sup class="footnote-marker">406</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Psalms 118:26" href="/Psalms.118.26"><i>Ps</i>. 118:26</a>.</i>, and both have fulfilled their obligation.
Rebbi Hoshaya stated: One answers Amen even if he did not eat, but he may not say: “Let us praise Him, of Whose bounty we ate,” except if he did eat. It is stated: “One answers neither an orphan Amen, nor a plucked Amen. Ben Azai said, if one answered an orphan Amen, may his children be orphans; hurried, may the end of his days be hurried; plucked, may his soul be plucked off. For a long Amen, they lengthen his days and years in a good way. What is an orphan Amen? Rebbi Huna said, that is one who is obligated to recite a blessing, he answers Amen, and is not conscious for what he answered Amen. It is stated: One answers Amen after a Gentile who recited a benediction for the Eternal. By the Name, one does not answer Amen after him. Rebbi Tanḥuma said, if a Gentile blesses you, answer after him Amen since it is written<sup class="footnote-marker">407</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 7:14" href="/Deuteronomy.7.14"><i>Deut</i>. 7:14</a>.</i> <i>You shall be blessed by all peoples</i>. A Gentile met Rebbi Ismael and blessed him. He answered: The word about you has already been said. He met a second one who cursed him; he answered: The word about you has already been said. His students said to him: Rebbi, did you say to the one what you said to the other? He said to them, so it is written<sup class="footnote-marker">408</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Genesis 27:29" href="/Genesis.27.29"><i>Gen</i>. 27:29</a>.</i> <i>Those who curse you are cursed, but those who bless you are blessed</i>.

Halakhah 10

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> The only difference between a High Priest anointed with the anointing oil and one invested<sup class="footnote-marker">409</sup><i class="footnote">The making of the anointing oil is described in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 30:22-33" href="/Exodus.30.22-33"><i>Ex</i>. 30:22–33</a> as a personal obligation of Moses. It cannot be duplicated by any other person. Therefore only the High Priests of the Tabernacle, of Siloh, and the First Temple could be anointed for their duties. The High Priests of the Second Temple were invested with the vestments of their office but could not be anointed. The special purification offering of the High Priest of a bull described in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 4:3-12" href="/Leviticus.4.3-12"><i>Lev</i>. 4:3–12</a> is restricted to the “anointed priest”. The purification offering of a High Priest of the Second Temple period was that of common people, a female goat or sheep; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 4:27-35" href="/Leviticus.4.27-35"><i>Lev</i>. 4:27–35</a>.</i> is the bull which comes for all commandments. The only difference between an acting High Priest and a deactivated High Priest<sup class="footnote-marker">410</sup><i class="footnote">Removed by a king of the Herodian dynasty or a Roman procurator.</i> is the bull of the Day of Atonement and the Tenth of an Epha<sup class="footnote-marker">416</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 6:15" href="/Leviticus.6.15"><i>Lev</i>. 6:15</a>, on the daily flour sacrifice of the High Priest.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> <sup class="footnote-marker">411</sup><i class="footnote">The entire Halakhah is also in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Horayot 3:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Horayot.3.3"><i>Horaiot</i>3:3 (Notes 132–188</a>). The copy of the first paragraph in <i>Horaiot</i> is incomplete; the later paragraphs are also in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Yoma 1:1:21-26" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Yoma.1.1.21-26"><i>Yoma</i>1:1 (Notes 123–149</a>).</i> It was stated: The Anointed Priest brings a bull, the one clothed in multiple garb does not bring a bull. This disagrees with Rebbi Meïr, for Rebbi Meïr said, the one clothed in multiple garb brings a bull<sup class="footnote-marker">412</sup><i class="footnote">The definite article used in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 4:3" href="/Leviticus.4.3"><i>Lev</i>. 4:3</a>, <i>the</i> priest, would alone have sufficed to characterize the High Priest, biblically distinguished from all others.</i>. What is Rebbi Meïr’s reason? <i>The Anointed</i>. Why does the verse say <i>priest</i>? To add the one clothed in multiple garb<sup class="footnote-marker">413</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Horayot 2:3" href="/Tosefta_Horayot.2.3">Tosephta <i>Horaiot</i> 2:3</a>.</i>. What is the rabbis’ reason? <i>The anointed</i>. I could think that this is the king. The verse says, <i>priest</i>. If priest, I could think the one clothed in multiple garb. The verse says, <i>anointed</i><sup class="footnote-marker">414</sup><i class="footnote">The double restriction, <i>the</i> priest (the High Priest), <i>anointed,</i> makes it clear that only an anointed high priest is meant. The rabbinic disagreement implies that no High Priest of Second Temple times ever brought a purification sacrifice for himself.</i>. Then I could think that I am adding also the one anointed for war<sup class="footnote-marker">415</sup><i class="footnote">The one mentioned in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 20:3" href="/Deuteronomy.20.3"><i>Deut</i>. 20:3</a> charged with addressing the army. He also is called <i>the</i> priest (<i>Soṭah</i> Chapter 8) and bound by all restrictions imposed on the High Priest in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 21:10-15" href="/Leviticus.21.10-15"><i>Lev</i>. 21:10–15</a> (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Megillah (Lieberman) 2:1" href="/Tosefta_Megillah_(Lieberman).2.1">Tosephta 2:1</a>).</i>. The verse says, <i>Anointed</i>; one who has no anointed person over him. The argument of the rabbis seems inverted. Here<sup class="footnote-marker">416</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 6:15" href="/Leviticus.6.15"><i>Lev</i>. 6:15</a>, on the daily flour sacrifice of the High Priest.</i> is written <i>anointed</i> and there is written <i>anointed</i>. Here they say, to include the one clothed in multiple garb<sup class="footnote-marker">417</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Horayot 3:2:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Horayot.3.2.1">Mishnah <i>Horaiot</i> 3:4</a> mentions the daily offering of a tenth of an <i>ephah</i> as duty of the High Priest clothed in multiple garb [<a class="refLink" data-ref="Sifra, Tzav, Chapter 5 1" href="/Sifra,_Tzav,_Chapter_5.1"><i>Sifra Ṣaw Pereq</i> 5(1</a>)].</i>. But here<sup class="footnote-marker">418</sup><i class="footnote">In the Chapter on purification sacrifices.</i> they say, to exclude the one clothed in multiple garb. Rebbi Hila said, each inference refers to its meaning. There the entire paragraph is said for Aaron. Why is said <i>priest</i>? To include the one clothed in multiple garb<sup class="footnote-marker">419</sup><i class="footnote">Aaron and his successors are mentioned in v. 13. In v. 15, the mention of “the priest, anointed from his descendants in his stead” does not seem to require a mention of anointing as a definition.</i>. But here the paragraph does not mention Aaron. If it had said <i>the Anointed</i> but not <i>priest</i>, I would have said, [he brings a bull for forgetting a topic, but for acting in error he brings a goat. Therefore it is necessary that it would mention <i>priest</i>. But if it had mentioned <i>priest</i> but not <i>the Anointed</i>, I would have said,]<sup class="footnote-marker">420</sup><i class="footnote">Unnecessary corrector’s addition.</i> this refers to the king<sup class="footnote-marker">421</sup><i class="footnote">Since <i>Cohen</i> may simply mean “public servant” (<a class="refLink" data-ref="II Samuel 8:18" href="/II_Samuel.8.18"><i>2S</i>. 8:18</a>).</i>. If you would say already this precedes the paragraph about the king<sup class="footnote-marker">422</sup><i class="footnote">Which is only the third in the Chapter.</i>, I would have said that for forgetting a topic he brings a bull but for acting in error he brings a goat<sup class="footnote-marker">423</sup><i class="footnote">As explained in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Horayot 2:3:2-3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Horayot.2.3.2-3"><i>Horaiot</i>2:3</a>, The High Priest may offer a bull only for his forgetting a topic in religious law. One could argue that for simple acting in error, he should bring a commoner’s sacrifice (or, since a male animal is mentioned, the goat characterized earlier as sacrifice for inadvertent idolatry). The specific mention of <i>priest</i> bars him from a commoner’s sacrifice.</i>. Therefore it is necessary that it mention <i>the Anointed</i> and that it mention <i>priest</i>.
Rebbi Joḥanan said, if he transgressed and offered his tenth of an <i>ephah</i> it is valid<sup class="footnote-marker">424</sup><i class="footnote">This refers to the statement in the Mishnah that the only difference between acting and emeritus High Priests are the High Priest’s bull on the Day of Atonement and the daily flour offering of a tenth of an <i>ephah</i>. It is now stated that if the ex-High Priest, who, as will be explained later in the Halakhah, should be unfit to serve as High Priest and is barred from serving as common priest, nevertheless acts as High Priest, the offering is legitimate.</i>.
<sup class="footnote-marker">425</sup><i class="footnote">Here starts the parallel in <i>Yoma</i> 1:1.</i>“One arranges for another Cohen as his replacement, maybe a disqualification of his will happen.<sup class="footnote-marker">426</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Yoma 1:1:1-27" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Yoma.1.1.1-27">Mishnah <i>Yoma</i>1:1</a>. Since the entire service of the Day of Atonement is valid only if conducted by the High Priest, a replacement must be available in case the High Priest becomes impure or otherwise incapacitated. The High Priest undergoes a week of preparation for the service, to train for a very crowded program. The question then arises whether the designated backup also has to undergo the same training, possibly at the same place.</i>” How? Does one leave them alone together? Rebbi Ḥaggai said, by Moses! If one would leave them alone together, he would kill him! <i>Him</i><sup class="footnote-marker">427</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 6:12" href="/Leviticus.6.12"><i>Lev</i>. 6:12</a>; the offering of the High Priest starting with the day he is anointed for his office. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Sifra, Tzav, Section 3 3" href="/Sifra,_Tzav,_Section_3.3"><i>Sifra Ṣaw Parašah</i> 3(3</a>). The singular indicates that only one High Priest can be appointed at one time. This implies that the reserve appointee for the day of Atonement cannot have the status of High Priest unless he actually is needed.</i>. One anoints one, one does not anoint two. Rebbi Joḥanan said, because of rivalry<sup class="footnote-marker">428</sup><i class="footnote">He disagrees and holds that while the two could not have been anointed on the same day, they could have been anointed on different days. The rule that the back-up Cohen has lower status is practical, not biblical, as is the entire institution of the back-up.</i>.
If one was incapacitated and the other officiated. The first has all the sanctity of the High Priesthood on him; the second one is qualified neither as High Priest nor as common priest<sup class="footnote-marker">429</sup><i class="footnote">As the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Yoma 12b" href="/Yoma.12b">Babli explains (<i>Yoma</i> 12b</a>), “one increases in sanctity but never decreases” (cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Bikkurim 3:3:15" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Bikkurim.3.3.15"><i>Bikkurim</i>3:3, Note 57</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Yoma 3:8:4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Yoma.3.8.4"><i>Yoma</i>3:8 Note 201</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Shevuot 1:5:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Shevuot.1.5.2"><i>Ševuot</i> 1:8 Note 169</a>). Since the service of the Day of Atonement is valid only if performed by the High Priest, the substitute becomes a temporary High Priest. He cannot act as a High Priest if the actual High Priest’s temporary disability is removed and he is permanently barred from acting as a common priest. As the Babli points out, if the High Priest dies, the substitute automatically becomes his successor.</i>. Rebbi Joḥanan said, if he transgressed and officiated, his officiating is valid<sup class="footnote-marker">430</sup><i class="footnote">A doubt about the legitimacy of officiating in the Temple does not invalidate the offering (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Terumot 8:1:4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Terumot.8.1.4"><i>Terumot</i> 8:1, Note 26</a>).</i>. Whose officiating? <sup class="footnote-marker">431</sup><i class="footnote">The case is told not only in the two parallels in <i>Horaiot</i> and <i>Yoma</i>, but also in abbreviated form in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Yoma 12b" href="/Yoma.12b">Babli, <i>Yoma</i> 12b</a> where, however, the ruling is not the king’s (necessarily of the Herodian dynasty) but “the rabbis’.” There is no reason to doubt the historicity of the Yerushalmi version.</i> Let us hear from the following: It happened to Ben Illem from Sepphoris that the High Priest experienced an emission of semen on the Day of Atonement<sup class="footnote-marker">432</sup><i class="footnote">The High Priest is taken to live in the Temple, and therefore deprived of sexual activity, for seven days preceding the Day of Atonement. In the night of the Day of Atonement he is deprived of sleep (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Yoma 1:7:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Yoma.1.7.1"><i>Yoma</i> Mishnah 1:7</a>) to avoid the danger of him having an involuntary emission. If he has one anyhow, he is disqualified for the entire day even if he immediately purifies himself in a <i>miqweh</i> since the disqualification by temporary impurity is not removed by the removal of the impurity until the following sundown (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 22:7" href="/Leviticus.22.7"><i>Lev</i>. 22:7</a>; cf. <i>Ma`aser Šeni</i> 3:2, Notes 21–22.)</i>; Ben Illem entered and officiated in his stead. He asked the king when he went out: “The bull and the goat which are brought today, from whose property are they offered? From mine or from the High Priest’s?” The king understood what he was asking and answered him, “Ben Illem, is it not enough for you that you served once before Him Who spoke and the world was created? It is enough for you.” Ben Illem understood that he was removed from the High Priesthood.
<sup class="footnote-marker">433</sup><i class="footnote">In addition to the two parallels there is a short version in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Yoma 47a" href="/Yoma.47a">Babli, <i>Yoma</i> 47a</a>. There, the names are Ismael and Joseph. The passages are discussed in detail by Grätz, <i>Geschichte der Juden</i> vol. 3/2<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup> <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Megillah 1:1:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Megillah.1.1.3">Note 19</a>/II. Josephus transcribes בֶּן קִמְחִית as τοῡ Καμύδου.</i> It happened that Simeon ben Qimḥit went out for a walk with an Arab king [on the evening before the Day of Atonement at sundown]<sup class="footnote-marker">434</sup><i class="footnote">The different versions of this text are discussed in <i>Horaiot</i>, Note 157. Probably it should read “with the king at sundown before the Day of Atonement.” If the Arab was not converted to Judaism, the High Priest would have had to leave the holy precinct on the Day of Atonement, a most unlikely happening before he had finished all his duties.<br>The version of the text presupposes that the king had immersed himself in a <i>miqweh</i> so he could enter the restricted area on the Temple Mount. Nevertheless, (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Chagigah 2:7:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Chagigah.2.7.1">Mishnah <i>Ḥagigah</i> 2:7</a>) “The garments of the vulgar are severely impure for Pharisees; the garments of Pharisees are severely impure for those eating heave; the garments of those eating heave are severely impure for those sacrificing.” The severe impurity of מִדְרָס referred to here is the impurity of seats or beds used by a sufferer from gonorrhea or a menstruating woman, which makes anyone touching it impure and requires immersion in water and waiting until sundown. Since the king could not sacrifice on the Day of Atonement, he could not have immersed himself with an intention which would make him co-pure with the High Priest. Since the incident happened at sundown, the High Priest was automatically disqualified for the next 24 hours.<br>While a living Gentile is not under the rules of biblical impurity, rabbinically every Gentile is impure and this impurity cannot be removed by immersion in water (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Avodah Zarah 36b" href="/Avodah_Zarah.36b">Babli <i>Avodah zarah</i> 36b</a>).</i> and a drop of spittle squirted on his garment and defiled him. His brother Jehudah entered and officiated in his stead. Their mother saw two of her sons as High Priests on the same day. Qimḥit had seven sons; all of them served as High Priests<sup class="footnote-marker">435</sup><i class="footnote">Under the Herodian kings, when the High Priesthood was conferred and removed at the whim of the king.</i>. The Sages sent and asked Qimḥit, what good deeds are in your hand? She told them, there should come over me if the beams of the roof of my house ever saw the hair on my head or the seam of my undershirt<sup class="footnote-marker">436</sup><i class="footnote">It is indecent for a married woman to be seen in public with uncovered hair. She was clothed at home as she was for the street and never undressed except in the dark. The Babli notes that many women follow this custom.</i>. They said, all flours are flour but Qimḥit’s flour is fine flour<sup class="footnote-marker">437</sup><i class="footnote">A pun on the name of <i>Qimḥit</i> “flour lady”.</i>. They recited about her<sup class="footnote-marker">438</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Psalms 45:14" href="/Psalms.45.14"><i>Ps</i>. 45:14</a>.</i>: <i>All the honor of the king’s daughter is inside; gold settings her garments</i><sup class="footnote-marker">439</sup><i class="footnote">According to Rashi’s commentary in <i>Yoma</i>, the argument means that the reward of a woman who behaves with dignity in her home is that her son will be High Priest whose garment is adorned with golden settings for precious stones. (Cf. <i>Tanḥuma Wayyišlaḥ</i> 6, <i>Bemidbar</i> 3.)</i>.
I could think that the one anointed for war<sup class="footnote-marker">415</sup><i class="footnote">The one mentioned in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 20:3" href="/Deuteronomy.20.3"><i>Deut</i>. 20:3</a> charged with addressing the army. He also is called <i>the</i> priest (<i>Soṭah</i> Chapter 8) and bound by all restrictions imposed on the High Priest in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 21:10-15" href="/Leviticus.21.10-15"><i>Lev</i>. 21:10–15</a> (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Megillah (Lieberman) 2:1" href="/Tosefta_Megillah_(Lieberman).2.1">Tosephta 2:1</a>).</i> should bring his tenth of an <i>ephah</i><sup class="footnote-marker">416</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 6:15" href="/Leviticus.6.15"><i>Lev</i>. 6:15</a>, on the daily flour sacrifice of the High Priest.</i>. [The verse says<sup class="footnote-marker">440</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 29:30" href="/Exodus.29.30"><i>Ex</i>. 29:30</a>. As often, the proof is from the part of the verse not quoted: <i>Seven days the priest shall wear them, who of his sons will stand in his stead to officiate in the Sanctuary</i>. The only hereditary office in Divine Service is that of the High Priest. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Yoma 72b-73a" href="/Yoma.72b-73a">Babli <i>Yoma</i>72b/73a</a>.</i>, <i>in his stead, of his sons</i>. One whose son will serve in his stead brings his tenth of an <i>ephah</i>.] This excludes the one anointed for war whose sons will not serve in his stead does not bring a tenth of an <i>ephah</i>. From where that the son of the anointed for war will not serve in his stead? The verse says<sup class="footnote-marker">440</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 29:30" href="/Exodus.29.30"><i>Ex</i>. 29:30</a>. As often, the proof is from the part of the verse not quoted: <i>Seven days the priest shall wear them, who of his sons will stand in his stead to officiate in the Sanctuary</i>. The only hereditary office in Divine Service is that of the High Priest. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Yoma 72b-73a" href="/Yoma.72b-73a">Babli <i>Yoma</i>72b/73a</a>.</i>, <i>seven days shall the priest wear them,</i> etc. If one officiates in the Tent of Meeting, his son will stand in his stead. This excludes the one anointed for war who does not officiate in the Tent of Meeting, [his son will not stand in his stead.] From where that he can be appointed as High Priest<sup class="footnote-marker">441</sup><i class="footnote">Since the Anointed for War is under the restrictions valid for the High Priest, one has to ascertain that his office be subordinate, not coordinate, to the High Priesthood and that an appointment to High Priesthood does not violate the rule that one may not reduce the holiness of one’s position (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Megillah 1:4:15" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Megillah.1.4.15">Note 151</a>).</i>? [As is written,] <sup class="footnote-marker">442</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="I Chronicles 9:20" href="/I_Chronicles.9.20"><i>1Chr</i>. 9:20</a>. The leader of the priests is the High Priest. Phineas was appointed Anointed for War by Moses, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 31:6" href="/Numbers.31.6"><i>Num</i>. 31:6</a>.</i><i>Phineas the son of Eleazar was leader over them; in earlier times the Eternal was with him</i>. When Rebbi Yose wanted to needle<sup class="footnote-marker">443</sup><i class="footnote">Hebrew verb built on a Greek root; cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Berakhot 3:1:22" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Berakhot.3.1.22"><i>Berakhot</i> 3, Note 96</a>.</i> Rebbi Eleazar ben Rebbi Yose<sup class="footnote-marker">444</sup><i class="footnote">R. Yose seems to have complained about a lack of leadership on the part of his son.</i>, he said to him, “before, he was with him.” <i>Before, he was with him</i>. In the days of Zimri<sup class="footnote-marker">445</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 27:1-15" href="/Numbers.27.1-15"><i>Num</i>. 27:1–15</a>.</i>, he protested. In the days of the concubine at Gibea<sup class="footnote-marker">446</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Judges 19-21" href="/Judges.19-21"><i>Jud</i>. 19–21</a>. In the opinion of <i>Seder Olam</i>, based on the teachings of R. Yose the Tanna (who is meant here), the affair at Gibea happened at the start of the period of the Judges, when Phineas was High Priest. Cf. the author’s edition of <i>Seder Olam</i> (Northvale NJ 1998), pp. 122–123.</i>, he did not protest.
And from where that he was asked in eight<sup class="footnote-marker">447</sup><i class="footnote">The Anointed for War has two jobs. One is to address the army as described in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 20:1-9" href="/Deuteronomy.20.1-9"><i>Deut</i>. 20:1–9</a>, the other to ask the Urim and Tummim oracle on behalf of the army commander. Since this oracle is mentioned only in connection with the High Priest’s garments (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 28:30" href="/Exodus.28.30"><i>Ex</i>. 28:30</a>) it is obvious that the Anointed for War must wear one of these garments for the oracle. But since all eight garments of the High Priest form an indivisible unit, he must wear all of them.</i>? Rebbi Abba, Rebbi Ḥiyya, Rebbi Ḥiyya in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: <i>And Aaron’s holy garments shall be for his descendants in his stead</i><sup class="footnote-marker">440</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 29:30" href="/Exodus.29.30"><i>Ex</i>. 29:30</a>. As often, the proof is from the part of the verse not quoted: <i>Seven days the priest shall wear them, who of his sons will stand in his stead to officiate in the Sanctuary</i>. The only hereditary office in Divine Service is that of the High Priest. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Yoma 72b-73a" href="/Yoma.72b-73a">Babli <i>Yoma</i>72b/73a</a>.</i>. Why does the verse say, <i>in his stead</i>? For greatness after him<sup class="footnote-marker">448</sup><i class="footnote">Also for a secondary greatness.</i>. And from where that he officiated in eight<sup class="footnote-marker">449</sup><i class="footnote">This seems logical. Since the Anointed for War is required to wear the High Priest’s garb, “one increases in sanctity but does not decrease” (cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Megillah 1:10:5" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Megillah.1.10.5">Note 429</a>). The opposing opinion disqualifies the Anointed for War from all office in the Sanctuary.</i>? Rebbi Immi in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: <i>And Aaron’s holy garments shall be for his descendants</i>. Why does the verse say, <i>in his stead</i>? For greatness after him. Rebbi Jonah said to him<sup class="footnote-marker">450</sup><i class="footnote">The name of R. Jonah’s interlocutor is not given; it is presumed to be his colleague R. Yose. He notes that R. Immi’s statement was identical to that of of R. Ḥiyya. In the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Yoma 73a" href="/Yoma.73a">Babli, <i>Yoma</i> 73a</a>, the students of R. Joḥanan already point out that R. Joḥanan only gave his opinion on interrogation of the oracle, not of officiating.</i>, I was with you; he did not say “officiated” but “was asked”. Rav Hoshaia brought a Mishnah of Bar Qappara from the South which stated: But the Sages are saying that he is qualified neither in the four of a common priest nor in the eight of a High Priest<sup class="footnote-marker">449</sup><i class="footnote">This seems logical. Since the Anointed for War is required to wear the High Priest’s garb, “one increases in sanctity but does not decrease” (cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Megillah 1:10:5" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Megillah.1.10.5">Note 429</a>). The opposing opinion disqualifies the Anointed for War from all office in the Sanctuary.</i>. Rebbi Abba said, it would be logical that he officiate in four. Why did they say that he does not officiate? Lest people say, we saw a High Priest, sometimes he officiates in four, sometimes he officiates in eight<sup class="footnote-marker">452</sup><i class="footnote">He holds that as a matter of principle, the Anointed for War could use the eight garments of the High Priest strictly for his duties outside the sanctuary and still be a common priest inside without violating the principle of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Megillah 1:10:5" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Megillah.1.10.5">Note 429</a>. The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Yoma 73a" href="/Yoma.73a">Babli disagrees (<i>Yoma</i>73a</a>) and bases the rule strictly on that principle. In contrast to the Babli, this would be strictly a rabbinic rule, not based on biblical principles, and therefore not a historical reconstruction by a new rule for the days of the Messiah.</i>. Rebbi Jonah said, would he not officiate inside and would he not be asked outside? Does one err between inside and outside? But did Rebbi Tarphon, the father of all of Israel, not err between blowing for assembly and the blowing for a sacrifice? As it is stated: <i>The descendants of Aaron, the priests, shall blow the trumpets</i><sup class="footnote-marker">453</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 10:8" href="/Numbers.10.8"><i>Num</i>. 10:8</a>.</i>, blameless ones, not with bodily defects, the words of Rebbi Aqiba. Rebbi Tarphon said to him, I would hit my sons<sup class="footnote-marker">454</sup><i class="footnote">His oath formula, cursing himself if his statement should be found false. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Shabbat 17a" href="/Shabbat.17a">Babli <i>Šabbat</i>17a</a>.</i> if I did not see Simeon, my mother’s brother, lame in one of his legs, standing in the Temple court with his trumpet in his hand and blowing! Rebbi Aqiba answered him, maybe you saw him only at the time of assembly<sup class="footnote-marker">455</sup><i class="footnote">The command to call all the community in the desert by the sound of trumpets (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 10:3" href="/Numbers.10.3"><i>Num</i>. 10:3</a>) is extended to use trumpets to introduce the public Torah reading in the Temple at Tabernacles in the Sabbatical Year (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 31:10-13" href="/Deuteronomy.31.10-13"><i>Deut</i>. 31:10–13</a>).</i>; but I was saying, at the time of sacrifices<sup class="footnote-marker">456</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 10:10" href="/Numbers.10.10"><i>Num</i>. 10:10</a>; cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sanhedrin 3:9:4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sanhedrin.3.9.4"><i>Sanhedrin</i>3:3 Note 155</a>.</i>. Rebbi Tarphon said to him, I would hit my sons but you did not deviate right or left. I am the one who saw the act and forgot. You derive it and agree with tradition. Therefore, anybody who separates from you is as if he separated himself from his life<sup class="footnote-marker">457</sup><i class="footnote">A similar text in <i>Sifry Num.</i>75 (a better text <i>Yalqut</i> #725).</i>.
<i>The priest shall atone who was anointed</i><sup class="footnote-marker">458</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 16:32" href="/Leviticus.16.32"><i>Lev</i>. 16:32</a>. The problem is the legitimacy of a priest appointed <i>ad hoc</i> as High Priest to conduct the service of the Day of Atonement for which common priests are disqualified.</i>. Since the entire chapter is said about Aaron, from where to include another priest<sup class="footnote-marker">459</sup><i class="footnote">The first 30 verses of the Chapter mention Aaron exclusively.</i>? The verse says, <i>who was anointed</i>; not only the anointed with the anointing oil; from where the one clothed in multiple garb? The verse says, <i>who was inducted into office</i>. And from where another who was appointed<sup class="footnote-marker">460</sup><i class="footnote">In an emergency of the Day of Atonement where no formal session of a court can be held. Even when anointing oil was available, simple investiture was enough.</i>? The verse says, <i>the priest shall atone</i><sup class="footnote-marker">461</sup><i class="footnote">Since it does not stress “the High Priest”, it follows that any priest can be appointed to fill the office.</i>. How is he being appointed? The rabbis of Caesarea in the name of Rebbi Ḥiyya bar Joseph, by mouth<sup class="footnote-marker">462</sup><i class="footnote">It does not need the laying on of hands nor a document of appointment. (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosafot on Yoma 12b" href="/Tosafot_on_Yoma.12b">Tosaphot <i>Yoma</i> 12b <i>s, v.</i> כהן</a>).</i>. Rebbi Ze`ira said, this implies that one may ordain Elders by word of mouth. Rebbi Ḥiyya bar Ada said, a Mishnah says so: “Recant the four things that you are used to say and we shall make you president of the Court.<sup class="footnote-marker">463</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Eduyot 5:6" href="/Mishnah_Eduyot.5.6">Mishnah <i>Idiut</i> 5:6</a>. The oral promise was irrevocable.</i>”

Halakhah 11

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> The only difference between a public altar and a private altar was the Pesah sacrifice<sup class="footnote-marker">464</sup><i class="footnote">The public altar is the one constructed for the Tabernacle at Sinai, which was the common altar for all tribes. The rules given in <i>Deut</i>. are interpreted to mean that if the altar and the Tabernacle were at one place, Shilo in the time of the Judges, and Jerusalem, private altars were forbidden, but when altar and Tabernacle were at different places, in the time between the destruction of Shilo and the building of the Temple when the Tent was in Nob, the Ark at Bet Shemesh, Qiryat Yearim, or Jerusalem and the altar at Gibeon, private altars were permitted since a place can be considered to be “selected by the Eternal” only if it is the place both of the altar and the complete Tent. Mishnah <i>Zevaḥim</i>14:4–10.</i>. This is the principle: Anything which can be vowed or donated<sup class="footnote-marker">223</sup><i class="footnote">Dedications of animals as Temple sacrifices.</i> is sacrificed on a private altar; but nothing which cannot be vowed or donated is sacrificed on a private altar.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> [“The only difference between a public altar and a private altar, etc.”] Rebbi Joḥanan spent three years that he did not visit the house of assembly because of pain. At the end Rebbi Eleazar saw in his dream: Tomorrow Sinai will come down and bring a new insight. He came and said before them, from where is this truth verified that divine service is by firstborns?<sup class="footnote-marker">470</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Zevachim 14:4" href="/Mishnah_Zevachim.14.4">Mishnah <i>Zevaḥim</i>14:4</a>: Before the Tabernacle was erected private altars were permitted and the service was in the hands of the firstborn.</i> From this verse<sup class="footnote-marker">471</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 3:13" href="/Numbers.3.13"><i>Num</i>. 3:13</a>. The reference is to the later part of the verse, <i>I sanctified for Me every firstborn in Israel</i>.</i>, <i>for Mine is every firstborn; on the day when I smote every firstborn in the Land of Egypt</i>, etc. And it is written<sup class="footnote-marker">472</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 12:12" href="/Exodus.12.12"><i>Ex</i>. 12:12</a>. The firstborn were sanctified to God because the gods of Egypt were destroyed.</i>, <i>and on all gods of Egypt I shall pass judgment</i>, etc. Before that what were they doing? <i>Rebecca took the desirable garments of her older son Esaw, which were with her in the house</i><sup class="footnote-marker">473</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Genesis 27:15" href="/Genesis.27.15"><i>Gen</i>. 27:15</a>.</i>. What are “the desirable”? That he was acting as High Priest. Rebbi Levi said, <i>the Eternal broke the staff of the evildoers</i><sup class="footnote-marker">474</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Isaiah 14:5" href="/Isaiah.14.5"><i>Is</i>. 14:5</a>.</i>, these are the firstborn who were the first to sacrifice to the Calf<sup class="footnote-marker">475</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Bamidbar Rabbah 4:5" href="/Bamidbar_Rabbah.4.5"><i>Num. rabba</i> 4(5</a>).</i>.
<sup class="footnote-marker">476</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Zevachim 115b" href="/Zevachim.115b">Babli <i>Zevaḥim</i>115b</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Zevachim 13:1" href="/Tosefta_Zevachim.13.1">Tosefta <i>Zevaḥim</i> 13:1</a>.</i>“Everything is brought on an altar<sup class="footnote-marker">477</sup><i class="footnote">A private altar when it is legitimate. The detailed rules of sacrifices spelled out in <i>Lev</i>. are valid only for places of public worship.</i>, domestic and wild animals<sup class="footnote-marker">478</sup><i class="footnote">On a public altar only cattle, sheep and goats are permitted.</i>, large and small birds<sup class="footnote-marker">479</sup><i class="footnote">On a public altar only pigeons and turtle doves are permitted.</i>, male and female<sup class="footnote-marker">480</sup><i class="footnote">By the rules of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 1:3" href="/Leviticus.1.3"><i>Lev</i>. 1:3</a>,<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 1:10" href="/Leviticus.1.10">10</a>, an elevation sacrifice can only be a male animal.</i>, whole [but not] (and)<sup class="footnote-marker">481</sup><i class="footnote">Clearly the text of the [corrector] is the only acceptable one.</i> defective, pure<sup class="footnote-marker">482</sup><i class="footnote">Kosher animals and birds, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 11" href="/Leviticus.11"><i>Lev</i>. 11</a>.</i> but not impure. All are brought as elevation sacrifice and do not need stripping and partitioning<sup class="footnote-marker">483</sup><i class="footnote">In the Babylonian sources (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Megillah 1:11:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Megillah.1.11.3">Note 476</a>): they need stripping (remove the hide) and partitioning.</i>. Gentiles today are permitted to do this.” From where male and female? Rebbi Abbahu in the name of Rebbi Yose bar Ḥanina: <i>and the cows they offered as elevation offering to the Eternal</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">484</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="I Samuel 6:14" href="/I_Samuel.6.14"><i>1S</i>. 6:14</a>, referring to the cows which drew the cart on which the Philistines returned the Ark to Bet Shemesh. Since the place at Shilo was inactive without the Ark in the Tabernacle, it was a time when private altars were permitted.</i> From where whole [but not] (and)<sup class="footnote-marker">481</sup><i class="footnote">Clearly the text of the [corrector] is the only acceptable one.</i> defective? Rebbi Yasa said, Rebbi Eleazar made it clear to the colleagues, <i>from all living, from integer flesh</i><sup class="footnote-marker">485</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Genesis 6:19" href="/Genesis.6.19"><i>Gen</i>. 6:19</a>. The animal must be complete from the outside. It does not need inspection of its internal organs as required for kosher consumption of the meat or as sacrifice on a public altar. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Zevachim 116a" href="/Zevachim.116a">Babli <i>Zevaḥim</i> 116a</a>.</i>, that they should be complete in their limbs. Plucked? Rebbi Eleazar made it clear, <i>any bird, any wing</i><sup class="footnote-marker">486</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Genesis 7:14" href="/Genesis.7.14"><i>Gen</i>. 7:14</a>. If the feathers are removed, the bird has no more wings.</i>, to exclude plucked ones. From where pure but not impure? Rebbi Abba the son of Rebbi Pappai, Rebbi Joshua from Sikhnin in the name of Rebbi Levi: Noah pondered a lesson from the Torah. He said, it already was said to me, <i>like vegetables from the field I gave you everything</i><sup class="footnote-marker">487</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Genesis 9:3" href="/Genesis.9.3"><i>Gen.</i>9:3</a>. While Adam was created as a vegetarian (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Genesis 1:29" href="/Genesis.1.29"><i>Gen</i>. 1:29</a>), Noah was told to be an omnivore.</i>. Why did Scripture increase the pure ones? For sacrifices. And Gentiles today are permitted to do this. Rebbi Abba in the name of Rav Jehudah, it is forbidden to a Jew to help him and it is forbidden to become his agent<sup class="footnote-marker">488</sup><i class="footnote">Since private altars were forbidden after the building of the Temple, Jews cannot be involved in any way in building or serving such an altar. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Zevachim 116b" href="/Zevachim.116b">Babli <i>Zevaḥim</i> 116b</a>.</i>.
Antoninus<sup class="footnote-marker">489</sup><i class="footnote">A crypto-Jewish Roman Emperor, probably of the Severan dynasty. It is futile to try to determine to whom one refers; cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kilayim 9:3:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kilayim.9.3.3"><i>Kilaim</i>9:4, Note 79</a>. It was implied already in Halakhah 1 that Gentiles may have part in the World to Come.</i> asked Rebbi, may I build an altar? He said to him, build it and hide its stones. May one make incense for him? He said to him, make it without one of its ingredients. Was it not stated, <i>you may not make for yourselves</i><sup class="footnote-marker">490</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 30:37" href="/Exodus.30.37"><i>Ex</i>. 30:37</a>.</i>? You may not make for yourselves [but] others may make for you<sup class="footnote-marker">491</sup><i class="footnote">Gentiles may make the incense for non-sacral use.</i>. Rebbi Ḥanania said, this was for Rebbi Romanos<sup class="footnote-marker">492</sup><i class="footnote">Rebbi’s agent for matters to be done outside the seat of the Patriarchate.</i> whom Rebbi sent to make it for him. <sup class="footnote-marker">493</sup><i class="footnote">A parallel, in slightly different order, is in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sanhedrin 10:5:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sanhedrin.10.5.3">Sanhedrin 10:6 (Notes 331–338</a>). There, in Halakhah 3:12, and in Midrashic sources, the Emperor is called Antolinus.</i> There are indications implying that (Antolinus) [Antoninus] converted; there are indications implying that (Antolinus) [Antoninus] did not convert. One saw him walking with a slight shoe on the Day of Atonement<sup class="footnote-marker">332</sup><i class="footnote">The scroll may not be used for public readings.</i>. What do you infer since even God-fearing people go outside thus? (Antolinus) [Antoninus] said to Rebbi, can I eat from the Leviathan<sup class="footnote-marker">495</sup><i class="footnote">The just feasting on Leviathan meat in the World to Come are also mentioned in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Vayikra Rabbah 22:7" href="/Vayikra_Rabbah.22.7"><i>Lev. rabba</i> 22(7</a>), <a class="refLink" data-ref="Bava Batra 74b-75a" href="/Bava_Batra.74b-75a">Babli <i>Bava batra</i>74b–75a</a>.</i> in the World to Come? He said to him, yes. He told him, from the Passover lamb you would not let me eat, but from Leviathan you make me eat? He answered, what can we do for you since about the Passover lamb it is written that <i>no uncircumcised man may eat from it</i><sup class="footnote-marker">496</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 12:48" href="/Exodus.12.48"><i>Ex</i>. 12:48</a>.</i>. When he heard this, he went and circumcized. He came to Rebbi and said to him, look at my circumcision. He answered him, at mine I never looked<sup class="footnote-marker">497</sup><i class="footnote">It is indecent to look at sexual organs. Other references to this insert are <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Megillah 3:2:7" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Megillah.3.2.7"><i>Megillah</i>3:3</a> (74a l.39); <i>Avodah zarah</i> 3:1 (42c l.5); <a class="refLink" data-ref="Shabbat 118b" href="/Shabbat.118b">Babli <i>Šabbat</i> 118b</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Pesachim 104a" href="/Pesachim.104a"><i>Pesaḥim</i> 104a</a>.</i>, and at yours I should look? Why is he called our holy teacher? Because he never in his life looked at his circumcision. And why is his name Naḥum the holiest of holies<sup class="footnote-marker">498</sup><i class="footnote">An otherwise unknown personality.</i>? Because he never in his life looked at the figure on a coin. This implies that (Antolinus) [Antoninus] converted. The statement of the rabbis implies that (Antolinus) [Antoninus] did (not) convert, as <sup class="footnote-marker">331</sup><i class="footnote">“Open at the start” means that the writing of the first paragraph starts only in the middle of the line. This is normal for paragraphs following a closed paragraph. Open at the bottom means that the remainder of the last line of the paragraph remains empty; this is the definition of an open paragraph. Open on both sides implies open at the start.</i> Rebbi Ḥizqiah, Rebbi Abbahu in the name of Rebbi Eleazar said: When in the Future World the proselytes come, (Antolinus) [Antoninus] comes at the head of all of them.
Rebbi Eleazar and Rebbi Yose ben Ḥanina. Rebbi Eleazar said, the Sons of Noah brought well-being offerings. Rebbi Yose ben Ḥanina said, the Sons of Noah brought elevation offerings<sup class="footnote-marker">499</sup><i class="footnote">Noah certainly sacrificed elevation sacrifices (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Genesis 8:20" href="/Genesis.8.20"><i>Gen</i>. 8:20</a>); therefore these are certainly permitted on any private altar. Well-being sacrifices can be permitted on a private altar only if they are permitted to Gentiles, the children of Noah. In order to permit well-being sacrifices on private altars one must find examples of such sacrifices from the time preceding the epiphany on Sinai. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Zevachim 116a" href="/Zevachim.116a">Babli <i>Zevaḥim</i> 116a</a>.</i>. Rebbi Eleazar objected to Rebbi Yose ben Ḥanina: Is it not written, <i>and Abel also brought from the firstlings of his sheep and of their excellence</i><sup class="footnote-marker">500</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Genesis 4:4" href="/Genesis.4.4"><i>Gen</i>. 4:4</a>. In Torah law, firstlings are sacrificed as special well-being offerings.<br>In <i>Lev</i>. it is quite clear that “fat” in general is חֵלֶב ;פֶּדֶר is used only for fat destined to the altar and forbidden to humans. The suffixed form is חֶלְבְּהֶ֑ן. In <i>Gen</i>. the suffixed form is וּמֵֽחֶלְבֵהֶ֑ן; one has to explain חֵלֶב in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Megillah 4:4:2-8" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Megillah.4.4.2-8">4:4</a> and 45:18 from Accadic <i>ẖilibū</i> “magnificence, splendor, excellence.” This interpretation is accepted here.</i>. What does Rebbi Yose ben Ḥanina do with this? From their luxurious ones. Rebbi Eleazar objected to Rebbi Yose ben Ḥanina: Is it not written, <i>he sent the young men of Israel and they offered elevation offerings</i><sup class="footnote-marker">501</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 24:5" href="/Exodus.24.5"><i>Ex</i>. 24:5</a>. The argument is the part of the verse which is not quoted: <i>they sacrificed oxen as well-being sacrifices to the Eternal</i>.</i>, etc.? What does Rebbi Yose ben Ḥanina do with this? Entire in their bodies, without stripping and partitioning<sup class="footnote-marker">502</sup><i class="footnote">Instead of שְׁלָמִים he reads שְׁלֵמִים. Cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Chagigah 6b" href="/Chagigah.6b">Babli <i>Ḥagigah</i> 6b</a>.</i>. Rebbi Eleazar objected to Rebbi Yose ben Ḥanina: [Is it not written, <i>Jethro, Moses’s in-law, took elevation and well-being offerings for God</i><sup class="footnote-marker">503</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 18:12" href="/Exodus.18.12"><i>Ex</i>. 18:12</a>. While the corrector’s addition seems to be a logical necessity, it probably is taken from <a class="refLink" data-ref="Zevachim 116a" href="/Zevachim.116a">Babli <i>Zevaḥim</i>116a</a>.</i>?] What does Rebbi Yose ben Ḥanina do with this? Following him who said that Jethro came after the giving of the Torah<sup class="footnote-marker">504</sup><i class="footnote">His sacrifices followed all rules of <i>Lev</i>.</i>. Rebbi Ḥuna said, Jehudah the great one and Rebbi Yannai disagreed. One said, Jethro came before the giving of the Torah; but the other one said, Jethro came after the giving of the Torah. We did not know who said what. Let us hear from the following: <i>Jethro, the priest of Midyan, Moses’s in-law, heard</i><sup class="footnote-marker">505</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 18:1" href="/Exodus.18.1"><i>Ex</i>. 18:1</a>.</i>. What did he hear? Ḥizqiah said, he heard the parting of the Reed Sea. Rebbi Joshua said, he heard the parting of the Reed Sea. [Rebbi Levi said, he heard the war of Amaleq.] The Great Jehudah said, he heard the giving of the Torah. Therefore he must be the one who said, Jethro came after the giving of the Torah. Rebbi Abba and Rebbi Ḥiyya in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan, the following supports Rebbi Yose ben Ḥanina: <i>Awake North, and come South</i><sup class="footnote-marker">506</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Song of Songs 4:16" href="/Song_of_Songs.4.16"><i>Cant</i>. 4:16</a>.</i>. <i>Awake North</i> refers to the elevation sacrifice which is slaughtered in the North. What means “awake”? What was sleeping<sup class="footnote-marker">507</sup><i class="footnote">Old practices resurrected.</i> and awoke. <i>And come South</i> refers to well-being sacrifices which are slaughtered in the South. What means “come”? Something new<sup class="footnote-marker">508</sup><i class="footnote">This is R. Yose ben Ḥanina’s argument that well-being sacrifices were introduced only by the Torah.</i>. What does Rebbi Yose ben Ḥanina do with this? When the diasporas in the North awake and build the Temple in the South. Rebbi Abba the son of Rebbi Pappai, Rebbi Joshua from Sikhnin in the name of Rebbi Levi: also the  following verse supports Rebbi Yose ben Ḥanina: <i>This is the teaching about the elevation sacrifice</i><sup class="footnote-marker">509</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 6:2" href="/Leviticus.6.2"><i>Lev</i>. 6:2</a>.</i><i>. This is the elevation sacrifice</i> which the Sons of Noah were sacrificing. When he comes to well-being sacrifice he says, <i>this is the teaching about the well-being sacrifice</i><sup class="footnote-marker">510</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 7:11" href="/Leviticus.7.11"><i>Lev</i>. 7:11</a>. Again the argument is from the continuation of the verse, not quoted.</i>. It is not written “which one brought” but <i>shall bring</i> from now on..
Where are burned oxen and burned goats burned<sup class="footnote-marker">511</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 6:23" href="/Leviticus.6.23"><i>Lev</i>. 6:23</a>. Any purification offering whose blood was brought inside the Sanctuary has to be burned (the purification sacrifices of the anointed High Priest, the community, and those of the Day of Atonement.)</i>? On a grave<sup class="footnote-marker">512</sup><i class="footnote">The paradigm of a place of impurity. In <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 4:12" href="/Leviticus.4.12"><i>Lev</i>. 4:12</a> it is ordered that the High Priest’s bull be burned on a place of purity. It is stated here that this applies only to the functioning Sanctuary in the desert, at Shilo, and in the Temple, but not at any other public altar.</i>. Since we have stated, the only difference between a public altar and a private altar was the <i>Pesaḥ</i> sacrifice<sup class="footnote-marker">513</sup><i class="footnote">Since purification sacrifices to be burned are public offerings they seem to be improper on private altars. Does the Mishnah authorize them on private altars?</i>? So in the Mishnah: The only difference between a public altar and a private altar was the <i>Pesaḥ</i> sacrifice exclusively<sup class="footnote-marker">514</sup><i class="footnote">This means either that all purification sacrifices are permitted on private altars when such altars are permitted, or that no purification sacrifices are permitted on any altar when private altars are permitted.</i>; not following Rebbi Jehudah, since Rebbi Jehudah said, purification sacrifice and <i>Pesaḥ</i> for the individual on the public altar, no purification sacrifice and <i>Pesaḥ</i> for the individual on a private altar. It was stated<sup class="footnote-marker">515</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Zevachim 117a" href="/Zevachim.117a">Babli <i>Zevaḥim</i> 117a</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Zevachim 13:5" href="/Tosefta_Zevachim.13.5">Tosephta <i>Zevaḥim</i> 13:15</a>.</i>: “Rebbi Jehudah said, anything the community sacrifices at the Tent of Meeting in the desert it sacrifices at the Tent of Meeting at Gilgal<sup class="footnote-marker">516</sup><i class="footnote">The temporary place of the Tabernacle during the conquest of Canaan under Joshua.</i>. What is the difference between the Tent of Meeting in the desert and the Tent of Meeting at Gilgal? At the Tent of Meeting in the desert was no permission of a private altar; at the Tent of Meeting at Gilgal was permission of a private altar. His altar may be on his roof, but he may sacrifice there only elevation and well-being sacrifices. But the Sages are saying, everything which community or private person sacrifice at the Tent of Meeting in the desert they sacrifice at the Tent of Meeting at Gilgal<sup class="footnote-marker">517</sup><i class="footnote">Everybody agrees that on a private altar one could bring only voluntary offerings. On the public altar according to R. Jehudah only public sacrifices are possible; according to the rabbis also obligatory sacrifices of individuals.</i>.” What is Rebbi Jehudah’s reason? <i>Do not do</i> at Gilgal <i>everything which we are doing here today</i><sup class="footnote-marker">518</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 12:8" href="/Deuteronomy.12.8"><i>Deut</i>. 12:8</a>.</i>. What may you do there? Anything straight-forward<sup class="footnote-marker">519</sup><i class="footnote">A reformulation of the end of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 12:8" href="/Deuteronomy.12.8"><i>Deut</i>. 12:8</a>.</i>. What is that? That are elevation and well-being sacrifices. What is the rabbis’ reason? At a place where any man may do what is right in his eyes. <i>Do not do everything which we are doing here today.</i> What may you do there? Anything straight-forward. What is that? That are elevation and well-being sacrifices. Rebbi Jehudah says, the individual was warned and the individual was permitted. He was warned, and his private altar is on his roof, and he is permitted elevation and well-being sacrifices. The rabbis are saying, also community and individuals were warned. The community was comprehensively permitted. The individual stays in his prohibition<sup class="footnote-marker">520</sup><i class="footnote">A commandment in the plural is directed to individuals, in the singular it is interpreted as a collective and addressed to the community as a whole. In <a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 12:8" href="/Deuteronomy.12.8"><i>Deut</i>. 12:8</a> the first part is in the plural, the second in the singular. R. Jehudah reads the entire verse as addressed to the individual; therefore on a public altar one may not sacrifice what one cannot do on a private altar (except the case of the <i>Pesaḥ</i> treated in the next paragraph which is a private well-being sacrifice required to be brought on the public altar), whereas the majority splits the verse to permit all kinds of public sacrifices on the public altar. In the tradition of the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Zevachim 117a" href="/Zevachim.117a">Babli <i>Zevaḥim</i>117a</a> the roles are switched.</i>.
It was stated in the name of Rebbi Simeon<sup class="footnote-marker">521</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Zevachim 13:5" href="/Tosefta_Zevachim.13.5">Tosephta <i>Zevaḥim</i>13:15</a>. The only sacrifices authorized on a public altar are those fixed beforehand, not depending on circumstances. In the Tosephta, the statement is attributed to R. Jehudah. But since the following text starts with “in addition, R. Simeon says …”, the prior text must be R. Simeon’s (Note of <i>Pene Moshe.</i>)</i>: “The community only brings what Scripture fixed for it.” Rebbi Simeon is of the opinion that since when Second Tithe was introduced it was not interrupted<sup class="footnote-marker">522</sup><i class="footnote">This statement does not belong here, it was copied from <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Megillah 1:12:2-9" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Megillah.1.12.2-9">Halakhah 14</a>. Second Tithe is the farmer’s property to be consumed in purity “before the Eternal” (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 14:23" href="/Deuteronomy.14.23"><i>Deut</i>. 14:23</a>). When the Tent was at Nob, the altar at Gibeon, and the Ark at Qiryat Yearim, it is difficult to see where “before the Eternal” was. He posits that the duty of Second Tithe was in force in the interval between Siloh and Solomon’s Temple.</i>. What is between them<sup class="footnote-marker">523</sup><i class="footnote">R. Simeon and the majority who permit on the public altar anything sacrificed in the desert.</i>? Rebbi Abba bar Mamal said, the bull of hidden things<sup class="footnote-marker">524</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 4:13-21" href="/Leviticus.4.13-21"><i>Lev</i>. 4:13–21</a>, the purification offering for erroneous acts of the community.</i> is between them. Rebbi Yose says, voluntary offerings of the community are between them. It was stated, both men and women<sup class="footnote-marker">525</sup><i class="footnote">May sacrifice on a private altar.</i>. Rebbi Joḥanan said, there is no woman here, “man” is written in the paragraph<sup class="footnote-marker">526</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 12:8" href="/Deuteronomy.12.8"><i>Deut</i>. 12:8</a>.</i>. It was stated, also a nazir’s sacrifices<sup class="footnote-marker">527</sup><i class="footnote">May be brought on a private altar. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Temurah 14b" href="/Temurah.14b">Babli <i>Temurah</i> 14b</a>.</i>. Rebbi Joḥanan said, there are no nazir’s sacrifices here; nazir’s sacrifices are an obligation. It was stated, also libations<sup class="footnote-marker">528</sup><i class="footnote">As detailed in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 15:1-16" href="/Numbers.15.1-16"><i>Num</i>. 15:1–16</a>, required for the sacrifices authorized on private altars. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Zevachim 111a" href="/Zevachim.111a">Babli <i>Zevaḥim</i> 111a</a>.</i>. Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Abun said, he who states “libations” does not hold with Rebbi, as it was stated, Rebbi says, I am saying that also after Israel entered the Land, libations only are brought inside<sup class="footnote-marker">529</sup><i class="footnote">At the altar standing in a sanctified enclosure of the Tabernacle.</i>. What is the reason? <i>In the Sanctuary pour alcoholic libations to the Eternal</i><sup class="footnote-marker">530</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 28:7" href="/Numbers.28.7"><i>Num</i>. 28:7</a>.</i>. A prohibition is written only for inside; from where for the outside? Let us hear from the following. From where that one who slaughters as <i>Pesah</i> for an individual on a private altar or the public altar in a time where altars are forbidden, that he transgresses a prohibition? The verse says, <i>you may not slaughter the Pesaḥ at one</i><sup class="footnote-marker">531</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 16:6" href="/Deuteronomy.16.6"><i>Deut</i>. 16:6</a>, read as: only at one place for all of Israel.</i>. He is punished with extirpation and you are saying so<sup class="footnote-marker">532</sup><i class="footnote">Since the sacrifice as described is one at an unauthorized place and this is punished by extirpation (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 17:4" href="/Leviticus.17.4"><i>Lev</i>. 17:4</a>), whereas the verse quoted treats this as a simple misdemeanor.</i>? Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish said, this is about one who slaughters in the afternoon not for its name<sup class="footnote-marker">533</sup><i class="footnote">Which disqualifies the sacrifice from fulfilling any obligation; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Zevachim 1:1" href="/Mishnah_Zevachim.1.1">Mishnah <i>Zevaḥim</i> 1:1</a>.</i> in a time when altars are permitted. Then why does he say, in a time when altars are forbidden? In a time when his altar is forbidden<sup class="footnote-marker">534</sup><i class="footnote">Since <i>Pesaḥ</i> may not be brought on a private altar and is a modified well-being offering, a private altar on the afternoon of the 14<sup class="footnote-marker">th</sup> of Nisan is disqualified for any kind of well-being offering.</i>. Why not for its name? That you should not say, it is well-being offering<sup class="footnote-marker">535</sup><i class="footnote">An animal vowed for <i>Pesaḥ</i> but not used for this purpose automatically is dedicated as well-being offering.</i> and qualified. In addition, from the following: I could think that if he slaughtered it in the afternoon it should be qualified. Why do you have (in the afternoon it should be qualified) [in the morning it should be disqualified]<sup class="footnote-marker">536</sup><i class="footnote">The corrector’s text is to be deleted.</i>? No. It is not for its name<sup class="footnote-marker">533</sup><i class="footnote">Which disqualifies the sacrifice from fulfilling any obligation; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Mishnah Zevachim 1:1" href="/Mishnah_Zevachim.1.1">Mishnah <i>Zevaḥim</i> 1:1</a>.</i>. Rebbi Yose said, this implies that well-being sacrifices brought because of <i>Pesaḥ</i><sup class="footnote-marker">536</sup><i class="footnote">The corrector’s text is to be deleted.</i> are qualified on an altar. Would an elevation offering be brought because of a reparation sacrifice be qualified on an altar according to Rebbi Eleazar? Let us hear from the following: “This is the principle: Anything which can be vowed or donated<sup class="footnote-marker">223</sup><i class="footnote">Dedications of animals as Temple sacrifices.</i> is sacrificed on a private altar; but nothing which cannot be vowed or donated is sacrificed on a private altar.”
Rebbi Yose ben Ḥanina said, the altar is permitted only by a prophet. What is the reason? <i>Guard yourself lest you bring your elevation offerings at any place you are seeing, except at a place</i> <sup class="footnote-marker">537</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 12:13-14" href="/Deuteronomy.12.13-14"><i>Deut</i>. 12:13–14</a>. The argument is from the part not quoted, <i>except at a place which the Eternal will chose</i>, by informing a prophet of His choice.</i> etc. <sup class="footnote-marker">538</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Ta`aniot</i> 2:8, Notes 164–165.</i> And Elijah sacrifices when local altars are forbidden? Rebbi Simlai said, the Word He said to him<sup class="footnote-marker">539</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="I Kings 18:36" href="/I_Kings.18.36"><i>1K</i>. 18:36</a>.</i>: <i>I acted on Your saying</i>. I acted following Your Word.

Halakhah 12

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> The only difference between Shilo and Jerusalem was that at Shilo one ate simple sacrifices<sup class="footnote-marker">465</sup><i class="footnote">Sacrifices parts of which were eaten by laity.</i> and Second Tithe at sight<sup class="footnote-marker">466</sup><i class="footnote">Any place at which the Tabernacle could be seen.</i> and in Jerusalem inside the wall. In both cases most holy sacrifices<sup class="footnote-marker">467</sup><i class="footnote">Sacrifices either burned as holocaust or eaten in part by priests only.</i> are eaten inside the curtains<sup class="footnote-marker">468</sup><i class="footnote">The enclosure of the sacred domain which for the Tabernacle was formed by curtains.</i>. After the holiness of Shilo {temporary altars} were permitted but after Jerusalem {temporary altars} are not permitted<sup class="footnote-marker">469</sup><i class="footnote">When Shilo was destroyed the historical record shows that Samuel and Saul sacrificed at several places, none of which could claim to be “chosen by the Eternal.” But Solomon declared the Temple to be God’s place <i>forever</i>(<a class="refLink" data-ref="I Kings 8:13" href="/I_Kings.8.13"><i>1K.</i>8:13</a>; <a class="refLink" data-ref="II Chronicles 6:2" href="/II_Chronicles.6.2"><i>2Chr</i>. 6:2</a>).</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> <sup class="footnote-marker">540</sup><i class="footnote">Corrector’s addition, inserted at the wrong place since this paragraph is a direct continuation of the preceding one, showing that at all times a prophet may legally build an altar separate from the official sanctuary. As noted in N. 537, Deuteronomy nowhere declares that only one sanctuary is tolerated, but only that the place of sacrifices must have been chosen by the Eternal. The high cost of official worship as demanded in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 28-29" href="/Numbers.28-29"><i>Num</i>. 28–29</a> automatically restricts permanent worship to one place, making any additional place used on the authority of a recognized prophet a temporary matter.</i> Rebbi Joḥanan bar Marius understood it from the following<sup class="footnote-marker">541</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Joshua 8:30" href="/Joshua.8.30"><i>Jos</i>. 8:30</a>. Since the altar on Mount Ebal was built at the time when the Tent and Moses’s altar were at Gilgal, this proves that at this time secondary public altars were not forbidden.</i>: <i>Then Joshua would build an altar for the Eternal, the God of Israel, on Mount Ebal</i>. Not only Mount Ebal, from where Shilo<sup class="footnote-marker">542</sup><i class="footnote">That several official and private altars were permitted after the destruction of Shilo.</i>? <i>Samuel took a milk lamb and brought it up totally as elevation offering for the Eternal</i><sup class="footnote-marker">543</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="I Samuel 7:9" href="/I_Samuel.7.9"><i>1S</i>. 7:9</a>.</i>. Rebbi Abba bar Cahana said, three sins were permitted for Samuel’s sheep: It and its hide<sup class="footnote-marker">544</sup><i class="footnote">In <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 1:6" href="/Leviticus.1.6"><i>Lev</i>. 1:6</a> it is decreed that an elevation offering is burned without its hide. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Zevachim 120a" href="/Zevachim.120a">Babli <i>Zevaḥim</i> 120a</a>.</i>, and deficient in time<sup class="footnote-marker">545</sup><i class="footnote">This is not spelled out in the verse. No lamb may be sacrificed on an official altar if it is not at least 8 days old; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 23:27" href="/Leviticus.23.27"><i>Lev</i>. 23:27</a>.</i>, and he was a Levite<sup class="footnote-marker">546</sup><i class="footnote">He was a descendant of Qoraḥ, and only descendants of Aaron may officiate at a public altar.</i>. Rebbi Yose said, if about this it implies nothing, since Rebbi Abba bar Cahana said, seven sins were permitted for Gideon’s bull<sup class="footnote-marker">547</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Judges 6:25-27" href="/Judges.6.25-27"><i>Jud</i>. 6:25–27</a>. The stones had been used for an altar of Baal, therefore they were forbidden for all usufruct together with the wood of the Asherah tree. They could have been used only by direct commandment from God. That the bull had been worshipped as a deity is deduced from the involved language in the verse, where a single bull is called “second” to show that two sins were committed with it. (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Temurah 28b" href="/Temurah.28b">Babli <i>Temurah</i> 28b</a>.) That sacrifices are permitted only during daytime is deduced from <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 7:38" href="/Leviticus.7.38"><i>Lev</i>. 7:38</a> (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Megillah 2:6:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Megillah.2.6.2">Halakhah 2:5</a>). In Gideon’s time the sanctuary of Shilo was in existence.</i>: Disqualified stones, and Asherah wood, and separated, and worshipped, and night, and outsider, and altar prohibition. He who wants may understand it well from that by Rebbi Samuel bar Naḥman: <i>When he returned to Rama, for there was his house, and there he judged Israel, and there he built an altar for the Eternal</i><sup class="footnote-marker">548</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="I Samuel 8:17" href="/I_Samuel.8.17"><i>1S</i>. 8:17</a>, after the destruction of Shilo.</i>.
