It is written<sup class="footnote-marker">549</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="I Samuel 9:24" href="/I_Samuel.9.24"><i>1S</i>. 9:24</a>.</i>, <i>the cook lifted the thigh and what was on it and put before Saul</i>, etc. Rebbi Samuel bar Naḥman said, the thigh and meat<sup class="footnote-marker">550</sup><i class="footnote">The thigh belongs to the officiating Cohen (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 29:27-28" href="/Exodus.29.27-28"><i>Ex</i>. 29:27–28</a>); Saul could have received it only if no Cohen was officiating at the altar. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Avodah Zarah 25a" href="/Avodah_Zarah.25a">Babli <i>Avodah zarah</i> 25a</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Zevachim 119b" href="/Zevachim.119b"><i>Zevaḥim</i> 119b</a>.</i>. Rebbi Joḥanan said, the thigh and the fat tail<sup class="footnote-marker">551</sup><i class="footnote">The fat tail is to be burned on the altar, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 3:9" href="/Leviticus.3.9"><i>Lev</i>. 3:9</a>.</i>. Rebbi Eleazar said, the thigh and the breast<sup class="footnote-marker">550</sup><i class="footnote">The thigh belongs to the officiating Cohen (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 29:27-28" href="/Exodus.29.27-28"><i>Ex</i>. 29:27–28</a>); Saul could have received it only if no Cohen was officiating at the altar. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Avodah Zarah 25a" href="/Avodah_Zarah.25a">Babli <i>Avodah zarah</i> 25a</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Zevachim 119b" href="/Zevachim.119b"><i>Zevaḥim</i> 119b</a>.</i>, as Rebbi Eleazar said, the thigh and the breast belong to the Cohanim at a public altar but to the owner at a private altar. Rebbi Ze`ira in the name of Rebbi Eleazar: The hide of the elevation sacrifice belongs to the Cohanim at a public altar<sup class="footnote-marker">552</sup><i class="footnote">In <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 7:8" href="/Leviticus.7.8"><i>Lev</i>. 7:8</a> it is decreed that the hide belongs to the officiating Cohen. Since a non-Cohen was shown to be able to officiate at a private altar, the hide belongs to the owner. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Zevachim 119b" href="/Zevachim.119b">Babli <i>Zevaḥim</i> 119b</a>.</i> but to the owner at a private altar. Rebbi Ze`ira in the name of Rav Jeremiah: The contribution of a thanksgiving sacrifice<sup class="footnote-marker">553</sup><i class="footnote">The officiating Cohen’s part of the breads accompanying the sacrifice, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 7:14" href="/Leviticus.7.14"><i>Lev</i>. 7:14</a>.</i> belongs to the Cohanim at a public altar but to the owner at a private altar. Rebbi Joḥanan asked, is the night qualified at a private altar<sup class="footnote-marker">554</sup><i class="footnote">Since on an official altar sacrifices are possible only during daytime, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Megillah 2:6:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Megillah.2.6.2">Halakhah 2:5</a>.</i>? Rebbi Eleazar answered, is it not written,<sup class="footnote-marker">555</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="I Samuel 14:34" href="/I_Samuel.14.34"><i>1S</i>. 14:34</a>. It is stated in the verse that the slaughter was in the night; in v. 35 it is stated that Saul built an altar.</i> <i>Saul said, disperse under the people and tell them, every man shall bring to me his ox</i>, etc. And it is written<sup class="footnote-marker">556</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="I Samuel 14:33" href="/I_Samuel.14.33"><i>1S</i>. 14:33</a>, they engaged in pagan slaughter.</i>, <i>they told Saul saying, behold the people are sinning against the Eternal by eating on the blood</i>, etc. How is this? The night for profane {slaughter}, and the day for sacrificial. When Rebbi Joḥanan heard this, he said, well did Rebbi Eleazar teach us<sup class="footnote-marker">557</sup><i class="footnote">That the verse emphasizes profane slaughter during nighttime. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Zevachim 120a" href="/Zevachim.120a">Babli <i>Zevaḥim</i> 120a</a>.</i>.
Rebbi Phineas in the name of Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Ila`y. One verse says, <i>he abandoned the dwelling at Shilo</i><sup class="footnote-marker">558</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Psalms 78:60" href="/Psalms.78.60"><i>Ps</i>. 78:60</a>. The argument is based on the second part of the verse, <i>a Tent He made dwell among mankind</i>.</i>, but an other verse says, <i>she brought him to the Eternal’s House at Shilo</i><sup class="footnote-marker">559</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="I Samuel 1:25" href="/I_Samuel.1.25"><i>1Sam</i>. 1:25</a>.</i>. How is this? A stone house below and the gobelins on top<sup class="footnote-marker">560</sup><i class="footnote">The three layers of covers of the Tent of Meeting were drawn over a stone wall. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Zevachim 118a" href="/Zevachim.118a">Babli <i>Zevaḥim</i> 118a</a>.</i>, and this was “rest.<sup class="footnote-marker">561</sup><i class="footnote">Private altars are prohibited in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 12:9" href="/Deuteronomy.12.9"><i>Deut</i>. 12:9</a> when Israel comes <i>to the rest and the inheritance</i>. “Rest” is defined as Shilo, “inheritance” as Jerusalem. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Megillah 10a" href="/Megillah.10a">Babli 10a</a>.</i>” Rebbi Ze`ira said, a minimum of ten hand-breadths, as in building a house. But was it not stated, just as the Tent of Meeting was erected so was the Tent at Shilo erected? Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Abun said, <i>its poles, its planks, its latches, its bases, and its columns</i><sup class="footnote-marker">562</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 39:33" href="/Exodus.39.33"><i>Ex</i>. 39:33</a>.</i>. Its poles were visible inside like stars in the sky.
The Great Rebbi Ḥiyya said to Rebbi Simeon ben Rebbi: I heard from your fathers that all covers<sup class="footnote-marker">563</sup><i class="footnote">The Tabernacles and the Temple erected in the Land, including Nob and Gibeon; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Zevachim 118b" href="/Zevachim.118b">Babli <i>Zevaḥim</i> 118b</a>..</i> [which existed] only were in Benjamin’s part. What is the reason? <i>He rests between his shoulders</i><sup class="footnote-marker">564</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 33:12" href="/Deuteronomy.33.12"><i>Deut</i>. 33:12</a>, in the blessing of Benjamin.</i>. But is it not written, <i>He despised the Tent of Joseph and did not choose the tribe of Ephraim, but chose the tribe of Judah</i><sup class="footnote-marker">565</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Psalms 78:67-68" href="/Psalms.78.67-68"><i>Ps</i>. 78:67–68</a>. This locates the Temple in the territory of Judah even though <a class="refLink" data-ref="Joshua 15:8" href="/Joshua.15.8"><i>Jos</i>. 15:8</a> puts the Northern border of Judah in the Hinnom valley (and probably Jaffa Street.)</i>. Did he choose Judah<sup class="footnote-marker">566</sup><i class="footnote">Since it was stated earlier that the Temple building was in Benjamin.</i>? But choosing, choosing. Since choosing there includes Benjamin, so choosing here includes Benjamin<sup class="footnote-marker">567</sup><i class="footnote">If the place at Shiloh was chosen in Benjamin, the one in Jerusalem also was in Benjamin (while occupied by Jebusites.)</i>. Rebbi Jonah, Rebbi Abbahu in the name of Rebbi Yose ben Ḥanina: <i>and the pleasure of the Dweller in the senna</i><sup class="footnote-marker">568</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 33:15" href="/Deuteronomy.33.15"><i>Deut</i>. 33:15</a>, in the blessing of Joseph.</i>. Sacral meat given to atonement is consumed in the part of Joseph<sup class="footnote-marker">569</sup><i class="footnote">Even if the Tent at Shiloh was on Benjamin territory, the place outside the Tent reserved for the priests’ eating of sacral meat was in Joseph.</i>. Rebbi Eudaimon of Sepphoris said, like vertex of a trapezoid<sup class="footnote-marker">570</sup><i class="footnote">A technical term otherwise used in the laws of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Kilayim 1:9:5" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Kilayim.1.9.5"><i>Kilaim</i>1:9, Note 202</a>.<br>It would make more sense to read “from Benjamin’s part into Joseph’s part.”</i> entered from Joseph’s part into Benjamin’s part; on it the altar was situated. What is the reason? <i>The border turned eastward to Taanat-Shilo</i><sup class="footnote-marker">571</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Joshua 16:6" href="/Joshua.16.6"><i>Jos</i>. 16:6</a>. Since this identifies <i>Taanat Shilo</i> (“the fig tree of Shilo) as a suburb of Shilo, they must read the description of the Ephraimite border in the verse as description of the Southern border. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Zevachim 118b" href="/Zevachim.118b">Babli <i>Zevaḥim</i> 118b</a>.</i>, up to the door-sill<sup class="footnote-marker">572</sup><i class="footnote">An Accadic word, <i>askuppu</i>.</i> of Shilo. Rebbi Jonah, Rebbi Abbahu in the name of Rebbi Yose ben Ḥanina: <i>and the pleasure of the Dweller in the senna</i>. That the Divine Presence dwelt in the greatness<sup class="footnote-marker">573</sup><i class="footnote">Reading the otherwise undocumented סנא as Arabic “elevation, greatness”.</i> of Joseph.
Rebbi Eleazar in the name of Rebbi Hoshaia: Since simple sacrifices and Second Tithe were eaten at sight<sup class="footnote-marker">466</sup><i class="footnote">Any place at which the Tabernacle could be seen.</i> the person who stated the Mishnah did not need to warn about the consumption but about its bringing<sup class="footnote-marker">574</sup><i class="footnote">The main point in Mishnah 14 is not that these have to be eaten there but, by implication, that they are forbidden except at the official Sanctuary. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Zevachim 118a" href="/Zevachim.118a">Babli <i>Zevaḥim</i> 118a</a>.</i>. What is the reason? <i>Guard yourself, lest you bring your elevation sacrifices</i><sup class="footnote-marker">575</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 12:13" href="/Deuteronomy.12.13"><i>Deut</i>. 12:13</a>, the verse forbidding altars which are not legitimized by a prophet.</i>, lest you bring what you are obliged to offer<sup class="footnote-marker">576</sup><i class="footnote">To an illegitimate place.</i>. Rebbi Simeon bar Miasha asked before Rebbi Eleazar: What means “at sight”? Shilo or Shiloh and the Sanctuary at Shilo? He told him, Shiloh and the Sanctuary at Shilo, as for example Bet-Maon, only that there is an interruption and here is no interruption<sup class="footnote-marker">577</sup><i class="footnote">It is necessary not only that the Sanctuary be seen but also that it be possible to reach the Sanctuary from the place of consumption without leaving the area from which it is seen. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Zevachim 118b" href="/Zevachim.118b">Babli <i>Zevaḥim</i> 118b</a>.</i>.
Rebbi Yasa in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: This is the indication: Any time the Ark is inside<sup class="footnote-marker">578</sup><i class="footnote">The Tabernacle or the Temple.</i>, private altars are forbidden; once it left, private altars are permitted. Rebbi Ze`ura asked before Rebbi Yasa: even temporarily, as in the case of Eli<sup class="footnote-marker">579</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="I Samuel 4:4" href="/I_Samuel.4.4"><i>1S</i>. 4:4</a>. While it turned out that the absence of the Ark was permanent, it was intended to be temporary. What was the status of private altars between the removal of the Ark from Shilo and its installation in Qiryat Yearim?</i>? Rebbi Abbahu in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan, “there”, “there”. Just as in the case of “there” mentioned there private altars are forbidden, so in the case of “there” mentioned here private altars are forbidden<sup class="footnote-marker">580</sup><i class="footnote">It is not quite clear to which verses this refers. In <a class="refLink" data-ref="Zevachim 119a" href="/Zevachim.119a"><i>Zevaḥim</i>119a</a> Rashi refers to the prohibition of private altars, where “there” is used both in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 12:7" href="/Deuteronomy.12.7"><i>Deut</i>. 12:7</a> and <a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 14:23" href="/Deuteronomy.14.23">14:23</a> regarding the obligation to sacrifice at the public shrine, and <a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 40:3" href="/Exodus.40.3"><i>Ex</i>. 40:3</a> regarding the place of the Ark in the Tabernacle.</i>. Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish asked before Rebbi Joḥanan, in that case if there is no Ark is there no <i>Pesaḥ</i><sup class="footnote-marker">581</sup><i class="footnote">In that case there could not have been any <i>Pesaḥ</i> sacrifice in the Second Temple, whose basic institutions were approved by Zachariah, Haggai, and Malachia. Since we know that the <i>Pesaḥ</i> was sacrificed there, the argument presented must be invalid.</i>? Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Abun said, “appellation”, “appellation”. Just as in the case of “appellation” mentioned there private altars are forbidden, so in the case of “appellation” mentioned here private altars are forbidden<sup class="footnote-marker">582</sup><i class="footnote">It is not clear what this means. It cannot refer to the biblical meaning of the word אַזְכָּרָה, the declaration by the priest that the part taken is God’s part to be burned on the altar, since this does not apply to the items in question. Therefore it must refer to the rabbinic meaning of אַזְכָּרָה, the Name YHWH, which appears many times both in reference to permitted and prohibited altars.</i>. And so Rebbi Jehudah and Rebbi Simeon were saying, Second Tithe in Nob and Gibeon. One understands Rebbi Jehudah, since Rebbi Jehudah said, purification sacrifice and <i>Pesaḥ</i> for the individual on the public altar, no purification sacrifice and <i>Pesah</i> for the individual on a private altar<sup class="footnote-marker">583</sup><i class="footnote">He expressly endorses <i>Pesaḥ</i> on any public altar, without reference to the Ark.</i>. Following Rebbi Simeon? Since Rebbi Simeon said, “The community only brings what Scripture fixed for it.<sup class="footnote-marker">584</sup><i class="footnote">But <i>Pesaḥ</i> depends on the calendar, it is not fixed beforehand and therefore should be forbidden on a public altar. The same holds a fortiori for Second Tithe which depends on the crop; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Megillah 1:11:7" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Megillah.1.11.7">Note 521</a>.</i>” Rebbi Simeon is of the opinion that since when Second Tithe was introduced it was not interrupted<sup class="footnote-marker">522</sup><i class="footnote">This statement does not belong here, it was copied from <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Megillah 1:12:2-9" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Megillah.1.12.2-9">Halakhah 14</a>. Second Tithe is the farmer’s property to be consumed in purity “before the Eternal” (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 14:23" href="/Deuteronomy.14.23"><i>Deut</i>. 14:23</a>). When the Tent was at Nob, the altar at Gibeon, and the Ark at Qiryat Yearim, it is difficult to see where “before the Eternal” was. He posits that the duty of Second Tithe was in force in the interval between Siloh and Solomon’s Temple.</i>.
<sup class="footnote-marker">585</sup><i class="footnote">This text is from <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Pesachim 2:4:7-9" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Pesachim.2.4.7-9"><i>Pesaḥim</i> 2:4 (Notes 210–227</a>) except for an unwarranted addition by the corrector in the <i>Pesaḥim</i> text.</i> From where that the Cohanim may fulfill their obligation with <i>ḥallah</i> or heave, and Israel with Second Tithe on Passover? The verse says, <i>you shall eat mazzot</i><sup class="footnote-marker">586</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 12:18" href="/Exodus.12.18"><i>Ex</i>. 12:18</a>,<a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 12:20" href="/Exodus.12.20">20</a>. <i>Mekhilta dR. Simeon ben Yohay</i> 12:20 (p. 24).</i>; this adds. Could I think that they may fulfill their obligation with First Fruits? The verse says, <i>in</i><b>all</b><i>your dwelling places you shall eat mazzot</i><sup class="footnote-marker">587</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 12:20" href="/Exodus.12.20"><i>Ex</i>. 12:20</a>.</i>, <i>mazzah</i> which may be eaten at any dwelling place; this excludes First Fruits which are not eaten at any dwelling place. They objected, but may Second Tithe be eaten at any dwelling place? It may be redeemed and be eaten at any dwelling place. Rebbi Abun bar Ḥiyya asked: What was bought with tithe money and became impure, following Rebbi Jehudah, since it is not subject to being redeemed and eaten at any dwelling place, one may not fulfill one’s obligation with it. Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish asked: <i>Ḥallah</i> from dough made from Second Tithe in Jerusalem, since it is not subject to being redeemed and eaten at any dwelling place, one may not fulfill one’s obligation with it.
I could think that they may fulfill their obligation with flat cakes for a thanksgiving offering and wafers of a <i>nazir</i>. The verse says, <i>seven days you shall eat mazzot</i><sup class="footnote-marker">588</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 12:15" href="/Exodus.12.15"><i>Ex</i>. 12:15</a>.</i>; <i>mazzah</i> which may be eaten all seven days. This excludes thanksgiving offering and wafers of a <i>nazir</i> which may not be eaten all seven days. Rebbi Jonah in the name of Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish: since flat cakes for a thanksgiving offering and (goats) [wafers]<sup class="footnote-marker">589</sup><i class="footnote">The scribe’s text clearly is in error.</i> of a <i>nazir</i> may be eaten in the entire domain of the Land of Israel, the presenter could not exclude because of “dwelling place”. Rebbi Yose in the name of Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish: this implies that wafers of a <i>nazir</i> may be eaten in the entire domain of the Land of Israel, the presenter could not exclude because of “dwelling place”. One understands about flat cakes for a thanksgiving offering. But it is not so for wafers of a <i>nazir</i>. Rebbi Joḥanan said, there is no <i>nazir</i> here; <i>nezirut</i> is an obligation<sup class="footnote-marker">527</sup><i class="footnote">May be brought on a private altar. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Temurah 14b" href="/Temurah.14b">Babli <i>Temurah</i> 14b</a>.</i>. Rebbi Abun bar Cahana said, explain it if his purification offering was presented in Shiloh but his elevation and well-being offerings in Nob or Gibeon. Rebbi Ḥananiah, Rebbi Ezrah asked before Rebbi Mana: Did not the teacher say in the name of Rebbi Yose: Holiday well-being offerings brought at an elevated place are qualified but they are not counted against an obligation of the owner? But it must be for Rebbi Jehudah, as Rebbi Jehudah said, purification and Passover offerings of individuals at a principal elevated place, but no purification and Passover offerings of individuals at a private altar. It only follows Rebbi Simeon, since “Rebbi Simeon said, when one of the bloods was sprinkled, the <i>nazir</i> is permitted to drink wine and to defile himself for the dead.<sup class="footnote-marker">590</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Nazir 6:9:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Nazir.6.9.1">Mishnah <i>Nazir</i> 6:11</a>. Therefore only the first sacrifice of the <i>nazir</i> is obligatory, the others together with the bread may be offered locally.</i>”
There are Tannaim who state, <i>rest</i> is Shilo, <i>inheritance</i> is Jerusalem. There are Tannaim who state, <i>rest</i> is Jerusalem, <i>inheritance</i> is Shilo<sup class="footnote-marker">591</sup><i class="footnote">Since in the verse quoted next the times when private altars are forbidden is called <i>rest</i> and <i>inheritance</i>. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Megillah 10a" href="/Megillah.10a">Babli 10a</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Zevachim 119a" href="/Zevachim.119a"><i>Zevaḥim</i> 119a</a>.</i>. He who says that <i>rest</i> is Shilo, <i>because up to now you did not come to the rest</i><sup class="footnote-marker">592</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 12:9" href="/Deuteronomy.12.9"><i>Deut</i>. 12:9</a>.</i>. <i>Inheritance</i> is Jerusalem, <i>My inheritance is a vulture, a hyena for Me</i><sup class="footnote-marker">593</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jeremiah 12:9" href="/Jeremiah.12.9"><i>Jer</i>. 12:9</a>.</i>. He who said, <i>inheritance</i> is Shilo, <i>My inheritance like a lion in the forest</i><sup class="footnote-marker">594</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jeremiah 12:8" href="/Jeremiah.12.8"><i>Jer</i>. 12:8</a>.</i><i>; rest</i> is Jerusalem, <i>this is My rest forever</i><sup class="footnote-marker">595</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Psalms 132:14" href="/Psalms.132.14"><i>Ps</i>. 132:14</a>.</i>, etc.; like one who made a bundle and rested with it<sup class="footnote-marker">596</sup><i class="footnote">Even if he has to work on the bundle, once he has assembled it he feels like being able to rest. So it is with Jerusalem, even though it was repeatedly destroyed, it is not to be replaced.</i>. At the Tent of Meeting private altars were forbidden. At Gilgal private altars were permitted. At Shilo private altars were forbidden. At Nob and Gibeon private altars were permitted. At Jerusalem private altars were forbidden. {A parable of} a king who said to his slave, drink wine neither from Tiberias, nor from Caesarea<sup class="footnote-marker">597</sup><i class="footnote">Caesarea Philippi, as always.</i>, nor from Sepphoris. Therefore from in-between is permitted. So at the Tent of Meeting they spent 39 years. At Gilgal they spent fourteen years, seven when they conquered and seven when they distributed<sup class="footnote-marker">598</sup><i class="footnote">As inferred from verses in <i>Seder Olam</i> 11 (in the author’s edition, pp. 116–119); <a class="refLink" data-ref="Zevachim 118b" href="/Zevachim.118b">Babli <i>Zevaḥim</i> 118b</a>.</i>. At Shilo they spent 369 years<sup class="footnote-marker">599</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Seder Olam</i> 11 (p. 117).</i>, at Nob and Gibeon they spent 57, thirteen at Nob<sup class="footnote-marker">600</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Seder Olam</i> 13 (p. 131.</i> and 44 at Gibeon<sup class="footnote-marker">601</sup><i class="footnote">Since Solomon started building the Temple in year 480 after the Exodus (<i>1K</i>. 6:1), the time at Gibeon was 480 - (40+14+369+13). This is not quite correct since the altar at Gibeon was closed only at the inauguration of the Temple, after a building period of 7 years.</i>. But in Jerusalem they spent 410 years at the first Temple and 420 at the last Temple<sup class="footnote-marker">602</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Yoma 9a" href="/Yoma.9a">Babli <i>Yoma</i> 9a</a>. The computation which gives too few years for the Second Temple is analyzed by the author in <i>Seder Olam</i>, pp. 248–250.</i>, to confirm what has been said, <i>the glory of this last Temple will be greater than that of the first</i><sup class="footnote-marker">603</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Haggai 2:9" href="/Haggai.2.9"><i>Hag.</i>2:9</a>.</i>.

Chapter 2



Halakhah 1

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> He who reads the Scroll out of order<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">Not in the order the verses are written in the text.</i> did not fulfill his obligation. If he read it by heart, read it in Aramaic or any language<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">Except Greek as stated in the Mishnah.</i>, he did not fulfill his obligation except that one may read to Greek speakers in Greek. The Greek speaker who heard it from Hebrew square script did fulfill his obligation.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “He who reads the Scroll out of order did not fulfill his obligation.” For it is written, <i>as in their writing</i><sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Esth</i>, 9:27.</i>. If he read it by heart he did not fulfill his obligation for it is written, <i>as in their writing</i>. If he read it in Aramaic he did not fulfill his obligation for it is written, <i>as in their writing</i>. In any language he did not fulfill his obligation for it is written, <i>in their writing and in their language</i><sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Esth</i>, 8:9. Different in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Megillah 17a" href="/Megillah.17a">Babli 17a</a>,<a class="refLink" data-ref="Megillah 18a" href="/Megillah.18a">18a</a>.</i>.
<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">This text is a slightly enlarged copy of a text in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Berakhot 2:4:8" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Berakhot.2.4.8"><i>Berakhot</i> 2:4 (Notes 183–185</a>) referring to the Mishnah which states that reading the <i>Shema</i> not in the sequence of its verses is not fulfilling one’s obligation.</i> Rebbi Jonah said that Naḥman bar Ada stated; Rebbi Yose said that Naḥman the Old stated: <i>They shall be</i><sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 6:6" href="/Deuteronomy.6.6"><i>Deut</i>. 6:6</a>.</i>, they shall be unaltered<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">Understanding the verb “to be” as “to stay, to exist”.</i>. We have stated: “The same holds for Hallel and reading of <i>Shema</i><sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">Word to be deleted in this context; it belongs to the text of <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Megillah (Lieberman) 2:1" href="/Tosefta_Megillah_(Lieberman).2.1">Tosephta 2:1</a>.</i> the Scroll.” One understands this for the Scroll since it is written, <i>as in their writing</i>. But for Hallel, since it is written, <i>from sunrise to sunset may the Name of the Eternal be praised</i><sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Psalms 113:1" href="/Psalms.113.1"><i>Ps</i>. 113:1</a>.</i>? The Name of the Eternal is praised from sunrise to sunset. What can you understand from that<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">If there is no difference between East and West, earlier and later, as declared at the start of <i>Hallel</i>, why should it be important that the Psalms be recited in order? Differently in the <a class="refLink" data-ref="Megillah 17a" href="/Megillah.17a">Babli 17a</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Pesachim 118a" href="/Pesachim.118a"><i>Pesaḥim</i> 118a</a>.</i>? Rebbi Abun said, still it is said in order. <i>When Israel left Egypt</i><sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Psalms 114:1" href="/Psalms.114.1"><i>Ps</i>. 114:1</a>.</i>, in the past, <i>not for us, Eternal, not for us</i><sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Psalms 115:1" href="/Psalms.115.1"><i>Ps</i>. 115:1</a>.</i>, in current generations, <i>I loved, truly the Eternal listened to my voice</i><sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Psalms 116:1" href="/Psalms.116.1"><i>Ps</i>. 116:1</a>.</i> in the days of the Messiah, <i>bind the holiday sacrifice with ropes</i><sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Psalms 118:27" href="/Psalms.118.27"><i>Ps</i>. 118:27</a>.</i> in the days of Gog and Magog, <i>You are my God and I shall thank You</i><sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Psalms 118:28" href="/Psalms.118.28"><i>Ps</i>. 118:28</a>.</i>, in the future world.
Rebbi Yose in the name of Rebbi Aḥa, Rebbi Ze`ira in the name of Rebbi Eleazar: Only if it was written in Greek<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">This refers to the statement in the Mishnah that Greek speakers satisfy their obligation by hearing the Megillah read in Greek translation. This implies that it must be read from a Greek text, not being freely translated from a Hebrew text; but writing a Greek text is not permitted as explained in this paragraph. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Shabbat 115b" href="/Shabbat.115b">Babli <i>Šabbat</i> 115b</a>.</i>. Where do we hold? If it was written in square script and he translated it into Greek, that is what we have stated “in any language.” If it was written in Greek and he translated it into {the language of} square script, that is what was stated, which is the difference between Torah scrolls and the Esther scroll? Only that Torah scrolls may be written in any language but the Esther scroll may only be written in square script. Rebbi Samuel bar Sosartai said, explain it if it was written <i>gignton</i><sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">No reasonable explanation of this word is known. As far as the meaning is concerned, the most acceptable conjecture is that of Pene Moshe, that the scroll was written in two columns, one in Hebrew square script and the other in Greek; but the word in the text cannot be read δίγλωττον (Brüll). The suggestion by S. Fränkel, to read ’εγένετο, the start of the LXX translation of the masoretic text of Esther, to the exclusion of the apocryphal additions, does not fit the context.</i>. Samuel stated: If in error he omitted one verse but the translator translated it, he fulfilled his obligation. We are saying “in any language,” and you are saying so<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">If it is a translation on the spot, Greek is forbidden like any other translation.</i>? Samuel follows his opinion [since] Samuel said, if it was written according to its rules the Greek speaker fulfills his obligation in Greek. Rebbi Abbahu in the name of Rebbi Eleazar: If he knows the language of square script and he knows Greek, he fulfills his obligation [in Greek or]<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote">Corrector’s addition which totally misunderstands the text; to be deleted. A person understanding Hebrew must hear the text in Hebrew. As noted in the text, if he speaks both Hebrew and Greek, he must hear the text in Hebrew and cannot read the scroll for Greek speakers ignorant of Hebrew. Only a person ignorant of Hebrew may in public read the Greek text from a bilingual scroll.</i> in the language of square script. Greek, he fulfills his obligation in Greek. If he knows the language of square script and he knows Greek, may he absolve the public from their obligation in Greek? This shall come following: anybody who is not obligated in a matter cannot not absolve the public of their obligation<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Rosh Hashanah 3:10:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Rosh_Hashanah.3.10.1">Mishnah <i>Roš Haššanah</i> 3:9</a>.</i>.

Halakhah 2

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If he read it piecewise while dozing<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Rosh Hashanah 3:10:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Rosh_Hashanah.3.10.1">Mishnah <i>Roš Haššanah</i> 3:9</a>.</i> he fulfilled his obligation. If he wrote it, studied it, corrected it, if he had the intention<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote">He intended to read the entire scroll while reading, or studying, or correcting a correctly written scroll.</i> he fulfilled his obligation. If it was written with a chemical, or vermillion, or gum, or vitriol<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote">Greek χάλκανθος.</i>, on papyrus, or on vellum<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote">Greek διφθέρα.</i>, he did not fulfill his obligation unless it is written in square letters on a scroll in ink.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> It was stated: Piecewise, he fulfilled his obligation. Transposed<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote">The order of verses switched.</i>, he did not fulfill his obligation. Piecewise means in pieces. Transposed, one minus one<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote">Greek παρά, cf. E. and H. Guggenheimer, Lešonenu 39 (1975) pp. 60–61.</i>.
<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote">A very similar paragraph is in <i>Ševi`it</i> 9:1 (Notes 11–12).</i> Rebbi Ḥaggai said, the colleagues did know neither what חֲלוֹגְלוּגוֹת ,סֵירוּגִין, means nor “who is superior, in wisdom or in years?<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Serugin</i> is from the Mishnah here, <i>Ḥalaglugot</i> from Mishnah <i>Ševi`it</i>9:1, and the problem whether wisdom or age gives preference arises in connection with <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Sanhedrin 4:2:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Sanhedrin.4.2.1">Mishnah <i>Sanhedrin</i> 4:2</a> that in criminal cases the senior judge has to give his opinion last.</i>” They said, let us go and ask in the house of Rebbi. They went to ask; a slave girl from the house of Rebbi came out and told them, enter in pairs. Each one said, that one should enter before me. They started to enter one by one. She said to them, why do you enter in סֵירוּגִין? One rabbi was carrying purslain; it fell from him. She said to him, Rabbi, your חֲלוֹגְלוּגוֹת are dispersed. She said to her<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote">This sentence is missing in <i>Ševi`it</i> 9:1. It seems that the pure Hebrew speaking head-slave ordered one of her underlings to bring a broom.</i>, bring a broom<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote">The problem is the <i>hapax</i> in <i>Is</i>. 12:23.</i>; she brought a willow-broom<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote">The Aramaic equivalent of the Hebrew expression for “broom”. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Megillah 18a" href="/Megillah.18a">Babli 18a</a>.</i>.
<sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote">This paragraph is copied from <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Berakhot 2:1:9-12" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Berakhot.2.1.9-12"><i>Berakhot</i> 2:1, Notes 24–39</a> (<b>ב</b>).</i> Rebbi Mana said in the name of Rebbi Jehudah who said in the name of Rebbi Yose the Galilean: If one interrupted so that he would have been able to read all of it, he did not fulfill his duty<sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote">This does not refer to reading the scroll, which is mentioned later, but to reading the <i>Shema`</i> as part of the morning and evening services, the topic of <i>Berakhot</i> 1–3. For the Megillah, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Megillah 18b" href="/Megillah.18b">Babli 18b</a>.</i>. Rebbi Abba, Rav Jeremiah in the name of Rav: The practice follows Rebbi Mana who said in the name of Rebbi Jehudah who said in the name of Rebbi Yose the Galilean, “if one interrupted.”. Rebbi Joḥanan in the name of Rebbi Simeon ben Josadaq: The same applies to Hallel and the Esther Scroll. Abba bar Rav Huna and Rav Ḥisda sat together and said, the same rule applies to blowing the Shofar<sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote">When one is required to hear 9 sounds in prescribed order.</i>. They went up to (Rebbi’s) &lt;rav’s&gt;<sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Berakhot</i> &lt;text&gt; is the correct one.</i> Yeshivah and heard Rav Ḥuna in the name of Rav: Even if one heard it in up to nine hours he fulfilled his obligation. Rebbi Ze`ira said: When I was there, this was a problem for me. When I came here, I heard Rebbi Yasa in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: Even if one heard it during an entire day, one has fulfilled one’s obligation. But only if one heard it in the correct order<sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote">While there are no time limits, the prescribed order cannot be changed.</i>. Rebbi Yose investigated: If one needed to hear the initial simple tone and another one the final simple tone, one blow of the Shofar serves the requirements of both of them<sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote">The obligation is to hear, not to blow the <i>shofar</i>. While the person who blows must have intent to let the listeners fulfill their religious duty, the listener must have intent to listen to the prescribed sound, which may vary from person to person.</i>. Rebbi Abun bar Ḥiyya investigated: The recitation of Shema` with its benedictions, the recitation of Shema` without its benedictions, its benedictions without it<sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote">This again refers to reading the <i>Shema`</i> which is preceded and followed by prescribed benedictions. In computing the maximal time limit allowed for interruption, are these three different occasions or only one? No answer is given.</i>? If he interrupted after a third, interrupted after a second third<sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote">This also applies to reading the <i>Megillah</i>. If there are several interruptions, does the time limit apply to each one separately or to the total time of interruptions? No answer is given.</i>? Does one estimate by the reader’s speed or by the average speed of all men? Rebbi Mattaniah said, is it not reasonable by the reader’s?
Menasheh was sitting before Rebbi Ze`ira and was dozing. He said to him, repeat because I did not concentrate<sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote">While the Mishnah permits the reader to be dozing occasionally because when he is reading he cannot do this without intent, the listener cannot be dozing and fulfill the obligation to hear the Scroll from beginning to end.</i>.
“If he wrote it,” this implies that work is permitted. Should we say on the Fourteenth, in walled cities<sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote">While the Mishnah permits the reader to be dozing occasionally because when he is reading he cannot do this without intent, the listener cannot be dozing and fulfill the obligation to hear the Scroll from beginning to end.</i>? “Studied it,” only he should not let his mind wander to other topics. “He corrected it.” It was stated: one is not explicit about its mistakes. Rebbi Isaac bar Abba from Mehasia and Rebbi Ḥananel were sitting before Rav. One said, <i>Yehudim</i>. And the other one said, <i>Yehudiim</i>, and neither of them changed his opinion. Rebbi Joḥanan was reading always “Yehudim<sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote">Irrespective of how it was written.</i>”. Rebbi Ḥelbo, Rav Jeremiah in the name of Rav: One verse says “book<sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Esther 9:32" href="/Esther.9.32"><i>Esth</i>. 9:32</a>.</i>”, another verse says “letter.<sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Esth</i>. 96:26.</i>” How is this? They made it light for sewing. They were lenient regarding its sewing, that if he stitched two or three stitches it is qualified<sup class="footnote-marker">43</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Megillah 19a" href="/Megillah.19a">Babli 19a</a>.</i>.

Halakhah 3

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> A villager who went to a walled city, or a dweller in a walled city who went to a village, if he intends to return to his place<sup class="footnote-marker">44</sup><i class="footnote">In time for the reading of the Scroll.</i> he reads as at his place; otherwise he reads with them.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> One understands the dweller in a walled city whose time is later. Is the time of a village dweller who went to a walled city not earlier? Rebbi Yudan said, there is here no village dweller who went to a walled city<sup class="footnote-marker">47</sup><i class="footnote">If the Mishnah refers only to the situation on the day of Purim, then for the villager it should make no difference whether he wants to return to his place or not since he will be unable to read at his place.</i>. And it was stated in the House of Rebbi so: the dweller in a walled city who went to a village<sup class="footnote-marker">48</sup><i class="footnote">There are versions of the Mishnah which do not include the statement objected to by R. Yudan.</i>. Rebbi Yose said, also here if he will stay with them<sup class="footnote-marker">49</sup><i class="footnote">The Mishnah in its entire formulation can be retained if one adds people intending to move from one place to the other, even if for the purpose of moving they have to return to their earlier place of residence.</i>. Rebbi Yose said, only if he left before dawn, but if he left after dawn he already is not liable<sup class="footnote-marker">50</sup><i class="footnote">If the dweller in a walled town left the village during the night, the rules for villages have no relevance for him (the Mishnah does not mention reading the Scroll in the night, only that the entire day is qualified for the reading, Mishnah 5.) But immediately at dawn he becomes obligated.</i>. Rav Naḥman bar Jacob asked, then a proselyte who circumcised after dawn already is not liable<sup class="footnote-marker">51</sup><i class="footnote">If the proselyte was not obligated at dawn because he was not yet Jewish, he cannot become obligated in the middle of the day.</i>? Rebbi Abun bar Ḥiyya asked, the village dweller who changed his abode in the night of the Fifteenth [is obligated here; and a dweller in a walled city who changed his abode in the night of the Fourteenth] is not liable at either place<sup class="footnote-marker">52</sup><i class="footnote">The text as it stands makes no sense. The corrector’s addition has to be deleted; both R. Isaya II of Trani and Rashba quote the text without his addition. The text is quoted by Rashba, Responsa 3:414, as: “the village dweller who changed his abode in the night of the Fourteenth is not liable at either place” if he arrived at the walled city after dawn of the 15th since then on Purim he is neither a village dweller nor a city dweller. This text is confirmed by Meïri (ed. M. Herschler, Jerusalem 1962, p. 55), who questions Rashba’s interpretation.</i>?
May a village dweller free a city dweller of his obligation? Should it follow “anybody not obligated for something may not free the public from their obligation”<sup class="footnote-marker">53</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Rosh Hashanah 3:9:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Rosh_Hashanah.3.9.1">Mishnah <i>Roš Haššanah</i> 3:8</a>. By this argument, neither the village dweller on the 15<sup class="footnote-marker">th</sup> nor the city dweller on the 14<sup class="footnote-marker">th</sup> may read for another person’s benefit.</i>? May a city dweller free a village dweller of his obligation? Should it follow “anybody not obligated for something may not free the public from their obligation”? Or should it follow what “Rebbi Ḥelbo said, Rebbi Ḥuna in the name of the Great Rebbi Ḥiyya: Everybody may fulfill his obligation on the Fourteenth, which is the time of its reading<sup class="footnote-marker">54</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Megillah 1:5:9" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Megillah.1.5.9">Chapter 1, Note 67</a>. By this argument, everybody may read for other persons on the 14<sup class="footnote-marker">th</sup>. The question is not resolved (but <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosafot on Yevamot 14a:6:1" href="/Tosafot_on_Yevamot.14a.6.1">Tosaphot <i>Yebamot</i> 14a, <i>s.v</i>. כי</a> read the text as following the first opinion.)</i>”? Rebbi Yudan asked. [A village dweller who] decided to move in the night of the Fifteenth? Is that not the Mishnah, “a villager who went to a walled city”? The Mishnah if he was in the walled city. What is problematic for him, if he was in a village. But those on an ocean trip or passing through deserts read normally on the Fourteenth. Rebbi Mana said, if he intended to return to his place. Rebbi Phineas said, <i>therefore the dispersed Jews</i><sup class="footnote-marker">55</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Esther 9:19" href="/Esther.9.19"><i>Esth</i>. 9:19</a>.</i>, in that hour he was dispersed<sup class="footnote-marker">56</sup><i class="footnote">Since he could not possibly reach his home on Purim. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Megillah 19a" href="/Megillah.19a">Babli 19a</a>.</i>.

Halakhah 4

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> From where does a person start to read and fulfills his duty? Rebbi Meïr says, all of it. Rebbi Jehudah says, from <i>a Jewish man</i><sup class="footnote-marker">45</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Esther 2:5" href="/Esther.2.5"><i>Esth</i> 2:5</a>.</i>. Rebbi Yose says, from <i>after these matters</i><sup class="footnote-marker">46</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Esther 3:1" href="/Esther.3.1"><i>Esth</i> 3:1</a>.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “From where does a person start to read and fulfills his duty? Rebbi Meïr says, all of it. Rebbi Jehudah says, from <i>a Jewish man</i><sup class="footnote-marker">45</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Esther 2:5" href="/Esther.2.5"><i>Esth</i>. 2:5</a>.</i>. Rebbi Yose says, from <i>after these matters</i><sup class="footnote-marker">46</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Esther 3:1" href="/Esther.3.1"><i>Esth</i>. 3:1</a>.</i>.” Rebbi Abbahu in the name of Rebbi Eleazar: He who says all of it, <i>about all words of this letter</i><sup class="footnote-marker">57</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Esther 9:26" href="/Esther.9.26"><i>Esth</i>. 9:26</a>.</i>. He who says from <i>a Jewish man, what they understood about it</i><sup class="footnote-marker">57</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Esther 9:26" href="/Esther.9.26"><i>Esth</i>. 9:26</a>.</i>. He who says from <i>after these matters, and what happened to them</i><sup class="footnote-marker">57</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Esther 9:26" href="/Esther.9.26"><i>Esth</i>. 9:26</a>.</i>. Rebbi Simon in the name of Rebbi Joshua ben Levi. He who says all of it, <i>all the facts of his strength and mightiness</i><sup class="footnote-marker">58</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Esther 10:2" href="/Esther.10.2"><i>Esth</i>. 10:2</a>.</i>. He who says from <i>a Jewish man, the Chapter of the greatness of Mordocai</i><sup class="footnote-marker">58</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Esther 10:2" href="/Esther.10.2"><i>Esth</i>. 10:2</a>.</i>. He who says from <i>after these matters, how the King aggrandized him</i><sup class="footnote-marker">58</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Esther 10:2" href="/Esther.10.2"><i>Esth</i>. 10:2</a>.</i>. Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Abun in the name of Rebbi Joshua ben Levi: <i>strength, all strength, with all strength</i><sup class="footnote-marker">59</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Esther 9:29" href="/Esther.9.29"><i>Esth</i>. 9:29</a>.</i>. <i>Strength</i>, is Haman’s strength. <i>All strength</i>, is Mordocai’s strength. <i>With all strength</i>, is Ahasuerus’s strength. It was stated<sup class="footnote-marker">60</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Megillah 19a" href="/Megillah.19a">Babli 19a</a>. In <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Megillah (Lieberman) 2:9" href="/Tosefta_Megillah_(Lieberman).2.9">Tosephta 2:9</a> in the name of R. Simeon ben Eleazar.</i>: Rebbi Simeon ben Yoḥay says, from <i>in that night</i><sup class="footnote-marker">61</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Esther 6:1" href="/Esther.6.1"><i>Esth</i>. 6:1</a>.</i>. From the place where there was Haman’s downfall came Mordocai’s rise.
Rebbi Abba, Rav Jeremiah in the name of Rav: practice follows Rebbi Meïr who says, all of it<sup class="footnote-marker">62</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Megillah 18b" href="/Megillah.18b">Babli 18b</a>,<a class="refLink" data-ref="Megillah 19a" href="/Megillah.19a">19a</a>.</i>. Rebbi Ze`ira in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: In matters of <i>eruvin</i> and public fast-days everybody follows Rebbi Meïr. Rebbi Jacob bar Aḥa in the name Rebbi Joḥanan: Even about the reading of the Esther scroll everybody follows Rebbi Meïr<sup class="footnote-marker">63</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Eruvin 6:8:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Eruvin.6.8.3"><i>Eruvin</i> Chapter 6, Note 102</a>. In <i>eruvin</i> one follows R. Meïr that <i>eruv</i> for courtyards and participation for alleys must be separate acts; in <i>Ta`aniot</i> that on a public fast day the priestly blessing is given three times. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Eruvin 72a" href="/Eruvin.72a">Babli <i>Eruvin</i> 72a</a>. These rules are popular usage, not rabbinic decrees.</i>.
Rebbi Ḥelbo, Rav Mattanah, Yose bar Menashia in the name of Rav: Following whom who says, all of it, or who says from <i>a Jewish man</i>, or whom who said from <i>after these matters</i>, but only from a complete scroll<sup class="footnote-marker">63</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Eruvin 6:8:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Eruvin.6.8.3"><i>Eruvin</i> Chapter 6, Note 102</a>. In <i>eruvin</i> one follows R. Meïr that <i>eruv</i> for courtyards and participation for alleys must be separate acts; in <i>Ta`aniot</i> that on a public fast day the priestly blessing is given three times. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Eruvin 72a" href="/Eruvin.72a">Babli <i>Eruvin</i> 72a</a>. These rules are popular usage, not rabbinic decrees.</i>. Rebbi Ḥelbo, Rav Mattanah, Yose bar Menashia in the name of Rav: If it was written like fifths<sup class="footnote-marker">64</sup><i class="footnote">A <i>Ḥumash</i> is a single book of the Pentateuch. One may not read from an Esther text written together with other Hagiographs since the Esther scroll is not holy and Hagiographs are no source of Halakhah.</i> one does not read from it in public. Rebbi Tanḥuma said, because of the uneducated. Ulla from Bireh, Rebbi Eleazar in the name of Rebbi Ḥanina: The learned fundraiser<sup class="footnote-marker">65</sup><i class="footnote">For the title “<i>ragil</i>” for the outstanding scholar who acts as fundraiser, cf. S. Liebermann <i>Tarbiz</i>4 (1933) pp. 378–9; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Eruvin 3:6:2" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Eruvin.3.6.2"><i>Eruvin</i> Chapter 3, Note 150</a>. The <a class="refLink" data-ref="Megillah 4a" href="/Megillah.4a">Babli (4a</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Megillah 20a" href="/Megillah.20a">20a</a>) has the same statement formulated as obligation for everybody.</i> has to read it in the night and to repeat during the day. We were of the opinion to say, to teach its Mishnah<sup class="footnote-marker">66</sup><i class="footnote">Since the technical term for teaching Mishnah is “to repeat”.</i>. Rebbi Abba Mari the Babylonian said, to repeat its reading<sup class="footnote-marker">67</sup><i class="footnote">Even the fundraiser who wants to visit as many places as possible has to read the Scroll during the day; his reading in the night following Babylonian practice does not excuse him from the obligation to read it during daytime.</i>.
Rebbi Berekhia, Rebbi Ḥelbo, Ulla from Bireh, Rebbi Eleazar in the name of Rebbi Ḥanina<sup class="footnote-marker">68</sup><i class="footnote">This has no material connection with the preceding text; it is added because it is a tradition of Ulla from Bireh as is the preceding one. A parallel text is in <i>Moed qatan</i> 3:7 (in two texts, <b>קר</b>), shortened in the Babli, <i>Ta`anit</i> 31a, Midrash <i>Ps.</i>, <i>Ps</i>. 48[5].</i>: In the future the Holy One, praise to Him, will lead a circular dance for the Just ones. What is the reason? <i>Put your hearts to its walls</i><sup class="footnote-marker">69</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Psalms 48:14" href="/Psalms.48.14"><i>Ps</i>. 48:14</a>.</i>, it is written “to its circular dance.<sup class="footnote-marker">70</sup><i class="footnote">This reading is not found in masoretic texts.</i>” And the Just one will point with their finger and say, <i>Truly, He is GOD, our Power, Forever; He will lead us beyond death</i><sup class="footnote-marker">71</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Psalms 48:15" href="/Psalms.48.15"><i>Ps</i>. 48:15</a>.</i><i>! Almut</i>, in strength, [in agility.]<sup class="footnote-marker">72</sup><i class="footnote">Corrector’s addition from <i>Megillah</i>.</i> <i>Almut</i>, like these girls. Akilas translated ’αθανασία, a world without death. And the Just one will point with their finger and say, <i>Truly, He is GOD, our Power, Forever; He will lead us beyond death!</i> He will lead us in this world, He well lead us in the future<sup class="footnote-marker">73</sup><i class="footnote">“Lead us in worlds” is the translation of LXX.</i>.
Rebbi Ḥelbo, Rav Ḥama bar Guria in the name of Rav Ḥama bar Uqba in the name of Rebbi Yose ben Ḥanina: <i>Family and family</i><sup class="footnote-marker">74</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Esther 9:28" href="/Esther.9.28"><i>Esth</i>. 9:28</a>.</i>, from here that the watches of priests and Levites will be unoccupied<sup class="footnote-marker">75</sup><i class="footnote">Since sexual activity pollutes and purity is regained only at sundown following immersion in water, priests and Levites officiating in the Temple have to serve far from their families. If they have to join their families for the celebration of Purim, the Temple service has to stop. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Megillah 3a" href="/Megillah.3a">Babli 3a</a>.</i>; Rebbi Ḥelbo said, from here that the house of assembly will be empty<sup class="footnote-marker">76</sup><i class="footnote">While the previous remark was theoretical, this one is practical and forbids sessions of the Academy in the afternoon of Adar 14.</i>. Simeon bar Abba in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan, <i>and their remembrance will not disappear from their descendants</i><sup class="footnote-marker">74</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Esther 9:28" href="/Esther.9.28"><i>Esth</i>. 9:28</a>.</i>, from here that the Sages dedicated a Tractate to it.

Halakhah 5

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> Everybody is qualified to read the Scroll<sup class="footnote-marker">77</sup><i class="footnote">In order to absolve others of their obligation.</i> except the deaf-and-dumb<sup class="footnote-marker">78</sup><i class="footnote">He would have to use sign language. But the deaf-and-dumb is not obligated for any religious observance . He does not have to read and therefore cannot do it for others (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Megillah 2:2:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Megillah.2.2.1">Note 20</a>).</i>, the insane, and the underage. Rebbi Jehudah qualifies the underage<sup class="footnote-marker">79</sup><i class="footnote">The other Sages hold that the parent of a minor has the obligation to teach his son to read the scroll and to make him read it. This is not a personal obligation of the minor and therefore he is unable to absolve the public of their obligation.</i>. One does nor read the scroll, nor circumcises, nor immerses<sup class="footnote-marker">80</sup><i class="footnote">In a <i>miqweh</i>.</i>, nor sprinkles<sup class="footnote-marker">81</sup><i class="footnote">With ashes of the Red Cow.</i>, nor may the woman observing a day for a day immerse herself<sup class="footnote-marker">82</sup><i class="footnote">A woman has a discharge during the 11 days following the week of her menstruation. The discharge cannot be menstrual; therefore she has to observe one day without discharge, immerse herself in a <i>miqweh</i> in order to be pure at sundown.</i>, before sunrise. But all who did it after dawn are qualified.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “Everybody is qualified to read the Scroll except the deaf-and-dumb, the insane, and the underage.” <sup class="footnote-marker">83</sup><i class="footnote">This paragraph appears also in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Berakhot 2:4:3-4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Berakhot.2.4.3-4"><i>Berakhot</i> 2:4 (Notes 160–166</a>,<b>ב</b>) and <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Terumot 1:1:18-20" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Terumot.1.1.18-20"><i>Terumot</i> 1:2 (Notes 90–93</a>,ת). The Mishnah in <i>Berakhot</i> states that in reading the <i>Shema`</i> one must hear what one says. The majority holds that this is a desideratum but not an absolute necessity. R. Yose the Tanna disagrees and therefore a person who is deaf but able to speak still cannot read the <i>Shema`</i>. Heave is the part of the harvest which is given to the Cohen as sanctum to be eaten in purity. The dedication must be a conscious act; therefore in Mishnah <i>Terumot</i> 1:1 the deaf-and-dumb person is excluded from those who may dedicate heave.</i> Rav Mattanah said: That formulation is Rebbi Yose’s. Rebbi Yose said: We could be of the opinion that the rabbis and Rebbi Yose disagree only about <i>Shema`</i> since there it is written: <i>hear</i>!, but not about any other obligations. Since Rav Mattanah said “that formulation is Rebbi Yose’s”, it is the same for all obligations. What is the reason of Rebbi Yose? <i>Bend your ear to His commandments</i><sup class="footnote-marker">84</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Exodus 15:26" href="/Exodus.15.26"><i>Ex</i>. 15:26</a>.</i>, your ears should hear what your mouth says. Rav Ḥisda said: The deaf and dumb person is not mentioned, it is a formula<sup class="footnote-marker">85</sup><i class="footnote">In all legal matters depending on a conscious act, “insane, deaf-and-dumb, and underage” are excluded. The formula should have been modified here but, since the Mishnah was transmitted orally and could not be written down, was not modified, to ease memorization.</i>. Rebbi Yose said: It is reasonable to think that Rav Ḥisda concedes that the statement from <i>Terumot</i> is from Rebbi Yose. Rabbi Ḥanania came in the name of Rav Ḥisda, this one is by Rebbi Yose. Rebbi Yose bar Rebbi Abun said: It must be said that that one is Rebbi Yose’s since we have stated the first five cases<sup class="footnote-marker">86</sup><i class="footnote">Mishnah <i>Terumot</i> 1:1: Five categories of people cannot consecrate heave and if they did it anyhow, their actions are null and void: the deaf-and-dumb, the insane, minors, one who is not the owner, and a non-Jew.” The sixth Mishnah then reads: Five categories of people should not consecrate heave (since a priori it requires the recitation of a benediction) but if they did it anyhow their actions are valid: the speechless, the drunk, the naked, the blind, those impure by emission of semen.” In between it is explained that חֵרֵשׁ means a deaf-and-dumb person who is not legally capable of acting but that the deaf who can talk belongs to the same category as the second group of five.</i> and did not include it, because their heave is no heave. Then we have stated the five later ones and did not include it. So in the end you must say, that one is by Rebbi Yose<sup class="footnote-marker">87</sup><i class="footnote">Therefore, Mishnah <i>Terumot</i> 1:2, explaining that the deaf person who is able to speak can give heave, has to be formulated separately and could not be included in <i>Terumot</i> 1:6 since the first and sixths Mishnayot are Rebbi Yose’s who will object to the inclusion of the deaf person in the second group but not to that of the speechless.</i>.
“Rebbi Jehudah qualifies the underage.” “Rebbi Jehudah said, I was a minor and read it in front of Rebbi Tarphon at Lydda. They said to him, you were a minor and there is no testimony from minors. Rebbi said, it happened and I read it in the presence of Rebbi Jehudah at Usha. They said to him, that is no proof since he is the one who permits it.” From then on the public used to read it in the synagogue<sup class="footnote-marker">88</sup><i class="footnote">If one reads הָרַבִּים then it means that one chooses to make a synagogal act out of reading the Esther scroll (replacing the earlier private ceremonies), since then the Scroll would be read by the communal חָזָן who certainly is an adult. But if one reads הַרֻבִּים then the translation is “from then on the youths would read it in the synagogue”, permitting minors to read in public, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Megillah (Lieberman) 2:8" href="/Tosefta_Megillah_(Lieberman).2.8">Tosephta 2:8</a>.</i>. Bar Qappara said, one has to read it in front of women and children because they also were in the doubt<sup class="footnote-marker">89</sup><i class="footnote">The doubt whether they all would be killed. The corrector replaced the Yerushalmi word, which he did not understand, by the equivalent Babli word.</i>. Rebbi Joshua ben Levi acted thus; he assembled his sons and the people of his house<sup class="footnote-marker">90</sup><i class="footnote">The women of his household. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Megillah 4a" href="/Megillah.4a">Babli 4a</a>.</i> and read it in front of them.
“One does nor read the scroll before sunrise.” For it is written<sup class="footnote-marker">91</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Esther 9:1" href="/Esther.9.1"><i>Esth.</i>9:1</a>.</i>, <i>on the day the enemies of the Jews thought they would rule over them</i>.
“Nor circumcises,” for it is written<sup class="footnote-marker">92</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 12:3" href="/Leviticus.12.3"><i>Lev</i>. 12:3</a>.</i>, <i>on the eighth day one circumcises the prepuce of his flesh</i>.
“Nor immerses,” for it is written, <i>he sprinkles, he immerses</i><sup class="footnote-marker">93</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 19:18" href="/Numbers.19.18"><i>Num</i>. 19:18</a>.</i>. Since sprinkling is during daytime, also immersion is during daytime. From where that sprinkling is during daytime? For it is written<sup class="footnote-marker">94</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 19:19" href="/Numbers.19.19"><i>Num</i>. 19:19</a>.</i>, <i>the pure one sprinkles on the impure one on the third day</i>. <sup class="footnote-marker">95</sup><i class="footnote">From here on this paragraph and the next are from <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Shabbat 2:1:13-15" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Shabbat.2.1.13-15"><i>Šabbat</i>2, Notes 63–71</a>. This paragraph is connected with the Mishnah here since it states only that immersion of males has to be during daytime. The next paragraph has no connection, it simply is copied from <i>Šabbat</i>.</i> All who require immersion immerse themselves normally during daytime except for the woman after her period and one who gave birth who only immerses herself during the night<sup class="footnote-marker">96</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Shabbat 121a" href="/Shabbat.121a">Babli <i>Šabbat</i>121a</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Pesachim 90b" href="/Pesachim.90b"><i>Pesaḥim</i>90b</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Yoma 6a" href="/Yoma.6a"><i>Yoma</i>6a</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Yoma 87a" href="/Yoma.87a">87a</a>.</i>. A woman after her period whose time has passed<sup class="footnote-marker">97</sup><i class="footnote">After the first evening she could have immersed herself after her period; cf. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Niddah 4:1:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Niddah.4.1.1"><i>Niddah</i>4:1 Note 3</a>. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Niddah 67b" href="/Niddah.67b">Babli <i>Niddah</i>67b</a>.</i> immerses herself either during the day or during the night. Rebbi Ḥiyya bar Abba preached this to the people of Tyre<sup class="footnote-marker">98</sup><i class="footnote">As practice to be followed.</i>, a woman after her period whose time has passed immerses herself either during the day or during the night. There<sup class="footnote-marker">99</sup><i class="footnote">In Babylonia, where the statement was attributed to R. Joḥanan; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Niddah 67b" href="/Niddah.67b"><i>Niddah</i>67b</a>. There, the reason is given “because of the discipline of her daughter”, that she should learn the rules clearly.</i> they say, even one whose time has passed not, because of her mother-in-law and her daughter-in-law. They saw a woman of the family of our rabbis<sup class="footnote-marker">100</sup><i class="footnote">The household of R. Jehudah Nesia. The deviation of Galilean from Babylonian practice is confirmed.</i> immersing herself normally during daytime. We shall say that her time had passed.
A woman after her period who was immersed by accident, Rebbi Shammai in the name of Rav: she is pure for her house but impure for purities<sup class="footnote-marker">101</sup><i class="footnote">She unintentionally fell into water which qualifies as a <i>miqweh</i>. In this opinion, an intent to become pure by immersion is needed only to be able to prepare pure food (“purities”) whereas to be permitted to her husband (“pure for her house”) she only has to be immersed since in the relevant biblical paragraph (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 15:19-24" href="/Leviticus.15.19-24"><i>Lev</i>. 15:19–24</a>) immersion is explicitly required only of people coming in contact with movables on which she sat during her period of impurity. The opposing opinion requires intent for any purification. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Chullin 31a" href="/Chullin.31a">Babli <i>Ḥulin</i>31a</a>.</i>. Rebbi Eleazar in the name of Rebbi Ḥanina, she is impure both for her house and purities. What is the reason? <i>It should be washed a second time and will be pure</i><sup class="footnote-marker">102</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Lev</i>. 15:59, about impurity of textiles. The remark “a second time” is taken as indication that the second washing has to follow the rules of the first; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Chullin 31b" href="/Chullin.31b">Babli <i>Ḥulin</i>31b</a>. A different interpretation in <i>Sifra Tazria` Pereq</i> 16(11)</i>. Since the first time it was intentionally, also the second time it must be intentionally. And from where that the first time it was intentionally? <i>The Cohen has to order that they should wash</i><sup class="footnote-marker">103</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 13:54" href="/Leviticus.13.54"><i>Lev</i>. 13:54</a>.</i>. Intentionally. And we see that rabbis are early for intercalations. Rav Naḥman in the name of Rebbi Mana: It is an obligation to be early, to hasten in commandments<sup class="footnote-marker">104</sup><i class="footnote">This refers to something completely different in the rules of Sabbath observation. Rebbi Naḥman requires the husband to direct his household to light Sabbath lights, and therefore begin the observance of the Sabbath, somewhat before the time of sundown since in the fulfilling of divine commandments one has to show eagerness. As an example it is noted that the rabbis who form the court which decrees intercalations always arrive early for the deliberations. This is a commentary on the last statement of the last Mishnah in Chapter <i>Šabbat</i>2.</i>.

Halakhah 6

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> The entire day is qualified for reading the Scroll, and to read the <i>Hallel</i><sup class="footnote-marker">105</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Psalms 113-118" href="/Psalms.113-118"><i>Ps</i>. 113–118</a>, to be recited on holidays. The recitation of <i>Hallel</i> after morning prayers is customary, not obligatory.</i>, and to blow the <i>shofar</i><sup class="footnote-marker">106</sup><i class="footnote">On New Year’s Day. If a person is unable to go to the synagogue, he may hear the required sounds during the entire day.</i>, and to take the <i>lulav</i>, and for the <i>musaf</i> prayer<sup class="footnote-marker">107</sup><i class="footnote">The recitation of <i>musaf</i> after the Torah reading is customary, not obligatory.</i>, and for <i>musaf</i> sacrifices, for confessions with bulls<sup class="footnote-marker">108</sup><i class="footnote">The confessions of the anointed priest (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 4:3" href="/Leviticus.4.3"><i>Lev</i>. 4:3</a>) and the representatives of the people (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 4:14" href="/Leviticus.4.14"><i>Lev</i>. 4:14</a>), as well as the <i>musaf</i> sacrifices, may be performed at any time after the daily morning and before the evening sacrifices.</i>, and for the declaration of tithes<sup class="footnote-marker">110</sup><i class="footnote">The farmer’s declaration, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 26:13-15" href="/Deuteronomy.26.13-15"><i>Deut</i>. 26:13–15</a>, as well as the declaration on the occasion of presentation of first fruits, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 26:3-10" href="/Deuteronomy.26.3-10"><i>Deut</i>. 26:3–10</a>.</i> and for the confessions of the Day of Atonement<sup class="footnote-marker">111</sup><i class="footnote">The confession of the High Priest (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 16:21" href="/Leviticus.16.21"><i>Lev</i>. 16:21</a>).</i>.<br><sup class="footnote-marker">116</sup><i class="footnote">A continuation of the sentence starting with: “the entire day is qualified.”</i> For leaning on<sup class="footnote-marker">117</sup><i class="footnote">Confession of the votary when bringing his sacrifice, while leaning on the head of the sacrificial animal; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 1:4" href="/Leviticus.1.4"><i>Lev</i>. 1:4</a>, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 3:2" href="/Leviticus.3.2">3:2</a>,<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 1:8" href="/Leviticus.1.8">8</a>,<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 1:13" href="/Leviticus.1.13">13</a>,<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 4:24" href="/Leviticus.4.24">4:24</a>,29,33.</i>, slaughter, weaving<sup class="footnote-marker">118</sup><i class="footnote">An act required for certain sacrifices, mostly well-being sacrifices, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 7:30" href="/Leviticus.7.30"><i>Lev</i>. 7:30</a>.</i> and presenting<sup class="footnote-marker">119</sup><i class="footnote">Presenting flour sacrifices and first fruits to the altar.</i>, taking a handful<sup class="footnote-marker">120</sup><i class="footnote">Of the flour offering to be burned on the altar.</i>, burning on the altar, breaking the neck<sup class="footnote-marker">121</sup><i class="footnote">Of a bird offered as elevation or purification sacrifice (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 1:15" href="/Leviticus.1.15"><i>Lev</i>. 1:15</a>,<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 5:8" href="/Leviticus.5.8">5:8</a>).</i>, receiving<sup class="footnote-marker">122</sup><i class="footnote">The sacrificial blood to be brought to the altar.</i>, sprinkling<sup class="footnote-marker">123</sup><i class="footnote">The blood on the horns of the altar.</i>, and to let the suspected adulteress drink<sup class="footnote-marker">124</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 5:11-31" href="/Numbers.5.11-31"><i>Num</i>. 5:11–31</a>.</i>, and to break the neck of the calf<sup class="footnote-marker">125</sup><i class="footnote">In case of an unsolved murder, <a class="refLink" data-ref="Deuteronomy 21:1-9" href="/Deuteronomy.21.1-9"><i>Deut</i>. 21:1–9</a>.</i>, and to purify the sufferer from skin disease<sup class="footnote-marker">126</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 14:1-32" href="/Leviticus.14.1-32"><i>Lev</i>. 14:1–32</a>.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “The entire day is qualified to read the Scroll,” as it is written<sup class="footnote-marker">91</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Esther 9:1" href="/Esther.9.1"><i>Esth.</i>9:1</a>.</i>, <i>on the day the enemies of the Jews thought they would rule over them</i>. To read the <i>Hallel</i>, as it is written<sup class="footnote-marker">112</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Ps</i>, 118:24.</i>, <i>this is the day the Eternal made, let us enjoy and be happy on it</i>. To blow the <i>shofar</i>, as it is written<sup class="footnote-marker">113</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Numbers 29:1" href="/Numbers.29.1"><i>Num</i>. 29:1</a>.</i>, <i>a day to shofar blowing it shall be for you</i>. To take the <i>lulav</i>, as it is written<sup class="footnote-marker">114</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 23:40" href="/Leviticus.23.40"><i>Lev</i>. 23:40</a></i>, <i>you shall take for yourselves on the first day</i>. For the <i>musaf</i> prayer, and for <i>musaf</i> sacrifices, as it is written<sup class="footnote-marker">115</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 7:38" href="/Leviticus.7.38"><i>Lev</i>. 7:38</a>. The verse continues: <i>to bring their sacrifices to the Eternal</i>.</i>, <i>on the day on which He commanded the Children of Israel</i>, etc.
“For leaning on, slaughter, weaving, presenting, taking a handful, burning on the altar, breaking the neck, receiving, sprinkling.” As it is written<sup class="footnote-marker">115</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 7:38" href="/Leviticus.7.38"><i>Lev</i>. 7:38</a>. The verse continues: <i>to bring their sacrifices to the Eternal</i>.</i>, <i>on the day on which He commanded the Children of Israel</i>.

Halakhah 7

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> The entire night is qualified for the cutting of the <i>omer</i><sup class="footnote-marker">127</sup><i class="footnote">Burning on the altar of the leftovers of the sacrifices of the previous day; <a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 6:5" href="/Leviticus.6.5"><i>Lev</i>. 6:5</a>.</i>, and to burning fat and parts<sup class="footnote-marker">128</sup><i class="footnote">Counting the 50 days between the bringing of the <i>omer</i> and Pentecost (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 23:15-16" href="/Leviticus.23.15-16"><i>Lev</i>. 23:15–16</a>). The verse puts the start of the count at the bringing of the <i>omer</i> which must be during daytime, but rabbinically it is connected to the cutting of the <i>omer</i> in the night preceding to offering. Since the statement is given as a quote, one has to assume that a Yerushalmi Mishnah mentioned “counting”, a continuing obligation also without a Temple, instead of “cutting”, which became obsolete with the destruction of the Temple. Both “cutting” and “counting” are mentioned in the Mishnah in Alfasi, confirmed there by the commentary “Rashi”, cf. <i>Dikduke Soferim Megillah</i>56a Note (כ).</i>. This is the principle: Anything commanded to be done by day is qualified the entire day, anything commanded to be done by night is qualified the entire night.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “The entire night is qualified for the counting<sup class="footnote-marker">128</sup><i class="footnote">Counting the 50 days between the bringing of the <i>omer</i> and Pentecost (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 23:15-16" href="/Leviticus.23.15-16"><i>Lev</i>. 23:15–16</a>). The verse puts the start of the count at the bringing of the <i>omer</i> which must be during daytime, but rabbinically it is connected to the cutting of the <i>omer</i> in the night preceding to offering. Since the statement is given as a quote, one has to assume that a Yerushalmi Mishnah mentioned “counting”, a continuing obligation also without a Temple, instead of “cutting”, which became obsolete with the destruction of the Temple. Both “cutting” and “counting” are mentioned in the Mishnah in Alfasi, confirmed there by the commentary “Rashi”, cf. <i>Dikduke Soferim Megillah</i>56a Note (כ).</i> of the <i>omer</i>.” Rebbi Joḥanan said, this is Rebbi Eleazar ben Rebbi Simeon’s, since Rebbi Eleazar ben Rebbi Simeon said, from the start of cutting you shall start to count<sup class="footnote-marker">129</sup><i class="footnote">And since the verse requires the cutting at the end of the holiday, counting has to occur immediately after the end of the holiday. But then one should not have the entire night for both cutting and counting.</i>. Then he should leave the Sabbath domain at nightfall and start to cut. It is impossible to be exact<sup class="footnote-marker">130</sup><i class="footnote">Since “immediately after” is a vague notion, in particular involving a complicated matter, moving vessels and sickle to a field, etc., by necessity the entire night has to be qualified.</i>; therefore the entire night is qualified.
If he was sacrificing the flour offering of the <i>omer</i> and it became impure in his hand, he says it<sup class="footnote-marker">131</sup><i class="footnote">That the grain became impure and he needs new one.</i> and one brings him another one; otherwise<sup class="footnote-marker">132</sup><i class="footnote">If no grain is left from the cutting done in the preceding night.</i> one tells him, be intelligent and shut up, be intelligent and shut up, the words of Rebbi. Rebbi Eleazar ben Rebbi Simeon says, always one tells him, be intelligent and shut up, since no <i>omer</i> which was not cut for its purpose is qualified. Rebbi Eleazar ben Rebbi Simon is of the opinion that the <i>omer</i> cannot come from storage<sup class="footnote-marker">133</sup><i class="footnote">A minority opinion in both Talmudim, explicitly contradicted in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Tosefta Menachot 10:7" href="/Tosefta_Menachot.10.7">Tosephta <i>Menaḥot</i>10:33</a>. <a class="refLink" data-ref="Menachot 72a" href="/Menachot.72a">Babli <i>Menaḥot</i>72a</a>.</i>. Rebbi Joḥanan said, that of Rebbi Eleazar ben Rebbi Simon parallels the system of Rebbi Aqiba, his father’s teacher, as we have stated there<sup class="footnote-marker">134</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Shabbat 19:1:1" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Shabbat.19.1.1">Mishnah <i>Šabbat</i>19:1</a>, about preparations for a circumcision required to be performed on the Sabbath. R. Aqiba then must hold that if the 16th of Nisan is a Sabbath, the <i>omer</i> must be cut on the Sabbath.</i>: “Any work which is possible to be performed on Friday does not push the Sabbath away; but any which is impossible to be performed on Friday pushes the Sabbath away.” But did we not state<sup class="footnote-marker">135</sup><i class="footnote"><a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Rosh Hashanah 1:5:3" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Rosh_Hashanah.1.5.3"><i>Roš Haššanah</i>1:6, Note 278</a>.</i>, “It happened that more than 40 couples passed by and Rebbi Aqiba held them back in Lydda.” Because they are 40 couples. But if it had been a single couple he would not have held them back<sup class="footnote-marker">138</sup><i class="footnote">This paragraph is copied in <a class="refLink" data-ref="Jerusalem Talmud Rosh Hashanah 1:8:2-4" href="/Jerusalem_Talmud_Rosh_Hashanah.1.8.2-4"><i>Roš Haššanah</i>1:10</a> (<b>ה</b>).<br>Since the <i>`omer</i> must be brought at a fixed time, in pharisaic-rabbinic tradition on the 16th of Nisan (in the standard Sadducee tradition of the Book of Jubilees on Nisan 22, in Boethusian tradition on the Sunday of the Passover week), the fixed date overrules any Sabbath prohibition. Since the grain has to be parched (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 2:14" href="/Leviticus.2.14"><i>Lev</i>. 2:14</a>), the offering involves a Sabbath violation. It is prescribed that the grain be newly cut (<a class="refLink" data-ref="Leviticus 23:10" href="/Leviticus.23.10"><i>Lev</i>. 23:10</a>). By tradition this has to be done in the night of Nisan 16.
